Jeff Gritchen/MediaNews Group/Orange County Register via Getty Images\n\u201cI think we are going to see a lot more of this,\u201d she added. \u201cThere is a push now and we do see both unit and dollar growth of spicy and \u2018swicy\u2019 products.\u201d\nStarbucks is just one of many coming in hot with new menu items, introducing three new Spicy Lemonade Refreshers beverages in April \u2014 which the company said was \u201cinspired by the \u2018swicy\u2019 trend, which combines sweet and spicy flavors in all sorts of creative ways.\u201d\nThe coffee chain also spiced things up with wildflower honey-infused with premium chili peppers in their Starbucks Reserve Roasteries and select stores in Chicago, New York City and Seattle, adding the Hot Honey Affogato and Hot Honey Espresso Martini to the menu.\nIn February, Coca-Cola released a \u201cspiced\u201d variation of its original recipe \u2014 a result of growing interest in spice.\n\u201cIf you go to the aisles, you\u2019ll see the amount of spiciness has gone up because consumers\u2019 taste palates have evolved. We realized that could be an opportunity for us,\u201d Coca-Cola\u2019s North America marketing chief, Shakir Moin, told the Associated Press at the time.\n3 Starbucks added the Hot Honey Affogato and Hot Honey Espresso Martini to the menu. Starbucks\nLyons Wyatt said that younger millennials, Gen Zers and Gen Alpha are the cohorts seeking out spice, and Circana found that 11% of 25-to-34-year-olds enjoy bold flavors \u2014 up 7 percentage points from 2019.\nThis is partly due to growing diversity in America and a wider range of ingredients being introduced, Jennifer Creevy, director of food and drink at trend forecasting and analytics firm WGSN, told CNN.\n\u201cTake the rise of K-drama and K-pop, as an example. Fans of this entertainment want to consume the media but also want to taste the flavors of Korea, hence the rise of spicy Gochujang, which is now in everything from dishes to snacks, drinks and desserts,\u201d she said.\nIn addition, according to Varchasvi Singh, food service analyst with Mintel, the stereotype that American palates are \u201cbland\u201d is a misconception, and this trend proves it.\n\u201cThere certainly is an expanded appetite for spicy foods and flavors, one that is driven by wanting to explore unfamiliar ingredients and cuisines,\u201d Singh told CNN. \u201cThis enthusiasm isn\u2019t a fad, it is here to stay.\u201d\n3 Lyons Watt said that consumers can expect to see more spice in all aisles of the grocery store. On behalf of TOMBSTONE Pizza\nLyons Watt said that consumers can expect to see more spice in all aisles of the grocery store: frozen foods, drinks, ice cream, chips and so on.\n\u201cMy whole motto for a couple of years has been, \u2018The hotter the better,\u2019 because consumers gravitate to it. Traditionally it was younger consumers that were driving this, but now we\u2019ve seen bold flavors being embraced by most age groups, but the dominant are still younger consumers,\u201d she shared.",

            "title": "Would You Feed Your Cat a Diet of Bugs? Take a Survey for Science",
            "description": "Researchers at the University of California, Davis want to interview cat owners about the growing trend of insect-based pet food.",
            "content": "Scientists in California want to know if you would ever feed your cat a bowl of bug chow. They\u2019re looking to survey cat owners about their attitudes on the emerging trend of insect-based cat diets, which some researchers and advocates say could be more sustainable and possibly even healthier than traditional pet food.\nWhy Was Now the Right Time to Come Back and Do 28 Years Later? CC Share Subtitles Off\nEnglish view video Why Was Now the Right Time to Come Back and Do 28 Years?\nThe research is being conducted by scientists with the Animal Welfare Epidemiology Lab at the University of California, Davis. In the past, they\u2019ve asked pet owners about the health of their aging cats, whether they would be willing to take advantage of remote vet visits, and about how well their cats socialize with others. This latest project is being led by Emily Jang, a fourth-year undergraduate researcher and animal biology major.\nAdvertisement\n\u201cOur research study mainly aims to garner cat owners\u2019 opinions on insect-based cat diets in order to assess what makes them more or less willing to purchase and use such foods,\u201d Jang told Gizmodo in an email.\nAdvertisement\nJang notes that several studies have pointed to the potential benefits of an insect-based diet for cats (and dogs). Cats in the wild will naturally eat insects occasionally, and some edible insects are known to have a rich mix of nutrients. And since most dietary allergies in cats are tied to beef, dairy, or fish, it\u2019s possible insect-based diets would be less likely to cause allergic reactions. The techniques needed to mass-produce insects for food are also thought to be more environmentally sustainable than traditional sources of meat, such as cattle.\nAdvertisement\nThere is an ongoing debate over whether edible insects can become an important part of human diets (including if enough people will ever be able to stomach the notion of eating them), but the insect-based pet food industry seems to be growing steadily. There are now dozens of brands being sold worldwide. Many of these are primarily in Europe, but the industry has begun to emerge in the U.S. as well.\nAccording to Jang, however, there\u2019s been very little research looking at what American cat owners think about all this. And that\u2019s a knowledge gap that her team is hoping to remedy.\nAdvertisement\n\u201cInsect-based cat foods have great potential for success and mass popularity, but we need to collaborate with U.S. cat owners to collect their opinions on this rising trend,\u201d Jang said.\nThe team is hoping to collect responses from at least 400 people over the age of 18 who own at least one cat. They\u2019ll be looking to see if people\u2019s attitudes toward insect cat diets could be tied to other factors, such as their concerns about the environment or their cats\u2019 existing dietary habits. They\u2019d also like to know if there\u2019s any information in particular about these diets that could convince cat owners to take a chance on them. Should the project go as scheduled, the team plans to submit their findings to a peer-reviewed journal by this September.\nAdvertisement\n\u201cI hope readers will venture into this rising topic of cat foods with sincerity and consideration,\u201d Jang said. Any willing cat owners can take part in the 10-minute survey by clicking here.",

( Dash N' Dine)\nBROOKFIELD, CT \u2014 The owners of a newly opened business in Brookfield are hoping to cash in on the hottest trend in food service.\nTim Roche and his wife Danitza opened Dash N' Dine at 540 Federal Road in the Oak Meadows shopping plaza earlier this month. The new business is a combination of deli, bakery, and hot buffet offering meals by the pound. But don't worry about finding a place to sit. There are none. Dash N' Dine is take-out service only.\nCurrently, the market is open weekdays 6:00 a.m. to 3:30 p.m., but the couple plans on expanding hours to 7 p.m. soon. In fact, the whole idea for the business arose from the realization that dinnertime in suburbia had gotten way too out of hand. \"The kids are going to swim practice, and theater practice and dance practice. And I think everybody is in the same boat. Nobody wants to make dinner anymore,\" he said.\nThe \"grab-and-go\" food service category has been growing steadily since 2020, when the pandemic fueled consumer desire for grabbing a quick pre-made meal without sitting down in a restaurant, according to industry trade site Supermarket Perimeter. The movement is blowing up inside delicatessens as well, where the trend has been toward \"global flavors,\" according to Deli Business. Here, too, Dash N' Dine has positioned itself ahead of the curve.\n\"'Oh, there's going to be a little bitterness on this Italian bruschetta arugula fig sandwich, but it's going to be offset by the sweetness of the fig and the saltiness of the other, you know?'\" Roche said, mimicking his wife, Danitza, a graduate of the Escoffier International Culinary Institute and the star of the new eatery's show. She was the head chef for the company that makes the private label brands for Big Y and Whole Foods, Roche explained, and the trend there was clear. \"The number one thing in the food industry is grab and go,\" he said.\nDash N' Dine is not the Roches' first foray into food service. They launched a personal chef servicefor events called Mexico Gourmet a few years ago in Bethel. Tim said the process of creating a business, post-pandemic, has become much simpler.\nHe's not just talking about the legal red tape.\n\"We found this place (540 Federal Road), on Facebook marketplace, on a Saturday morning, I think he posted it,\" he said. \"And we were the only people who came in. We came in and we shook hands right there. 'We'll take it.' It was incredible.\"\nThe couple's courtship moved nearly as quickly. He met his Mexican-born wife for the first time in Germany at the Wacken Open Air Festival in 2008 (\"It's like a Woodstock for heavy metal\"), fell immediately in love, and since then, \"everything with her has been a dream come true.\" Dash N' Dine is open Monday through Friday, 6:00 a.m. to 3:30 p.m., 8:00 a.m. to 3 p.m. Saturdays, and closed Sundays.",

This article contains spoilers for Sh\u014dgun episode 5.\nSummary The treatment of the pheasant in Sh\u014dgun episode 5 continues to showcase cultural differences through food trends.\nThe pheasant and its removal from Blackthorne's home causes the death of Uejiro, the gardener, through a tragic misunderstanding.\nThe hanging of pheasants is a common practice with game such as birds and other hunted creatures to mature meat.\nSh\u014dgun episode 5 includes a story in which John Blackthorne hangs a pheasant to rot, continuing a food trend of the show and proving to be vital to his character. John Blackthorne, played by Cosmo Jarvis in Sh\u014dgun's cast of characters, begins the latest installment of the show by dealing with the aftermath of Nagakado's killing of Jozen. Thanks to Nagakado's actions, it seems as though war is brewing between Toranaga, his allies, and the forces of Ishido's council by the time of Sh\u014dgun episode 5's ending.\nConcerning where Blackthorne fits into things, his training of Toranaga's men in the tactics of naval warfare may give the latter an edge against Ishido's warriors. Rather than continuing his training of the Samurai, a subplot of Sh\u014dgun episode 5 focused intently on Blackthorne's home in Japan, his relationship with Mariko, and the returning Buntaro, who was thought dead after episode 3. From reveals about Lady Mariko's family in Sh\u014dgun to a tragedy surrounding Blackthorne's gardener, these story threads coalesced thanks to the strangest of plot devices: a rotting pheasant.\nRelated What Happened To John Blackthorne\u2019s Gardener In Shogun Episode 5 The unfortunate fate of John Blackthorne's gardener in Sh\u014dgun episode 5 demonstrates how much power the Englishman wields as Toranaga's Hatamoto.\nJohn Blackthorne's Decision To Let The Pheasant Rot Explained\nA pheasant became the unlikely catalyst of Sh\u014dgun episode 5's story.\nimage via Hulu\nAt the beginning of Sh\u014dgun episode 5's story, Toranaga presents Blackthorne with a pheasant as a gift for training his warriors in British naval warfare. Upon receiving the pheasant, Blackthorne confuses those in his home by hanging the pheasant outside his house. He tells those in his service to leave it hanging, even days later, as the bird begins to rot and decay. Despite the continued bad smell, drawing of flies, and overall rotting of the pheasant\u2019s carcass, John leaves it hanging for reasons that confuse both the Japanese inhabitants of his home and Sh\u014dgun\u2019s audience.\nInterestingly, the decision to let the pheasant hang for days is one versed in historical accuracy. In the 17th century, and even to this day in some cases, game such as pheasants and other birds were often hung after being obtained. The reason for this stemmed from personal taste, with the process of hanging creatures like pheasants making the meat more ripe, thus tasting better upon being cooked. Some even preferred to allow pheasants to rot, explaining why Blackthorne did the same in Sh\u014dgun episode 5.\nSh\u014dgun Season 1 Episodes Remaining Release Date \"Chapter Six: Ladies of the Willow World\" March 26, 2024 \"Chapter Seven: A Stick of Time\" April 2, 2024 \"Chapter Eight: The Abyss of Life\" April 9, 2024 \"Chapter Nine: Crimson Sky\" April 16, 2024 \"Chapter Ten: A Dream of a Dream\" April 23, 2024\nSh\u014dgun's Pheasant Ends Up Being More Important Than Food\nThe rotting pheasant becomes part of a greater plot point in Sh\u014dgun episode 5.\nClose\nWhen first hanging the pheasant, Blackthorne insists that no one should touch the bird who works within the confines of his house. He states that the bird is forbidden, and utters three words in broken Japanese to get his point across: \"If touched - die!\" In Blackthorne's mind, this is simply an exaggeration common in the English language, uttered so that the Japanese patrons of his home know not to touch the pheasant. Blackthorne only wishes for the bird to mature to his tastes, as he wishes to prepare it well given it was a gift from Lord Toranaga.\nWhile Blackthorne did not strictly mean that someone should die if they touch the pheasant, this is what eventually transpires in Sh\u014dgun episode 5's ending.\nHowever, a central theme of Sh\u014dgun episode 5 and the show as a whole revolves around Blackthorne underestimating the dedication to honor present in 17th-century Japan. While Blackthorne did not strictly mean that someone should die if they touch the pheasant, this is what eventually transpires in Sh\u014dgun episode 5's ending. Under the orders of Muraji, the village headman and Toranaga's spy, Uejiro removed Blackthorne's pheasant. Uejiro was Blackthorne's gardener, with Fuji - Blackthorne's consort - later enforcing the apparent orders of her master by sentencing Uejiro to death for touching the bird.\nThe fate of Uejiro, strongly implied to have been orchestrated by Muraji who later frames the late Uejiro as Toranaga's spy, horrifies Blackthorne. The Englishman underestimated how dedicated the Japanese are to order and honor, believing himself to have killed Uejiro indirectly by giving what Fuji mistook for an order of death should the pheasant be removed. While the pheasant and Blackthorne's treatment of it seemed strange and somewhat humorous at first, the fate of Uejiro soon turned the ordeal - like the pheasant itself - rotten and sour.\nUejiro's body was not shown in Sh\u014dgun episode 5, potentially meaning the gardener survived due to the nature of Muraji and Toranaga's plot involving spies.\nThe Pheasant Is Another Example Of Shogun Using Food To Show Cultural Differences\nBlackthorne's pheasant highlights the differences between England and Japan in the 17th century.\nCustom Image by Sam MacLennan\nThe final way in which Sh\u014dgun episode 5's pheasant is important is that it continues the show's trend of highlighting cultural differences through food. This was first showcased in Sh\u014dgun episode 4 through natt\u014d, the meal that Mariko and Fuji were shown to eat. When Blackthorne tried the natt\u014d, the two women thought it was funny, as an Englishman might not care for the somewhat acquired taste. This trend of using food to highlight cultural differences continued with the pheasant in Sh\u014dgun episode 5, as well as the rabbit stew that Mariko, Fuji, and Buntaro refused to eat.\nNew episodes of Sh\u014dgun release every Tuesday on FX and Hulu.",

            "content": "Australians are being warned against taking part in the viral trend of \u201cmushroom foraging\u201d after a food safety body deemed it potentially life-threatening.\nThe alert comes less than a year after three people died after eating a beef Wellington \u2013 allegedly containing death cap mushrooms \u2013 at a family lunch in Leongatha, Victoria in July 2023.\nFollowing the high-profile incident, Erin Patterson, 49, was charged with the murder of her estranged husband\u2019s parents Don and Gail Patterson, and aunt Heather Wilkinson, as well as the attempted murder of his uncle Ian Wilkinson.\nMs Patterson will defend the charges.\n4 A warning has been issued against the viral trend of mushroom foraging. AFP via Getty Images\nDeath cap mushrooms are known to thrive a couple of weeks after wet weather and have been previously sighted in Canberra, Melbourne, Adelaide, and Tasmania.\nThe Food Safety Information Council issued a statement on Sunday, warning Australians who want to partake in wild mushroom foraging to be \u201cextremely careful\u201d if they want to avoid \u201cdeadly\u201d death cap mushroom poisoning.\nSocial media influencers who forage for wild food have encouraged a spike in recreational mushroom gathering across Australia, but Food Safety Information Council chair Cathy Moir wants to emphasize the \u201clife-threatening\u201d risks involved.\n4 Erin Patterson was charged with the murder of Don and Gail Patterson, and Heather Wilkinson and the attempted murder of Ian Wilkinson. 7 News\n\u201cWe are particularly concerned about this growing online promotion of mushroom foraging with some wild mushroom social media groups having over 40,000 members seeking to have their photos of mushrooms identified by fellow foragers,\u201d she said.\nInfluencers and search engines like Google are often not reliable sources for accurately identifying Australian mushrooms as they often only recognize Northern Hemisphere mushrooms and fungi species are known to change appearance during their growth cycle.\nWhat to do if you think you\u2019ve eaten a death cap mushroom?\nThe poison in a quarter of a cap of one death cap mushroom, if eaten, is enough to kill a healthy adult and the toxin cannot be destroyed by peeling, cooking, or drying.\n4 Heather Wilkinson and Ian Wilkinson Provided\nSymptoms of poisoning include vomiting, diarrhea, and stomach cramps and usually appear 10 to 16 hours after eating.\nThese symptoms may ease for two to three days before a terminal phase of begins on day three or four.\nWithout early, effective medical intervention people may go into a coma and die after two or three weeks of liver and kidney failure.\n4 The Latrobe Valley Law Courts building AFP via Getty Images\nWhile most of the deaths from mushroom poisoning in Australia result from death cap mushrooms there are other species of wild mushrooms that have been known to make people seriously ill.\nThese include the Cortinarius and Galerina species, the ghost mushroom (which is commonly mistaken for oyster mushrooms), and the yellow stainer which resembles a field mushroom.\n\u201c[They] are difficult to distinguish from some other wild mushrooms so we recommend you play it safe and only eat mushrooms that you have purchased from a supermarket, greengrocer or other reputable source,\u201d Ms Moir said.\n\u201cWe recommend supporting local mushroom growers especially as they lost sales last year after extensive media coverage of alleged death cap poisonings.\u201d\nShe added that people who want to grow mushrooms at home should be careful not to accidentally contaminate them with wild spores, which could be toxic.",

            "title": "Japan Ice Cream Market Size and Trend Analysis by 2031",
            "description": "Ice cream is a popular dessert in Japan and holds a significant presence in the country's food culture.\u00a0Japan Ice Cream Market\u00a0is estimated to grow",
            "content": "Ice cream is a popular dessert in Japan and holds a significant presence in the country\u2019s food culture. Japan Ice Cream Market is estimated to grow at a CAGR of 2.96% for the forecast period between FY2024-FY2031 and reach USD 6.77 billion in FY2031 from USD 5.36 billion in FY2023. Factors such as changing consumer preferences, innovation in flavours and formats, and a focus on health and wellness are likely to drive the market. Additionally, increased emphasis on convenience, e-commerce, and expanding retail channels are expected to contribute to the market\u2019s expansion.\nThere has been a growing demand for premium and artisanal ice cream products in Japan. Consumers are increasingly seeking unique and high-quality flavours, textures, and ingredients. Ice cream manufacturers are focusing on creating innovative and experiential products to attract consumers. Seasonal and limited-edition ice cream products are popular in Japan, especially during festivals and specific times of the year. These products often feature flavours and designs that align with a particular season or occasion. Though, traditional yet unique flavours such as matcha (green tea), black sesame, wasabi, sakura (cherry blossom), hojicha (roasted green tea), yuzu (Japanese citrus), and azuki bean are a preferred choice of consumers, there is also a demand for international flavours such as chocolate, vanilla, strawberry, and more adventurous options.\nRecently, in May 2023, the Japanese ice cream brand Cellato, by Omer, introduced the world\u2019s more expensive ice cream costing over USD 6400 which made it gain a title in Guinness World Records.\nDownload Free Sample Report@ https://www.marketsandata.com/industry-reports/japan-ice-cream-market/sample-request\nImpulse Ice Creams, a Highly Preferred Choice\nOn-the-go or single-serve ice creams are referred to as impulse ice creams which are widely popular in Japan as their unique packaging and bit-sized portion make them a preferred option among consumers who want to have a quick consumption of ice cream. Impulse ice creams lead the Japan ice cream market with a prominent market share followed by take-home ice cream which is forecast to register a faster growth rate owing to the increasing consumption of ice cream during winter.\nIncreasing Popularity of Vegan Ice Creams\nVeganism has been gaining popularity across the globe including Japan where more than 2% of the country\u2019s population (over 2.5 million people) are vegan. Other than veganism, lactose intolerance, shifting changes in consumer lifestyles, and environmental concerns are all factors behind the switch towards a plant-based diet which in turn is fueling the demand for non-dairy-based ice creams in Japan. Currently, there are not many options for vegan ice creams in Japan and this can act as a great opportunity for market players who are willing to expand their presence in Japan or are looking towards broadening their product portfolio.\nGarigari-Kun, a popsicle by a Japanese ice cream company Akagi Nyugyo is an icy treat that comes in a wide range of flavors such as strawberry, cola, pineapple, and pink grapefruit also suitable for vegans. Nearly 400 million of these popsicles are consumed in Japan, annually.\nUnique Packaging and Focus on Textures & Toppings\nManufacturers are continuously introducing new and innovative flavours to cater to these changing consumer preferences. Texture plays a crucial role in consumer preferences for ice cream. Japanese consumers often value a smooth, creamy, and rich mouthfeel in their ice cream. Products with unique textures, such as chewy mochi or crunchy toppings, are also well-received. To stay competitive and meet evolving consumer preferences, ice cream manufacturers in Japan constantly innovate and introduce new flavours, formats, and experiences. They closely monitor consumer trends and preferences to adapt their product offerings accordingly. For instance, Lotte Co., Ltd., a Japanese confectionery company, produced an ice cream Yukimi Daifuku which is a mochi covered vanilla ice cream. Moreover, Japanese manufacturers focus on featuring impressive, eye-catchy and innovative packaging designs, especially to attract children as their audience.\nHealth and Wellness Trends\nHealth and wellness trends have had a significant impact on the ice cream market, including in Japan, though dairy-free products have a small market in Japan. Gradually, the demand for premium and added-value products is growing in the country. Consumers are seeking healthier options in their food choices, including ice cream. This has led to a rise in demand for ice cream products that are lower in sugar, fat, and calories. To their surprise, when Shizuoka-based EECO, a Japanese producer of organic foods, rebranded their handmade organic plant-based ice cream in the middle of 2020, it quickly sold out. Manufacturers have responded by developing healthier alternatives, such as low-sugar, low-fat, and reduced-calorie ice creams. Plant-based ice creams, often made from ingredients like almond milk, coconut milk, or soy milk, provide alternatives for those who avoid or are intolerant to dairy products. These options have gained traction in the market, catering to a growing segment of health-conscious and environmentally-conscious consumers. The main factor influencing this shift is health concerns.\nImpact of COVID-19 on the Japan Ice Cream Market\nThe COVID-19 pandemic had a notable impact on the ice cream market in Japan, as it had on the global food and beverage industry. With the implementation of lockdowns, social distancing measures, and temporary closures of food service establishments, there was a notable decline in out-of-home consumption of ice cream. Ice cream parlours, cafes, and restaurants faced reduced foot traffic or complete closures, leading to a significant decrease in sales from these channels. Consumer concerns about safety and hygiene increased during the pandemic. Ice cream manufacturers and retailers implemented strict safety measures, such as enhanced sanitation protocols, contactless delivery options, and modified packaging to ensure consumer confidence and address health and safety concerns. The impact of COVID-19 on the ice cream market continues to evolve as the situation progresses. The industry\u2019s recovery and future growth will depend on factors such as vaccination rates, changes in consumer behaviour, and the overall economic landscape.\nJapan Ice Cream Market: Report Scope\n\u201cJapan Ice Cream Market Assessment, Opportunities, and Forecast, FY2017-FY2031F\u201d, is a comprehensive report by Markets & Data, providing in-depth analysis and assessment of the current scenario of the ice cream market in Japan, industry dynamics and opportunities, and forecasts (FY2024-FY2031).             "title": "Ube: What does it mean when a traditional food becomes a trend?",
            "description": "Critics and trend forecasters have been hailing Ube as the \"next big thing\" for nearly a decade",
            "content": "Prior to the new year, California-based T. Hasegawa USA declared in its 2024 Food and Beverage Flavor Trends Report that ube will be the flavor of the year. The bright, purple tuberous root hailing from the Philippines has enjoyed a surge in popularity in recent years. But this isn\u2019t the first time the ingredient has been superficially lauded \u2014 existing merely as something punchy and not anything more than that.\nBack in 2014, Filipino cuisine as a whole was thrown around as the \"next big thing\u201d thanks to Andrew Zimmern. The \u201cBizarre Foods\u201d host claimed the trend was \u201cjust starting\u201d and would officially take off two years later in 2016.\n\u201cI think it\u2019s going to take another year and a half to get up to critical mass, but everybody loves Chinese food, Thai food, Japanese food, and it\u2019s all been exploited. The Filipinos combined the best of all of that with Spanish technique,\u201d Zimmern told NBC\u2019s Today at the time. \u201cThe Spanish were a colonial power there for 500 years, and they left behind adobo and cooking in vinegar \u2014 techniques that, applied to those tropical Asian ingredients, are miraculous.\u201d\nIndustry trend forecasters soon began claiming the same. In 2015, National Geographic wrote, \u201cThe Filipino Food Wave Is Coming\u201d while Mashable declared that Filipino cuisine would be a \u201chuge food trend you'll devour in 2016.\u201d When the trend failed to take off as anticipated, culinary personalities and industry professionals once again attempted to push it as the hottest trend in waiting. Anthony Bourdain jumped on the bandwagon and so did restaurateur April Bloomfield, who called Filipino food the \"next big thing.\u201d\nMuch of the hype surrounding ube solely fixates on its aesthetics. Ube has a distinctive nutty, vanilla-like flavor profile, but praise is mainly awarded to its bright violet hue, which provides a pop of color in savory dishes, desserts and baked goods. That\u2019s why ube has been called the \u201cnew IT Food on Instagram\u201d and most recently, on TikTok. There\u2019s minimal focus, however, on Ube\u2019s rich history and its cultural significance. Many have failed to note that ube (which is not to be confused with purple sweet potato) comes from the yam's name in Tagalog, the national language of the Philippines.\nRarely will trend forecasters add that ube is not just a food, but also a symbol of Filipino resilience. Ube Halaya \u2014 a classic Filipino dessert made of purple yam, coconut milk, and butter \u2014 is commonly served during special occasions as an emblem of both celebration and togetherness.\nUbe is also just a sliver of Filipino cuisine, which isn\u2019t regarded as mainstream in the same way as Mexican and Chinese food. In fact, Filipino cuisine is regarded as a \u201cmonolithic cuisine,\u201d said chef and former co-host of the \u201cRacist Sandwich Podcast\u201d Soleil Ho in a 2017 Thrillist report. Simply calling it a trend doesn\u2019t do any justice to Filipino chefs or the greater Filipino community, Ho added. Food journalists and major publications aren\u2019t supporting specific Filipino chefs, their cookbooks or their upcoming projects \u2014 they\u2019re solely ogling at the cuisine. In 2017, when Scandinavian food (or New Nordic food) grew increasingly popular throughout the United States, the media\u2019s treatment of the cuisine was vastly different:\n\u201cAll of these stories about Filipino food being the next big trend \u2014 they don't tend to have lists of chefs of restaurants that translate into immediately benefiting the community,\u201d Ho told the outlet. \u201cBut when you look at these New Nordic stories, there are links to Amazon pages for cookbooks chefs have written and info on how to get reservations at a chef's next project.\u201d\nWe need your help to stay independent Subscribe today to support Salon's progressive journalism\nThat\u2019s all to say that in most cases, merely eating a specific cuisine isn\u2019t an act of respect towards the people and communities behind it. Casting a specific cuisine \u2014 or key ingredients within a cuisine \u2014 as a trend signifies that it\u2019s fleeting. One minute it\u2019s hot, the next minute it\u2019s not and pushed to the side, ultimately left to be forgotten. That\u2019s not how traditional food should be regarded.\nIn recent years, there has been much discourse about using the term \u201cethnic\u201d to describe food. The term itself means \u201ccharacteristic of or belonging to a non-Western cultural tradition,\u201d which isn\u2019t overtly racist, but has become so covertly. \u201cEthnic\u201d implies that food made by immigrants \u2014 particularly immigrants of color \u2014 is cheap and inferior. You\u2019ll rarely see French, German or Italian food be labeled as such. But take a look at Indian, Ethiopian or Filipino cuisines and the \u201cethnic\u201d label will more often than not be there.\nWant more great food writing and recipes? Subscribe to Salon Food's newsletter, The Bite.\n\u201cImmigrants' identities are deeply tied to the foods we bring with us,\u201d wrote Lavanya Ramanathan in her piece, titled \u201cWhy everyone should stop calling immigrant food ethnic,\u201d for The Washington Post. \u201cWhen we hear our cuisine described as exotic, hodgepodge, greasy or cheap, you might as well be remarking disdainfully about our clothes or skin color.\u201d\nCategorizing a traditional cuisine or food as both \u201cethnic\u201d and a \u201ctrend\u201d thus feels like a double-whammy. There\u2019s also the issue of who determines what constitutes a trend. Typically, it\u2019s white folks \u2014 white journalists, white foodies, white culinary experts \u2014 who are making such declarations. According to data obtained by Zippia, Inc., an online recruitment service, 67.3% of food writers are white, as of 2021. Comparatively, 12.3% of writers are Hispanic or Latino, 9.5% are Asian and 6.3% are Black. White approval and acceptance basically determines whether a food or cuisine is \u201ctrendy.\u201d They also determine whether a food or cuisine is worthy of public acclaim, which is, well, wrong.\nAs for ube, the tuberous root is becoming more widespread in Western grocery stores and restaurants. Trader Joe\u2019s notably sells a slew of ube-themed food items, including ube flavored ice cream, Ube Mochi Pancake & Waffle Mix and Ube Spread. Many online critics have complained about their lack of authentic ube flavor, saying it\u2019s non-existent in some products and downright disappointing in others: \"It's them trying to profit off of our culture without paying any tribute to us,\" wrote one user on Reddit. As for where folks can enjoy delicious, bona fide ube, many recommended checking out local Filipino bakeries, Filipino grocers and Asian stores.",

After years in the biz, we\u2019re pretty confident about our trends forecast, FYI.\nWholesome eating instead of restricting\nBlue Apron\nFirst things first \u2014 it\u2019s important to recognize that everyone\u2019s diet and lifestyle is different, so always consult with a dietitian or nutritionist for advice on what may work best for you. However, it\u2019s safe to say nobody is privy to restrictive diets (you know, the \u201cit\u2019s Jan. 1, so I\u2019m going to live off of kale and only eat X amount of portions\u201d type of living).\nFor that reason, the New York Post predicts \u2014 more or less, knows \u2014 that wholesome eating is a surefire \u201cin\u201d for 2024. Focusing on a balanced diet in mind, there are a variety of meal kits to look into (not to mention, we tested 20+ of them for years). Blue Apron happens to be one of our favorites for customized recipes and a rotisserie of options.\nEye creams, eye creams and more eye creams\nAmazon\nIf we may say so ourselves, eye cream is an underrated step in the skincare process. From our experience speaking with board-certified dermatologists, they likely consider eye cream to be an optional step in between your favorite cleanser and moisturizer \u2014 though they\u2019ll typically mention how eye cream can be the cherry on top of your skincare sundae.\nWhat\u2019s out? The seven-step skincare routine. Plus, they\u2019re adorable, chic and affordable.\nMuted pink colors over hot pink tones\nAnthropologie\nAs an aside from the \u201cBarbiecore\u201d days of 2023, the New York Post doesn\u2019t deem pink out of style.             "title": "Study bolsters evidence that severe obesity increasing in young US kids",
            "description": "A new study adds to evidence that severe obesity is becoming more common in young U.S. children. There was some hope that children in a government food program might be bucking a trend in obesity rates.",
            "content": "NEW YORK (AP) \u2014 A new study adds to evidence that severe obesity is becoming more common in young U.S. children.\nThere was some hope that children in a government food program might be bucking a trend in obesity rates \u2014 earlier research found rates were dropping a little about a decade ago for those kids. But an update released Monday in the journal Pediatrics shows the rate bounced back up a bit by 2020.\nThe increase echoes other national data, which suggests around 2.5% of all preschool-aged children were severely obese during the same period.\n\u201cWe were doing well and now we see this upward trend,\u201d said one of the study\u2019s authors, Heidi Blanck of the U.S. Centers for Disease Control and Prevention. \u201cWe are dismayed at seeing these findings.\u201d\nThe study looked at children ages 2 to 4 enrolled in the Women, Infants and Children program, which provides healthy foods and other services to preschool-aged children in low-income families. The children were weighed and measured.\nThe researchers found that 2.1% of kids in the program were severely obese in 2010. Six years later, the rate had dipped to 1.8%. But by 2020, it was 2%. That translates to about 33,000 of more than 1.6 million kids in the WIC program.\nSignificant increases were seen in 20 states with the highest rate in California at 2.8%. There also were notable rises in some racial and ethnic groups. The highest rate, about 2.8%, was in Hispanic kids.\nExperts say severe obesity at a very early age is nearly irreversible, and is strongly associated with chronic health problems and an early death.\nIt\u2019s not clear why the increase occurred, Blanck said.\nWhen WIC obesity rates dropped, some experts attributed it to 2009 policy changes that eliminated juice from infant food packages, provided less saturated fat, and tried to make it easier to buy fruits and vegetables.\nThe package hasn\u2019t changed. But \u201cthe daily hardships that families living in poverty are facing may be harder today than they were 10 years ago, and the slight increases in the WIC package just weren\u2019t enough,\u201d said Dr. Sarah Armstrong, a Duke University childhood obesity researcher.\nThe researchers faced challenges. The number of kids in WIC declined in the past decade. And the study period included 2020, the year the COVID-19 pandemic hit, when fewer parents brought their children in to see doctors. That reduced the amount of complete information available.\nDespite it\u2019s limitations, it was a \u201cvery well done study,\u201d said Deanna Hoelscher, a childhood obesity researcher at the UTHealth Houston School of Public Health, \u201cIt gives you a hint of what\u2019s going on.\u201d\nWhat\u2019s happened since 2020 is not yet known. Some small studies have suggested a marked increase in childhood obesity \u2014 especially during the pandemic, when kids were kept home from schools, eating and bedtime routines were disrupted and physical activity decreased.\n\u201cWe are thinking it\u2019s going to get worse,\u201d Hoelscher said.\n___\nThe Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute\u2019s Science and Educational Media Group. The AP is solely responsible for all content.",

            "title": "Millions more Americans were food insecure in 2022 than 2021 -USDA",
            "description": "Millions more Americans had difficulty securing enough food in 2022 compared to the year prior, including 1 million households with children, interrupting a years-long trend of declining hunger in the U.S., according to a report from the U.S. Department of Agriculture released on Monday.",
            "content": "Ellie Amador picks up a United States Department of Agriculture (USDA) Farmers to Families food box as food is distributed at the nonprofit New Life Centers' food pantry in Chicago, Illinois, U.S. March 16, 2021. REUTERS/Daniel Acker/File Photo Acquire Licensing Rights\nOct 25 (Reuters) - Millions more Americans had difficulty securing enough food in 2022 compared to the year prior, including 1 million households with children, interrupting a years-long trend of declining hunger in the U.S., according to a report from the U.S. Department of Agriculture released on Monday.\nWhile the USDA did not provide an explanation for the rise, previous reports from food banks and the U.S. Census Bureau have indicated that hunger is up in the U.S. as low-income Americans struggle to recover from the pandemic and from the end of expanded food assistance.\nThe USDA report found that 12.8% of households, or 17 million households, struggled to get enough food in 2022, up from 10.2%, or 13.5 million households, in 2021.\nNearly 7 million of those households faced very low food security, meaning members' normal eating patterns were disrupted or food intake dropped because of limited resources, USDA said.\nFood insecurity had fallen steadily between 2011 and 2021 before spiking in 2022, the report showed.\nRegular surveys by the Census Bureau since the start of the pandemic have also showed rising hunger. More than 27.6 million Americans reported experiencing food scarcity in the most recent survey, conducted between Sept. 20 and Oct. 2, up 9.5% from the start of the year.\nHunger could be exacerbated further if the government shutdown narrowly avoided in September comes to pass in November, anti-hunger groups warn.\nThe USDA said in September that the Women, Infants, and Children program, for instance, would stop distributing benefits to its 7 million participants within days of a shutdown.\n\"WIC is one of our best lines of defense against nutrition insecurity, and Congress must ensure it remains available for anyone who needs it,\" said Nell Menefee-Libbey, public policy manager at the National WIC Association.\nReporting by Leah Douglas; editing by Jonathan Oatis\nOur Standards: The Thomson Reuters Trust Principles.",

            "content": "What do Pete Davidson, Bella Hadid, Jake Gyllenhaal and Travis Scott all have in common, aside from their A-list status? They\u2019ve recently embraced a unique kind of street style \u2014 one that\u2019s emblazoned with logos of famous delicatessens and their most popular offerings, like knishes, pastrami, pickles and bagels.\nAptly dubbed \u201cdelicore,\u201d the latest food-fashion fad celebrates one of New York City\u2019s most prized staples: the local deli. Last September, delis officially earned their \u201cIt\u201d status within the high fashion world when whimsical clothing designer Batsheva Hay debuted her Spring 2023 collection at the famed Ben\u2019s Kosher Deli amid New York Fashion Week. However, prior to then, delis enjoyed their fair share of up-and-coming collaborations. In 2020, Gyllenhaal partnered with Russ & Daughters on a tie-dye salmon-hued T-shirt benefiting the Independent Restaurant Coalition. In 2021, the Hollywood star and deli joined forces again to release a $150 black \u201cLOX\u201d hoodie benefitting the Actors Fund. And in the following year, Coach and gourmet emporium Zabar\u2019s collaborated to put out limited-edition swag, including a $495 sweater and a $550 leather tote.\nOutside of high fashion, several delis \u2014 both old and new \u2014 began selling their own merchandise in the wake of pandemic shutdowns. Desperate for new revenue streams, restaurants and shops turned to retail, wrote Maggie Hennessy for Bon App\u00e9tit. Their efforts were ultimately successful and even captured the attention of countless celebrities. Take for example Davidson, Scott and former New York Giants wide receiver Victor Cruz, who have all sported Uncle Paulie\u2019s hats from Uncle Paulie\u2019s Deli.\nSure, delicore may have the word \u201cdeli\u201d in its name, but it\u2019s not exclusively being claimed by delis. The tasty trend is also a prominent aesthetic in Old Jewish Men (OJM), a popular social-media-account-turned-fashion-brand. OJM first began on Instagram about five-and-a-half years ago when founder Noah Rinsky was visiting his parents who retired in northern Israel. Amid the pandemic, Rinsky and his co-partner Bryan Seversky expanded OJM into a clothing company that specializes in comfort wear, like T-shirts, hats and sweaters. Think of the kinds of clothes commonly seen in Adam Sandler and Larry David\u2019s wardrobe.\nThe crux of OJM is right in its name \u2014 old Jewish men. And because delis have roots in Jewish culture, history and cuisine, it only made sense for OJM to adopt the trend into its clothes.\n\u201c[For] old Jewish men, one of their natural gathering points is the deli,\u201d explained Seversky. \u201cSo it makes sense for us as a brand to kind of celebrate that culture\u2026It's celebrating this golden era that in the future, won't exist since these old guys are keeping it alive.\u201d\nWe need your help to stay independent Subscribe today to support Salon's progressive journalism\nA few deli-themed OJM apparel include its Pickle Princess Crop Tank, a green-hued tank adorned with silver gems; and its Brain on Drugs shirt, a simple white tee with an image of a plain and severely burnt bagel. OJM also has a Mets-style \u201cMeats\u201d jersey, which was inspired by a bizarre, meat-fueled dream:\n\u201cMy friend Will had this sweaty, meat-induced fever dream one night where he had this idea of combining the Mets with a meat design. And I realized that there\u2019s such a strong connection between the Mets, which is the unofficial old Jewish man\u2019s baseball team in New York, and deli culture, which is also huge with old Jewish men.\nWant more great food writing and recipes? It started off with simple T-shirts and hats and then, we created a jersey.\u201d\nAs for Seversky\u2019s thoughts on why OJM\u2019s deli-themed apparel have been a major hit amongst consumers, he said that delicore, as a whole, celebrates a persevering, widely-loved industry. Such clothing invokes the same feelings of pride and loyalty as a sports jersey or a band tee.\n\u201cWhen you have a piece of clothing that is the branding of the deli, it\u2019s almost like wearing a sports jersey or a band shirt,\u201d Seversky said. \u201cYou're showing allegiance to something. I think what people are drawn to about the deli is that it\u2019s a spot for the everyday man. Delis are steeped in tradition and tap into our desire for comfort and nostalgia. That being said, a casual clothing brand coupled with good deli eats just makes sense.             "title": "Is 'girl dinner' healthy",
            "description": "\"Girl dinner\" is the latest food fad, with #girldinner gaining 1.3 billion views on TikTok. Many have branded the trend \"disturbing\" \u2014 but there might be more nutritional value to this snacklike dinner than one might think.",
            "content": "\u201cGirl dinner\u201d is the latest food trend, with the hashtag #girldinner gaining a whopping 1.3 billion views on TikTok.\nWomen everywhere are showing off what they pulled together from their fridges and pantries to have as a low-effort dinner.\nMaybe you put a plate together of some leftover slices of deli meat, fruits and cheese and crackers rather than making a full-out meal.\nIt takes just a few minutes and you use practically no energy: That is a girl dinner.\nSelf-proclaimed \u201cgirl dinner\u201d creator Olivia Maher claimed in a May TikTok that \u201cin medieval times peasants had to eat nothing but bread and cheese,\u201d arguing that the scraps are actually what she would consider her \u201cideal meal.\u201d\nMany have branded the trend \u201cdisturbing\u201d \u2014 but there might be more nutritional value to this snacklike dinner than one might think.\nA sort of charcuterie board for one, the \u201cgirl dinner\u201d can be a healthy option if there\u2019s enough variety.\n\u201cAs long as you\u2019re centering your plate around a protein source and including healthy fats and fibers, then you can add other fun stuff to your plate,\u201d Olivia Amitrano said. Getty Images\nWebMD medical team member Dr. Neha Pathak told The Post that \u201ca \u2018girl dinner\u2019 gives you the chance to take back the joy, focus on you and be mindful of what your body needs without having to worry about anyone else.\n\u201cWhenever I prescribe healthy-balanced meals made up mostly of fruits and veggies, I know that for a lot of people that\u2019s overwhelming and too much work. \u201c\nPathak \u2014 who is dual board-certified in internal medicine and lifestyle medicine \u2014 stressed the importance of making sure your plate has filling and healthy portion sizes, with enough protein, vegetables and carbohydrates.\n\u201cYou just want to be sure you\u2019re making the meal about a healthy, joyful you, and not making the meal all about disease-causing, unhealthy processed foods,\u201d Pathak shared. \u201cPretty is the point \u2026 you want your plate to be colorful and beautiful.\n\u201cAs a married, lifestyle medicine physician mom with three daughters, there\u2019s actually nothing I look forward to more than a night that\u2019s just a \u2018girl dinner,'\u201d Pathak said, adding that she\u2019s all for taking the extra step of adding \u201cgirl lunches\u201d and \u201cgirl breakfasts\u201d to the mix.\nOlivia Amitrano, the \u201cOG wellness influencer,\u201d told The Post that the \u201cgirl dinner\u201d trend can actually be \u201cone of the most balanced ways\u201d to prepare a meal for the gut microbiome \u2014 which is integral to digestion and generating nutrients \u2014 and to enrich gut diversity.\nAmitrano, founder of wellness brand Organic Olivia, shared that gut diversity is not about the number of good \u201cbugs\u201d within our gut microbiome, but rather the richness and diversity of the bacteria.\n\u201cGirl dinner\u201d doesn\u2019t have to be all healthy foods \u2014 there\u2019s still room for treats. Getty Images\nSo rather than dosing our bodies with probiotics, achieving gut diversity should be done through eating fiber and greens from diverse sources.\nIn fact, a 2019 report from the environmental watchdog World Wildlife Fund found that 75% of the food we consume comes from just 12 plant sources and five animal sources\n\u201cWe\u2019re grabbing the same food staples from the grocery stores over and over again and we get stuck in these ruts of meals that we hyper-fixate on, and we eat the same thing every day because it\u2019s easy or we have it prepped or we have the same Sweetgreen order,\u201d Amitrano explained, noting the importance of expanding one\u2019s palate to a range of plants and fibers.\nGirls everywhere are showing off what they pulled together from their fridges and pantries for a low-effort dinner. Getty Images/iStockphoto\nAmitrano said that her \u201cperfect girl dinner\u201d would be protein-forward, centering on cottage cheese as a dip/sauce/base for a hefty serving of protein, adding some cut-up veggies for color on the plate, as well as a source of carbs such as a piece of toast with hummus or avocado to get healthy fat in the mix.\n\u201cI\u2019m putting together all these little leftover fun things that are in my fridge, and I actually end up making a far more diverse meal than I would have if I just had my usual chicken salad that I make every single night of the week,\u201d she said.\nShe believes having a \u201cgirl dinner\u201d presents an opportunity to try unusual, fibrous plants and foods that one wouldn\u2019t normally consider as a side dish \u2014 pickles and other fermented items, for example.\nAmitrano also observed that meals leading to higher levels of gut diversity could have health benefits for women with polycystic ovary syndrome or patients with Type 2 diabetes.\n\u201cYou want to get as many different plants and odd foods into your diet per week as possible, and \u2018girl dinner\u2019 is a great way to do that,\u201d Amitrano said.\nRather than dosing our bodies with probiotics, achieving gut diversity can be done through eating diverse sources of fiber and plants. Getty Images/iStockphoto\nBut \u201cgirl dinner\u201d doesn\u2019t have to be all healthy foods \u2014 there\u2019s still room for treats.\n\u201cAs long as you\u2019re centering your plate around a protein source and including healthy fats and fibers, then you can add other fun stuff to your plate, like chips or pretzels or a glass of wine, whatever else you want to put in there that\u2019s fun, as long as you have the base of protein and fiber first,\u201d Amitrano added.\nHowever, the trend arrives amid criticism that TikTok promotes \u201ctoxic\u201d diet culture and \u201cglorifies\u201d excessive weight loss, and alongside an alarming rise in adolescent eating disorders since the COVID-19 pandemic.\nAmitrano noted that unhealthy \u201cgirl dinners\u201d can happen because people are too exhausted to put together a nourishing meal.\n\u201cSometimes \u2018girl dinners\u2019 on TikTok can look a little sad \u2014 it\u2019s just too little food,\u201d she said. \u201cI think that there is a risk there that we might not be meeting our caloric needs. Just eating enough is so important when you\u2019re having \u2018girl dinner.\u2019\n\u201cYou want to make sure you\u2019re looking at it as an opportunity for abundance and having fun with what\u2019s left on your fridge and putting a lot on your plate and seeing what you gravitate towards, and not limiting yourself or judging yourself,\u201d Amitrano continued. \u201cDon\u2019t look at it as a diet trend or a way to eat less, but look at it as a way to have more fun with your food and not pigeonhole yourself into that same meal rut that we get stuck in.\u201d\nWhether or not one should be worried by the increase of the trend is a concern of context and pattern.\nEating disorders specialist Chase Bannister told The Post that it\u2019s unreasonable to regard one single meal as a cause for alarm.\nThe trend comes amid lots of criticism that TikTok promotes \u201ctoxic\u201d diet culture and \u201cglorifies\u201d weight loss, as well as the alarming rise of adolescent eating disorders since the COVID-19 pandemic. Getty Images\nHowever, if an eating habit arises incapable of meeting nutritional needs, \u201cthat\u2019s a red flag moment.\n\u201cIt\u2019s no crime to have fun with food. That said, this trend raises some important questions, both clinically and culturally,\u201d Bannister said.\nOne big concern to Bannister is what could happen when people \u201coverlook the thinly-veiled misogyny of these words,\u201d he said.\n\u201cWhat makes food \u2018girl food\u2019? And, perhaps bluntly, when surveying the landscape of images, why are there so many salads and so few cheeseburgers? I do wonder what the perfectly-sized, perfectly-presented \u2018girl\u2019 of the \u2018girl dinner\u2019 is supposed to look like. And what are the slow, creeping costs of the performative social media enterprise upon bodies and minds?\u201d he questioned.\nBannister continued, \u201cAs a clinician, these questions concern me greatly, but these questions escape the bounds of the therapy session. As people, we need to call attention to potentially risky behaviors, to the commercialization of bodies, and to the problem of conflating our encounters with food, body, weight, size, and appearance with our human value.\n\u201cWe owe ourselves \u2014 and each other \u2014 better than that.\u201d\nBut Bannister, founder of the Veritas Collaborative system of eating disorders treatment centers, added that it\u2019s absolutely possible to have these non-traditional meals without engaging in disordered eating.\n\u201cWe find ways to nourish our bodies while leading busy lives, traveling or in myriad social situations, and few of us have endlessly stocked pantries to craft a perfectly balanced meal every moment our body needs energy or gives us hunger cues,\u201d he said. \u201cGetting creative with the foods we have available to us is part of being human.\u201d",

            "title": "Korean corn dogs are, frankly, a hit from N.Y. to Kansas",
            "description": "Buoyed by the growing popularity of Korean culture \u2014 K-pop, K-dramas, K-beauty and K-food \u2014 nicknamed the Korean wave or \u201cHallyu,\u201d Korean corn dogs rode a wave of popularity with influencers and trend-chasing diners.",
            "content": "For the weekend before her 30th birthday, Mirina Landry was \u201ccraving some kind of new experience,\u201d but didn\u2019t have time to travel far. So she and her partner drove the hour from Topeka to Overland Park, Kansas, to try Korean corn dogs, the batter-dipped meat or cheese on a stick, often dusted with sugar, ramen noodles, puffed rice or an increasing arms race of toppings and sauces.\n\u201cI\u2019ve been seeing a lot of stuff on social media with the hot dogs and the mozzarella, but also with different flavors like Hot Cheetos and stuff like that. It all looks so good,\u201d Landry, who described herself as \u201cwhite as white can be,\u201d said after her visit to K-Street Hotdog, where she opted for one made of mozzarella cheese and encrusted with fried potato cubes.\nKorean corn dogs were one of the biggest food trends in 2021. That summer, newly vaxxed and guided by TikTok and Instagram, people waited in lines an hour deep at outposts like Two Hands or Oh K-Dog NYC in New York. Buoyed by the growing popularity of Korean culture \u2014 K-pop, K-dramas, K-beauty and K-food \u2014 nicknamed the Korean wave or \u201cHallyu,\u201d the photogenically crispy, gooey, portable snack rode a wave of popularity with influencers and trend-chasing diners.\nBut even as the hype subsided in diverse coastal hot spots like New York and Los Angeles, the Korean corn dog has maintained \u2014 and even increased \u2014 its following as franchises like Two Hands and Ssong\u2019s Hotdog expand their footprint into the American heartland. Hundreds are scheduled to open in Arkansas, Kansas, Texas and Missouri, in cities not known to be hubs of Asian food, a testament to the enduring popularity of Korean food and to its adoption into an expanding American palate.\nMirina Landry at K-Street Hotdog in Overland Park, Kan. Courtesy of Mirina Landry\nFrom a postwar processed treat to food trend\nUnlike American corn dogs, Korean corn dogs (called hot dogs in Korea) use a wheat or mochi (rice flour) batter in place of cornmeal, resulting in a chewier texture. They\u2019re made of a meat hot dog or a mozzarella stick or half of each, or fish cake, which was the original Korean version, a culmination of both the Japanese occupation of the early 20th century and the longstanding presence of U.S. troops after World War II.\nProcessed meats such as wieners, sausages and hams were known to Koreans in postwar South Korea due to the U.S. military presence. Jooyeon Rhee, Penn State Institute for Korean Studies\n\u201cProcessed meats such as wieners, sausages and hams were known to Koreans in postwar South Korea due to the U.S. military presence, but they were used mainly as ingredients for army stew (budae jjigae). What Koreans call \u2018hot dogs\u2019 are really corn dogs that became a popular street food from the mid-1970s,\u201d said Jooyeon Rhee, an associate professor and the director of the Penn State Institute for Korean Studies.\nIn the 1970s, South Korea was a low-income country under a military dictatorship, and meat such as pork or beef was hard to come by. So, the original corn dogs were a mixture of flour and fish, a derivative of the Japanese fishcake \u201ckamaboko.\u201d\n\u201cThe wieners, more than the fried dough, were the main reason for the popularity of the Korean corn dogs among schoolchildren. Imagine how school kids became utterly disappointed when the wieners inside their corn dogs were much smaller than usual on some days. Or worse, there were no wieners at all, which happened from time to time: You could not tell whether there was one unless you digged deep enough to see the stick,\u201d Rhee said, speaking from personal experience.\n\u201cCorn dogs were a thing of the past when Koreans had not yet been exposed to Western foods, but they were revived through nostalgia marketing about a decade ago. A number of corn dog franchises emerged, and major food companies now produce and export packaged corn dogs to many countries,\u201d she added.\n\u2018We literally don\u2019t have any Korean customers\u2019: How K-dog popularity grew across the U.S.\nFood industry veterans say that even with the swiftness of social media phenomena, trends can take a while longer to travel nationally but can have a longer shelf life.\n\u201cKansas is a little different, trends are a lot slower. Stuff like that took off in bigger cities a lot earlier, even the yogurt ice creams [Korean chains like Red Mango] took off in big states, died off and then came to smaller states,\u201d said David Ahn, 43, one of the officers of the company that owns K-Street Hotdog.\nA lot of younger people are interested in stuff like this. A lot of Latinas, Caucasians and African American people, too. Mostly because of K-pop. David Ahn\nAhn\u2019s family business already owned a Korean grocery store, bakery and three Korean fried chicken restaurants in the Kansas City area, when they considered adding to their Korean culinary offerings. In 2021, they opened a Ssong\u2019s franchise, which was initially very successful and then plateaued. After severing ties with the chain, the Ahns rebranded as K-Street Hotdog earlier this year, using their own recipes and building out the menu with side items like the popular tornado fries and hotteok, a dessert pancake with a caramelized cinnamon and sesame filling.\n\u201cA lot of the bigger cities rely more on Korean customers, and there\u2019s only so many Korean people. We target Caucasians and African Americans. Because we literally don\u2019t have any Korean customers ... maybe 5%, if that,\u201d Ahn, who emigrated from South Korea when he was 12, said. \u201cA lot of younger people are interested in stuff like this. A lot of Latinas, Caucasians and African American people, too. Mostly because of K-pop.\u201d\nRobin Rhee, president of the Asian food distributor Rhee Bros, has been watching Korean food grow in popularity for the past two decades, and notes that food trends for ethnic food in America can take circuitous paths.\n\u201cWe see a lot of growth in Korean hot dogs and Korean food in general and snacks, in between Sioux City and Kansas City. It\u2019s the college towns. Asian immigrants are moving to middle America for jobs \u2014 Chinese, Cambodian, Hmong. Korean food is not just popular with white Americans but other Asian ethnicities because they\u2019re listening to Korean music, watching dramas. The colleges where Asian Americans are going now are not just UCLA or NYU \u2014 the same goes for Asian international students, and they\u2019re driving their friends to it as well,\u201d he said.\nAbout three years ago, distributors started importing Korean corn dogs to the U.S. at about the same time the franchises started opening. The trend speaks to \u201cthe increasing popularity of Korean food,\u201d he added.\nLast Saturday, Kristy Fitzpatrick, 30, and two of her friends from St. Louis were in Kansas City for a Taylor Swift concert and stopped at K-Street Hotdog. She and one of the friends had tried Korean corn dogs in Phoenix at a local H-Mart, and that had piqued the interest of the other friend.\n\u201cThey were great, we all loved them \u2014 just stinks that there aren\u2019t any shops like that in St. Louis,\u201d she said.\nFor now.",

            "title": "\u2018Girl dinner\u2019 is TikTok\u2019s latest food trend. I\u2019m one step ahead.",

            "content": "But artful ones. Users are displaying girl dinners of cheese and sliced baguette, some fruit and veggies, a draped frill of prosciutto, olives, pickles, a handful of nuts. There doesn\u2019t have to be a whole chicken or juicy steak meatspreading center stage; there doesn\u2019t have to be pasta or potato padding. Girl dinner can be eaten on the couch while binge-watching \u201cThe Bear\u201d or reading a novel or staring into space. Yet it is not compatible with another TikTok term of the moment, \u201c bed rotting \u201d (116 million-plus views), the trend formerly known as depression. Bed-rotting dinner is Doritos from the bag, ice cream from the carton, if it happens at all. It is not cute snacks assembled cutely on a cute plate, a dinner that makes a woman feel self-cared for, nourished, and a touch transgressive. (Girl dinner is also not, as some have said, eating disordered. Gauging and satisfying one\u2019s own appetite is the opposite of that.) Girl dinner is the culinary equivalent of Rep. Maxine Waters repeating over and over, \u201c Reclaiming my time .\u201d Girl dinner is what Lisa Simpson would eat after she moves to New York in her 20s and gets an intellectually rewarding if underpaid job as a junior literary agent.\nOn TikTok, people are currently communing about \u201c girl dinner .\u201d This trend with more than 114 million views showcases the way women eat when we\u2019re alone, no roommates or partners or children to accommodate. In the absence of others\u2019 desires, which we might attempt to intuit or interpret or magically fulfill, it is so easy to hear what we want for ourselves.\nGet The Big To-Do Your guide to staying entertained, from live shows and outdoor fun to the newest in museums, movies, TV, books, dining, and more. Enter Email Sign Up\nI have eaten many girl dinners in my life, and some bed-rotting ones too. But what I\u2019m eating these days takes a few steps deeper into the enchanted realm of IDGAF. Let\u2019s call it Cucumbers With Stuff. It could also, theoretically, be called \u201chot girl dinner\u201d \u2014 but, like, literally hot. It is not likely to spark a TikTok trend.\nAdvertisement\nIt started because I impulsively purchased a wedge of Cambozola, the Shiloh Jolie-Pitt of cheese, merging the best attributes of precursors Camembert and Gorgonzola. Then I got home and thought, great, now I\u2019m going to eat the entire thing myself. I solved the problem by making Cambozola dressing, crumbling it into a mixture of mayo, sour cream, shallots, garlic, lemon juice, and sherry vinegar and mashing it all together. I would serve iceberg wedges with dinner, and the cheese would be shared among everyone at the table, I thought, idly dunking a cucumber spear into the bowl. And then another cucumber spear. Nobody was really around for dinner that night, so I stuck the dressing in the fridge, and then I just kept eating it with cucumbers and didn\u2019t share it the next day or the next, and as predicted, I ate the entire wedge of cheese myself, but also a lot of cucumbers.\nAdvertisement\nThen there was the leftover spicy peanut dressing from some noodles, which I used up by dunking cucumbers in it. Some of those cucumbers I wrapped in wasabi seaweed snacks, and some I blitzed with yuzu furikake. Who wants to eat eat? It\u2019s hot. Cucumbers are cool. They\u2019re the popsicles of the vegetable world. They can be halved, seeds scraped out to create a canoe one can fill with leftover flaked salmon or chicken salad, a vessel by which to neatly cast off and float away from the responsibility of making dinner. Bye bye!\nAdvertisement\nMostly I eat Cucumbers With Stuff standing at the counter. Eventually I stopped pretending there would be entrees to support my condiments, and started making blue cheesy dressings and spicy peanut dressings and whatnot with the cucumbers specifically in mind. Radishes, past-their-prime snap peas blanched halfway back to life, leftover steamed broccoli, and cherry tomatoes have at times joined the chat. But now this is starting to sound fancy, like crudites. It is not. It is lazy-times summer leftovers eaten from containers and cutting boards. There is no cute plate or cute presentation, although there could be. But that would be getting away from the spirit of Cucumbers With Stuff, which has no aspirations toward fashion, aesthetics, TikTokery, trend making, audience growing, or follower gaining. There is nothing performative about it, not even performance that is only for oneself, as with girl dinner. \u201cEat like no one is watching,\u201d as Mark Twain definitely once said. Cucumbers With Stuff has zero million views, and it plans to stay that way.\nDevra First can be reached at devra.first@globe.com. Follow her on Twitter @devrafirst.",

The fact that the cravings can hit you at any time. When you crave something from McDonald's, nothing else goes close to it, at all,\" she explained as she lathered on the Oreo McFlurry mixture to her hash brown.\nRELATED: The Grimace shake won\u2019t kill you: Why are people on TikTok pretending to die after drinking it?\n\"You were worth waiting for,\" she says as she took a bite.\nObviously, the post sparked some debate among users.\n\"'Just an Oreo McFlurry with hash brows\" like it's a common craving,'\" wrote one user in response.\nAnother user suggested taking the hack even further by dipping it into the pancake syrup. \"Life changing,\" they wrote.\nThe official account for McDonald's Australia stitched Arvan's video endorsing the item writing \"Amazing idea.\"\nIn Arvan's video, she describes the sandwich as \"salty, crunch, potato-ey, sweet,\" rating it a \"10 out of 10,\" in the viral video, which has been viewed over 6 million times as of July 5.\nSince Arvan posted her video, it appears that McDonald's has started to run out of McFlurry's and hash browns as teenagers and adults with the munchies began to flock to nearby locations to try the item.\nAlthough Mcdonald's could not confirm the shortage, it is commonly known that most locations report having their ice cream machines to be broken on a regular basis.\nTikToker @bechardgrave revealed in a video posted on May 29 that she had to drive to \"three different McDonald\u2019s to find one that has both soft serve and hash browns available right now.\"\nWhile it may be too early to confirm, it appears that many are crowning this item as the food hack of the year.\n\"I thought this was going to be trash,\" wrote TikToker Foodandlove5.",

            "title": "Gen Z's web-fueled love affair with chain restaurant merch",
            "description": "The slow yet ongoing trend is helping casual fast food chains attain both a loyal customer-base and fan-base",
            "content": "In late 2019, when the pandemic slowly began wreaking havoc in all sectors of life, the food industry was hit hard as many restaurants struggled to stay afloat. Many had to temporarily shut down their services. Others weren't so lucky and had to shut down operations completely.\nAs dreary as it all was, several restaurant and fast food chains used the opportunity to revamp their marketing techniques. That includes creative social media posts \u2014 namely on TikTok, where several businesses, like Wingstop, SONIC and KFC, meshed Gen-Z humor with food advertising. That also includes new menu offerings. And, better yet, nostalgic menu offerings, such as McDonald's limited-time adult Happy Meals.\nA few major chains even released their own merchandise, from seasonal trinkets to full apparel and accessories, and garnered favorable responses from consumers. People were ecstatic when Pizza Hut \u2014 in partnership with Chain \u2014 the Los Angeles based pop-up culinary collective, released its limited-edition reversible Hut Hat. The reversible bucket hats touted Pizza Hut's iconic red roof on one side and a checkered design with black, white and red on the other. Prior to the Hut Hat, there was KFC Russia's limited-edition bucket hats, which are adorned with the brand's iconic red and white stripe pattern and logos, along with the limited-edition clothing line released by KFC's U.K. wing.\nPerhaps the most notable food accessory sensation is Panera's chic and tasty BAGuette bag, which is essentially an elongated green purse that resembles a loaf of French bread. The bags initially sold out, then were restocked only to sell out again. A few bags were later resold on eBay for more than $200. One seller even listed the bag for an astounding $3,290.\nTime and time again, food merchandise has remained a hit amongst customers and those perpetually online alike. So to keep up with the demand, several restaurant chains have launched online merch shops in an effort to boost sales and provide customers with all the food-themed merchandise they can dream of.\nThe ongoing trend first began in 2021, when Cheez-It launched its self-proclaimed \"first ever online store\" \u2014 fittingly called CheezItHQ.com. The online shop has everything from clothing to drinking accessories to fun miscellaneous items. There's Cheez-It-themed socks, beanies and hoodies and a onesie. There's a can koozie, dual wine sleeves, and a wine tumbler. There's even a fanny pack, an apron, and a fleece blanket.\nOf course, because Cheez-It is a famed snack brand, its online marketplace also carries the signature snacks, like an exclusive Extra Toasty Superfan 4-Pack that includes brand new Extra Spicy, Extra Cheesy, and Cheddar Jack varieties with a box of the original version of Cheez-Its Extra Toasty crackers.\n\"The Cheez-It brand is always looking for new ways to connect with fans, and through this online shopping experience we're offering consumers a new platform to enjoy everything they love about Cheez-It in a fresh, exciting way,\" Jordan Narducci, Kellogg's director of global direct to consumer ecommerce, said in an announcement, per Food & Wine. \"This Direct-to-Consumer site gives us the opportunity to bring future food innovations to market faster than ever before, while also collecting feedback from our consumers in real time.\"\nFollowing suit was Red Lobster, which in November 2022, launched their first digital pop-up shop for the holiday season at RedLobsterShop.com. The limited-time-only shop reopened after three years and sold a slew of unique merchandise specific to seafood and the Red Lobster brand. There's an ugly sweater with an insulated pocket for Cheddar Bay Biscuits, a Cheddar Bay Sleigh Ugly Sweater, a Claw Hoodie, Critter Beanie, and a Red Lobster vintage-style snapback cap. There's also a trio of Red Lobster ornaments along with an ornament dedicated to Cheddar, the rare orange lobster that was found and rescued from a Red Lobster location in 2022.\nIn the same vein as Narducci, Red Lobster's chief marketing officer, Patty Trevino said the restaurant's holiday online shop was all for their loyal fans, who could \"bring their love of Red Lobster home for the holidays.\"\nThe most recent addition to the trio is Panera, which opened its first official online merchandise storefront, The Panera Shop, in April of this year. In addition to the iconic BAGuettes, the store offers other Panera-themed swag, or \"carb couture,\" like mac and cheese tracksuits, \"all my friends are bread\" crewnecks, and even a toddler onesie that reads \"Just Baked.\"\nWant more great food writing and recipes? Subscribe to Salon Food's newsletter, The Bite.\nIt's not hard to see that online merch shops are a slow yet ongoing trend in the restaurant industry \u2014 two is a coincidence, but three is a trend, per the age-old adage. As I previously wrote in an April piece on food and fashion, casual fast food continues to be hot and buzzy subjects for content creators, who have garnered significant followings simply by sharing menu hacks, mukbangs and videos of themselves eating their go-to orders from their local Mickey D's. Such content is enticing, attractive and tempting \u2014 much like the food that's being spotlighted. Restaurant-specific merch only adds to that buzzy factor, allowing for restaurants to not only garner a customer-base but also, a fan-base.\nThat, in turn, helps boost revenue and overall sales. In a May 23 announcement, Panera mentioned that it has \"strengthened its leadership in the fast casual segment by expanding its leading digital capabilities, which today account for 53% of total sales at Panera Bread.\" The brand also increased its loyalty base to 53 million members and launched an innovative loyalty subscription model that has resulted in 25% of all Panera transactions now coming from Unlimited Sip Club members. The club is part of Panera's greater reward membership called MyPanera loyalty, which offered exclusive-access to their online store from May 1 through May 7. Other perks include special offers, birthday rewards, discounts and more.\nWhether other casual restaurants will jump on the trend is still to be seen. But it certainly seems pretty likely. Perhaps Pizza Hut will be the next to open up its own online store, where Hut Hats and other pizza-themed paraphernalia will be readily available for purchase.",
            "url": "https://www.salon.com/2023/07/08/gen-zs-web-fueled-love-affair-with-chain-restaurant-merch/",
