        {
            "title": "The high cost of living affecting Canadians\u2019 ability to access healthy food",
            "description": "Canada appears to be a \u201ctrading-down\u201d market, a trend that may persist for some time.",
            "content": "Photo: Pixabay\nCanada appears to be a \u201ctrading-down\u201d market, a trend that may persist for some time.\nRecent data from Statistics Canada on the food retail and service industries and fresh GDP figures paint a dismal picture, especially for those looking to attract more food companies or grocers to our country.\nOur population grew by over three percent last year, yet our GDP increased by less than one percent. While other industrialized economies, such as France and Germany, are experiencing worse economic headwinds, Canada\u2019s economy is highly integrated with the United States, the world\u2019s most robust economy at present.\nDespite our proximity to this economic superpower, the benefits of our geography seem to have stalled. The most alarming aspect of the January GDP numbers is that Canada\u2019s hottest economic sector is currently the public service, while private investments have stalled, primarily due to higher interest rates.\nThe gap in GDP per capita between Canada and the U.S. has widened by 106 percent since 2015, and this trend shows no signs of reversing. In other words, despite our growing population, Canada is becoming poorer, not richer.\nFor those in the food business, this is certainly not good news. Statistics Canada\u2019s reports on food and service sales confirm that consumers are less wealthy while facing higher food and menu prices. As of January 2024, the average Canadian spends $248 a month on food retail sales per capita, down from $258 in January 2023 to $282 in February 2017. These figures are all in real dollars, which makes the situation even worse.\nBased on Canada\u2019s Food Price Report 2024, an individual\u2019s monthly expenditure for a healthy diet should be $339. Again, the current average monthly spending is $248. Until July 2021, Canadians spent more on average than the desired budget to support a healthy diet. Since then, it has clearly been a challenge.\nCanadians are either wasting less or finding alternative ways to source food outside conventional channels like grocery stores, such as dollar stores and non-traditional grocery discounters. Per capita, food expenditures in our country have never been as low as they are now.\nOne might think that grocers are struggling with this situation, but they are readjusting their strategies and putting more pressure on suppliers with higher fees and lower prices. These are perfect conditions for a potential price war later this year, so don\u2019t be surprised if it happens.\nThe data on food service provides a different perspective. On average, Canadians spent $169 at restaurants in January, about the same as last year and an increase from $149 in January 2018. However, these sums are in real dollars. The current retail/service split in Canada is that about 41 percent of all money spent on food is at restaurants, compared to a split closer to 54 percent in the U.S., favouring food service. Given the market\u2019s frugality, it\u2019s surprising to witness such substantial spending at restaurants, where you usually get less value for your money.\nThe days of uncertainty regarding the balance between working from home and working away from home are long gone. The food economy has, for all intents and purposes, normalized. Food inflation is causing Canadians to spend less at grocery stores, which may seem counterintuitive, but this is what the data is telling us. Currently, about 18 percent of all retail dollars are devoted to food, compared to 21 percent in 2017.\nSimply put, the cost of living is a problem for many Canadian households, and trading down is much easier with food. People may be ordering in more often to avoid tips and overpriced beverages, for example.\nAll of this is based on our trust in Statistics Canada, which may not be all that strong. However, Statistics Canada is merely an indicator, and Canadians have no other way to know what is really going on out there other than reading reports from the federal agency. Regardless of how we interpret the data, the numbers are not encouraging.\nThis is what happens when our population grows, but our collective economic wealth stagnates.\nSylvain Charlebois is senior director of the agri-food analytics lab and a professor in food distribution and policy at Dalhousie University.\nThis article is written by or on behalf of an outsourced columnist and does not necessarily reflect the views of Castanet.",

            "publishedAt": "2024-04-24T11:00:00Z",

            "title": "Higher chocolate prices part of wider trend as climate, other factors disrupt supply",
            "description": "Higher chocolate prices this Easter after bad crops on the other side of the world are just the latest example of disruptions in the food supply chain, a trend experts say consumers are noticing in growing numbers.",
            "content": "Higher chocolate prices this Easter after bad crops on the other side of the world are just the latest example of disruptions in the food supply chain, a trend experts say consumers are noticing in growing numbers.\n\u201cI think people are becoming more interested in where their food comes from,\u201d said Sophia Carodenuto, a professor of geography at the University of Victoria whose research specializes in global food systems.\nThe past few years have seen a number of high-profile disruptions including a spike in lettuce prices due to flooding in California, rising orange juice prices because of bad crops and higher wheat prices linked to the Russia-Ukraine war.\nThese kinds of disruptive events feel like they\u2019re becoming more common, said Graeme Crosbie, senior economist at agriculture lending firm Farm Credit Canada.\nCocoa futures have \u201cgone vertical\u201d this year, especially in the last four or so months, said Crosbie.\nFutures are a way of measuring commodity prices based on contracts for future delivery, a common way to track prices for commodities like wheat, gold and oil.\nA February report by agriculture-focused co-operative bank CoBank said cocoa prices were nearly 65 per cent higher than a year ago, and New York futures prices were at a 46-year high.\nBad weather and disease in West Africa have damaged crop yields, said Crosbie, hurting supply for the product that goes into Halloween, Valentine\u2019s Day and Easter candy.\n\u201cThe confection business is going to bear the brunt of the margin impact due to cocoa,\u201d Hershey chief financial officer Steven Voskuil told analysts on a conference call in February.\nMost cocoa, especially the cocoa found in many popular chocolate products, comes from West Africa, Carodenuto said. C\u00f4te D\u2019Ivoire, which she said produces about 40 per cent of the world\u2019s cocoa, saw a 30 per cent decline in production over the past year due to climate change and disease, she said.\n\u201cThat\u2019s one of the main drivers of ... this huge rise in prices on the commodity markets,\u201d she said.\n\u201cI think we're seeing this all over the world, that the rainy season and the dry season are no longer predictable the way that they had been.\u201d\nUnlike some crops, cocoa production is highly concentrated, meaning huge portions of the world\u2019s supply are grown in a handful of areas, said Crosbie. This makes the crop and its supply chain more vulnerable to disruptions.\nCocoa prices don\u2019t directly translate to retail prices, since there are many things other than cocoa that make up a chocolate bar, Crosbie said. But they do have an effect, and he expects retail prices to increase.\nAccording to Statistics Canadainflation data, the price of confectionary items rose more than nine per cent between January 2023 and 2024, compared with overall inflation for food purchased from stores of 3.4 per cent.\nMichael Medline, the chief executive of Sobeys parent company Empire Co. Ltd., told investors earlier in March the grocer is seeing \u201csizable\u201d price increases from some of its suppliers that will \u201cinevitably affect the customer.\u201d\n\u201cThis is largely driven by some commodities like sugar and cocoa continuing to be very volatile due to ongoing climate and geopolitical factors impacting global supply,\u201d he said.\nHigher cocoa prices are an obstacle for manufacturers who have already been struggling with higher sugar prices over the past three years, said senior food and beverage economist Billy Roberts in the CoBank press release.\n\u201cThat could lead to a further erosion of chocolate volume sales and begin to impact dollar sales as well,\u201d he said.\nConsumers are becoming more aware of these kinds of disruptions as food prices have risen across the board, Crosbie said.\n\u201cI think people are certainly paying more attention to the prices themselves, and even how their food is produced.\u201d\nIn a 2021 survey by Deloitte, almost three-quarters of respondents said it\u2019s important for them to understand where their food comes from.\nAnd cocoa is one of many food products that consumers are eyeing more critically.\nCocoa is indigenous to Central America, said Carodenuto, and naturally grows under a canopy of rainforest trees in a diverse ecosystem. But large swaths of rainforest in West Africa have actually been wiped out to make room for cocoa farming, meaning less ecological diversity and more vulnerable crops.\nHigher commodity futures also don\u2019t necessarily dictate the prices farmers are being paid in real time, noted Carodenuto.\nIn Ghana and C\u00f4te D\u2019Ivoire, the largest-producing countries for cocoa, the government creates a minimum price for farmers for the season, she said. But the large multinational companies buying and trading cocoa enter into forward contracts, meaning prices are agreed upon in advance.\nIt takes a lot of manual labour and investment to build a cocoa farm, so farmers need support, especially financially, Carodenuto said.\nThere\u2019s hope that the higher futures prices will lead to more income next year, but it\u2019s not a guarantee, she added \u2014 cocoa prices are cyclical, meaning there will likely be a price crash at some point.\nConsumers looking to make ethical spending decisions face a difficult choice, said Carodenuto, especially given the price gap between premium and ethically sourced chocolate and popular, mass-market confections.\nCarodenuto said shoppers don\u2019t need to stop buying chocolate, but they should educate themselves and seek supply chain transparency by looking for the origin of the cocoa in a product.\nShoppers who can afford to spend more can also seek out businesses that specialize in sourcing ethical cocoa products, she said.\nIn the long term, there is lots of potential for cocoa to be grown in a more sustainable manner, she said, but it could mean lower production in the short term.\n\u201cIt's just that, who's going to pay for that? ... It shouldn't be the most marginalized actor in the system. It shouldn't be the smallholder farmer who has to pay for that.\u201d\nThis report by The Canadian Press was first published March 25, 2024.\n\u2014 With files from The Associated Press\nRosa Saba, The Canadian Press",
            "url": "https://www.guelphtoday.com/national-business/higher-chocolate-prices-part-of-wider-trend-as-climate-other-factors-disrupt-supply-8503321",

            "title": "Higher chocolate prices part of wider trend as climate, other factors disrupt supply",
            "description": "Higher chocolate prices this Easter after bad crops on the other side of the world are just the latest example of disruptions in the food supply chain, a trend experts say consumers are noticing in growing numbers.",
            "content": "Higher chocolate prices this Easter after bad crops on the other side of the world are just the latest example of disruptions in the food supply chain, a trend experts say consumers are noticing in growing numbers.\nHigher chocolate prices this Easter after bad crops on the other side of the world are just the latest example of disruptions in the food supply chain, a trend experts say consumers are noticing in growing numbers.\n\u201cI think people are becoming more interested in where their food comes from,\u201d said Sophia Carodenuto, a professor of geography at the University of Victoria whose research specializes in global food systems.\nThe past few years have seen a number of high-profile disruptions including a spike in lettuce prices due to flooding in California, rising orange juice prices because of bad crops and higher wheat prices linked to the Russia-Ukraine war.\nThese kinds of disruptive events feel like they\u2019re becoming more common, said Graeme Crosbie, senior economist at agriculture lending firm Farm Credit Canada.\nCocoa futures have \u201cgone vertical\u201d this year, especially in the last four or so months, said Crosbie.\nFutures are a way of measuring commodity prices based on contracts for future delivery, a common way to track prices for commodities like wheat, gold and oil.\nA February report by agriculture-focused co-operative bank CoBank said cocoa prices were nearly 65 per cent higher than a year ago, and New York futures prices were at a 46-year high.\nBad weather and disease in West Africa have damaged crop yields, said Crosbie, hurting supply for the product that goes into Halloween, Valentine\u2019s Day and Easter candy.\n\u201cThe confection business is going to bear the brunt of the margin impact due to cocoa,\u201d Hershey chief financial officer Steven Voskuil told analysts on a conference call in February.\nMost cocoa, especially the cocoa found in many popular chocolate products, comes from West Africa, Carodenuto said. C\u00f4te D\u2019Ivoire, which she said produces about 40 per cent of the world\u2019s cocoa, saw a 30 per cent decline in production over the past year due to climate change and disease, she said.\n\u201cThat\u2019s one of the main drivers of ... this huge rise in prices on the commodity markets,\u201d she said.\n\u201cI think we're seeing this all over the world, that the rainy season and the dry season are no longer predictable the way that they had been.\u201d\nUnlike some crops, cocoa production is highly concentrated, meaning huge portions of the world\u2019s supply are grown in a handful of areas, said Crosbie. This makes the crop and its supply chain more vulnerable to disruptions.\nCocoa prices don\u2019t directly translate to retail prices, since there are many things other than cocoa that make up a chocolate bar, Crosbie said. But they do have an effect, and he expects retail prices to increase.\nAccording to Statistics Canadainflation data, the price of confectionary items rose more than nine per cent between January 2023 and 2024, compared with overall inflation for food purchased from stores of 3.4 per cent.\nMichael Medline, the chief executive of Sobeys parent company Empire Co. Ltd., told investors earlier in March the grocer is seeing \u201csizable\u201d price increases from some of its suppliers that will \u201cinevitably affect the customer.\u201d\n\u201cThis is largely driven by some commodities like sugar and cocoa continuing to be very volatile due to ongoing climate and geopolitical factors impacting global supply,\u201d he said.\nHigher cocoa prices are an obstacle for manufacturers who have already been struggling with higher sugar prices over the past three years, said senior food and beverage economist Billy Roberts in the CoBank press release.\n\u201cThat could lead to a further erosion of chocolate volume sales and begin to impact dollar sales as well,\u201d he said.\nConsumers are becoming more aware of these kinds of disruptions as food prices have risen across the board, Crosbie said.\n\u201cI think people are certainly paying more attention to the prices themselves, and even how their food is produced.\u201d\nIn a 2021 survey by Deloitte, almost three-quarters of respondents said it\u2019s important for them to understand where their food comes from.\nAnd cocoa is one of many food products that consumers are eyeing more critically.\nCocoa is indigenous to Central America, said Carodenuto, and naturally grows under a canopy of rainforest trees in a diverse ecosystem. But large swaths of rainforest in West Africa have actually been wiped out to make room for cocoa farming, meaning less ecological diversity and more vulnerable crops.\nHigher commodity futures also don\u2019t necessarily dictate the prices farmers are being paid in real time, noted Carodenuto.\nIn Ghana and C\u00f4te D\u2019Ivoire, the largest-producing countries for cocoa, the government creates a minimum price for farmers for the season, she said. But the large multinational companies buying and trading cocoa enter into forward contracts, meaning prices are agreed upon in advance.\nIt takes a lot of manual labour and investment to build a cocoa farm, so farmers need support, especially financially, Carodenuto said.\nThere\u2019s hope that the higher futures prices will lead to more income next year, but it\u2019s not a guarantee, she added \u2014 cocoa prices are cyclical, meaning there will likely be a price crash at some point.\nConsumers looking to make ethical spending decisions face a difficult choice, said Carodenuto, especially given the price gap between premium and ethically sourced chocolate and popular, mass-market confections.\nCarodenuto said shoppers don\u2019t need to stop buying chocolate, but they should educate themselves and seek supply chain transparency by looking for the origin of the cocoa in a product.\nShoppers who can afford to spend more can also seek out businesses that specialize in sourcing ethical cocoa products, she said.\nIn the long term, there is lots of potential for cocoa to be grown in a more sustainable manner, she said, but it could mean lower production in the short term.\n\u201cIt's just that, who's going to pay for that? ... It shouldn't be the most marginalized actor in the system. It shouldn't be the smallholder farmer who has to pay for that.\u201d\nThis report by The Canadian Press was first published March 25, 2024.\n\u2014 With files from The Associated Press\nRosa Saba, The Canadian Press",

            "title": "Higher chocolate prices part of wider trend as climate, other factors disrupt supply",
            "description": "Higher chocolate prices this Easter after bad crops on the other side of the world are just the latest example of disruptions in the food supply chain, a trend experts say consumers are noticing in growing numbers.",
            "content": "Higher chocolate prices this Easter after bad crops on the other side of the world are just the latest example of disruptions in the food supply chain, a trend experts say consumers are noticing in growing numbers.\n\u201cI think people are becoming more interested in where their food comes from,\u201d said Sophia Carodenuto, a professor of geography at the University of Victoria whose research specializes in global food systems.\nThe past few years have seen a number of high-profile disruptions including a spike in lettuce prices due to flooding in California, rising orange juice prices because of bad crops and higher wheat prices linked to the Russia-Ukraine war.\nThese kinds of disruptive events feel like they\u2019re becoming more common, said Graeme Crosbie, senior economist at agriculture lending firm Farm Credit Canada.\nCocoa futures have \u201cgone vertical\u201d this year, especially in the last four or so months, said Crosbie.\nFutures are a way of measuring commodity prices based on contracts for future delivery, a common way to track prices for commodities like wheat, gold and oil.\nA February report by agriculture-focused co-operative bank CoBank said cocoa prices were nearly 65 per cent higher than a year ago, and New York futures prices were at a 46-year high.\nBad weather and disease in West Africa have damaged crop yields, said Crosbie, hurting supply for the product that goes into Halloween, Valentine\u2019s Day and Easter candy.\n\u201cThe confection business is going to bear the brunt of the margin impact due to cocoa,\u201d Hershey chief financial officer Steven Voskuil told analysts on a conference call in February.\nMost cocoa, especially the cocoa found in many popular chocolate products, comes from West Africa, Carodenuto said. C\u00f4te D\u2019Ivoire, which she said produces about 40 per cent of the world\u2019s cocoa, saw a 30 per cent decline in production over the past year due to climate change and disease, she said.\n\u201cThat\u2019s one of the main drivers of ... this huge rise in prices on the commodity markets,\u201d she said.\n\u201cI think we're seeing this all over the world, that the rainy season and the dry season are no longer predictable the way that they had been.\u201d\nUnlike some crops, cocoa production is highly concentrated, meaning huge portions of the world\u2019s supply are grown in a handful of areas, said Crosbie. This makes the crop and its supply chain more vulnerable to disruptions.\nCocoa prices don\u2019t directly translate to retail prices, since there are many things other than cocoa that make up a chocolate bar, Crosbie said. But they do have an effect, and he expects retail prices to increase.\nAccording to Statistics Canadainflation data, the price of confectionary items rose more than nine per cent between January 2023 and 2024, compared with overall inflation for food purchased from stores of 3.4 per cent.\nMichael Medline, the chief executive of Sobeys parent company Empire Co. Ltd., told investors earlier in March the grocer is seeing \u201csizable\u201d price increases from some of its suppliers that will \u201cinevitably affect the customer.\u201d\n\u201cThis is largely driven by some commodities like sugar and cocoa continuing to be very volatile due to ongoing climate and geopolitical factors impacting global supply,\u201d he said.\nHigher cocoa prices are an obstacle for manufacturers who have already been struggling with higher sugar prices over the past three years, said senior food and beverage economist Billy Roberts in the CoBank press release.\n\u201cThat could lead to a further erosion of chocolate volume sales and begin to impact dollar sales as well,\u201d he said.\nConsumers are becoming more aware of these kinds of disruptions as food prices have risen across the board, Crosbie said.\n\u201cI think people are certainly paying more attention to the prices themselves, and even how their food is produced.\u201d\nIn a 2021 survey by Deloitte, almost three-quarters of respondents said it\u2019s important for them to understand where their food comes from.\nAnd cocoa is one of many food products that consumers are eyeing more critically.\nCocoa is indigenous to Central America, said Carodenuto, and naturally grows under a canopy of rainforest trees in a diverse ecosystem. But large swaths of rainforest in West Africa have actually been wiped out to make room for cocoa farming, meaning less ecological diversity and more vulnerable crops.\nHigher commodity futures also don\u2019t necessarily dictate the prices farmers are being paid in real time, noted Carodenuto.\nIn Ghana and C\u00f4te D\u2019Ivoire, the largest-producing countries for cocoa, the government creates a minimum price for farmers for the season, she said. But the large multinational companies buying and trading cocoa enter into forward contracts, meaning prices are agreed upon in advance.\nIt takes a lot of manual labour and investment to build a cocoa farm, so farmers need support, especially financially, Carodenuto said.\nThere\u2019s hope that the higher futures prices will lead to more income next year, but it\u2019s not a guarantee, she added \u2014 cocoa prices are cyclical, meaning there will likely be a price crash at some point.\nConsumers looking to make ethical spending decisions face a difficult choice, said Carodenuto, especially given the price gap between premium and ethically sourced chocolate and popular, mass-market confections.\nCarodenuto said shoppers don\u2019t need to stop buying chocolate, but they should educate themselves and seek supply chain transparency by looking for the origin of the cocoa in a product.\nShoppers who can afford to spend more can also seek out businesses that specialize in sourcing ethical cocoa products, she said.\nIn the long term, there is lots of potential for cocoa to be grown in a more sustainable manner, she said, but it could mean lower production in the short term.\n\u201cIt's just that, who's going to pay for that? ... It shouldn't be the most marginalized actor in the system. It shouldn't be the smallholder farmer who has to pay for that.\u201d\nThis report by The Canadian Press was first published March 25, 2024.\n\u2014 With files from The Associated Press\nRosa Saba, The Canadian Press",

            "title": "From salad trees to 'charcuterwreaths,' has the festive food trend gone overboard?",
            "description": "Wedge salad Christmas trees and 'charcuterwreaths' are trending this year. Whether or not they're practical may not matter.",
            "content": "A food influencer arranges a cheese star at the top of a Christmas tree-shaped display of baby romaine lettuce wedges.\nIn the background, Mariah Carey rallies all holiday-lovers with her annual call to action, belting \"It's time!\" as her Yuletide anthem All I Want for Christmas Is You starts playing. The influencer, with a twinkle in her eye, squirts blue cheese salad dressing out of a squeeze bottle in a criss-cross pattern to look like a garland.\nThe caption reads, \"Everyone needs a good salad this Holiday Season! So why not make it festive?\"\nThe 5.4 million views for this one video on Instagram alone speaks to the appeal of holiday food boards. Hummus wreaths, charcuterie Santas and upright snack-based Christmas trees are being posted and shared across Instagram, Pinterest and TikTok. On TikTok, videos about \"Christmas charcuterie boards\" have racked up 20.9 million views so far.\nSearch results for 'Christmas charcuterie board' on TikTok, where videos have racked up 20.9 million views so far. (TikTok)\nThe Daily Mail says \"buttercream boards\" are this year's trendy festive offering, Marie Claire just proclaimed that \"Charcuterwreaths\" are the latest it food and U.K. magazine The Grocer has declared \"tasty sharing boards\" as one of food retailers' top Christmas trends this year. There are even charcuterie chalets, or meat mansions, because why stop with a gingerbread house?\nIf you can't choose just one, well, you're in luck, because there are also 122.9 million views on trending TikTok videos about Christmas board parties, or \"bring a board night,\" where every guest brings a different-themed board of their own.\nShareable snacks arranged to look like Christmas themes aren't necessarily new (think of the gingerbread house), and the infamous butter board started to trend last year. But the craze this year could be related to high food prices, says Sylvain Charlebois, director of the Agri-Food Analytics Lab at Dalhousie University in Halifax.\nBoards can be shared, which can make them more economical than a meal, he said. For the same reason, Charlebois sees potlucks being more popular this year, too.\n\"Hosting is very expensive,\" he said. \"People are always looking for affordable and imaginative ideas when hosting friends and family.\"\nHave people gone over-board?\nSome of the comments on these videos tell a less enthusiastic story.\nSome point out that it seems difficult to keep the food warm (think of the eggs on a brunch board), that dipping cookies into a shared smear of buttercream seems like a quick and easy way to spread germs and that some boards \u2014 especially the large charcuterie boards loaded with specialty cheeses and meats \u2014 are actually quite expensive to make.\n\"This is cute but not practical. Difficult to eat,\" wrote one viewer on the Instagram salad tree. \"Does everything need to be a damn show,\" wrote another.\n\"Have we taken the board trend too far?\" one woman asked in a reaction video about buttercream boards. \"The thought of cleaning up a board smeared in greasy butter or sticky frosting kind of makes my eye twitch.\"\n\"What bothers me most of all, I think, is that it seems all for show,\" a writer quipped about butter boards last year in Food and Wine magazine. \"For the love of all that is holy, please just use a plate, napkin, or your hands, without reaching for your phone to capture the moment.\"\nSome of the boards and displays are more about show than practicality, Charlebois said, but that doesn't mean the trend is going away anytime soon. For a younger generation, TikTok is the new cookbook, he explained, and lots of people draw their ideas and inspiration from social media.\n\"Things can go overboard at times, no pun intended,\" Charlebois said.\nStores and food manufacturers are looking to take advantage of that potential audience, where individual wedge salad Christmas trees garner 5.4 million views. It's all about being visual, he added.\n\"The whole idea is really to get on people's phones and screens as much as possible.\"",

Plantaform harnessed an approach first used by NASA, to create an indoor gardening system that uses \u201cfogponics,\u201d a subset of aeroponics that uses ultrasonic atomizers to break down the water into a fine mist, using 98 per cent less water than conventional systems, CEO Alberto Aguilar told The Globe. Researched and developed in collaboration with the National Research Council of Canada, the egg-shaped Rejuvenate can grow up to 15 plants with refills of water and nutrients every two to three weeks.             "content": "Credited with bringing the Indian food trend to the Sault years ago, Neeta Marwah has opened the doors to Noodlebox \u2013 the city\u2019s latest takeout chain that specializes in Southeast Asian street food\nLong-time Indian restauranteur Neeta Marwah celebrated the grand opening of Noodlebox in The Shoppes on TransCanada with friends, family, and dignitaries on July 24, 2023\n1 / 1 Long-time Indian restauranteur Neeta Marwah celebrated the grand opening of Noodlebox in The Shoppes on TransCanada with friends, family, and dignitaries on July 24, 2023\nNeeta Marwah has had a lot of ups and downs throughout her culinary career, but it\u2019s safe to say she\u2019s riding a high this summer.\nWidely regarded as a trailblazer for bringing South Asian cuisine to the area, the former owner of Rasoi has started a new business venture with a Noodlebox location at The Shoppes on TransCanada.\nMarwah and her team at Noodlebox opened their doors last Thursday.\nSince then, the service has been non-stop.\n\u201cWe keep selling out of stuff \u2013 the lineups have been constant,\u201d she says. \u201cWe\u2019ve actually had to put out a \u2018sold out\u2019 board every day by 6:15 because we just haven\u2019t been able to keep up. We\u2019re amazed at how the community has accepted it.\u201d\nBorn near New Delhi, Marwah originally began making and selling homecooked dishes out of her east-end home back in 2009 \u2013 a time when there were practically no Indian cuisine options in town.\nShe later opened up Rasoi, a take-out storefront in what\u2019s now Antico Ristorant\u00e9. Marwah eventually relocated the business to a dine-in establishment in the downtown core.\nBut in 2020, the pandemic changed everything for the restaurateur.\n\u201cI had to close the kitchen because of COVID,\u201d she says. \u201cAfter we shut down, I was just catering to friends and family, weddings, and baby showers. But eventually I was ready for something new.\u201d\nAfter doing some research and collaborating with friends and business partners Maulik Jariwala and Nilay Patel, the trio headed to Vancouver in 2022 to scope out the Noodlebox chain, which originally started in British Columbia more than 20 years ago.\nBefore she knew it, Marwah became a franchisee.\n\u201cWe actually opened up our first location in Barrie this past May, so the Sault is our second Noodlebox now,\u201d she says. \u201cBy spring of 2024, we want to be in a third location. We\u2019re looking into other northern Ontario towns like Sudbury, Timmins, North Bay, and Thunder Bay.\u201d\nJust less than a week after opening the establishment that specializes in Southeast Asian street food, Marwah has been blown away by the community\u2019s response.\n\u201cWhen I initially started at home, I had backorders then too,\u201d she says. \u201cBut this time, it was quite surprising for me. We\u2019ve had so many cultures who have recently moved in after COVID, and everyone in Sault Ste. Marie has been so open to the different cuisines. It makes me really happy.\u201d\n\u201cWe haven\u2019t even had third-party deliveries like UberEats yet. With the constant crowd we\u2019re getting we\u2019ll probably have to delay it for a couple weeks.\u201d\nMarwah was joined by friends, family, and business colleagues at the restaurant on Monday for an official ribbon cutting ceremony. Mayor Matthew Shoemaker and MP Terry Sheehan even stopped by to help celebrate the occasion.\n\u201cAt the age of 90, my grandma flew alone from India to be here for the opening,\u201d she says. \u201cWe were so blessed to have her here. My husband Rajat and my kids Ahaan and Arsh have been so supportive. I was going through a rough time after I closed down Rasoi. If those three hadn\u2019t pushed me, I honestly wouldn\u2019t have been doing this.\u201d\nWhile she\u2019s taken a step back from her own cooking to focus on Noodlebox, Marwah says the early success of the new business has given her lots of time to be thankful.\n\u201cI\u2019ve built so many relationships in this town \u2013 it\u2019s like a family to me,\u201d she says. \u201cThe customers who I\u2019ve known for so many years, and the many friends I\u2019ve made from this work, is very precious to me.\u201d",

            "title": "Report shows more Ontarians relying on food banks and visiting them more frequently",
            "description": "Ontario residents have been visiting food banks in greater numbers and more often for six years running, a coalition of hunger relief organizations said Monday, noting the troubling trend appeared to escalate during the most recent year on record.",
            "content": "Ontario residents have been visiting food banks in greater numbers and more often for six years running, a coalition of hunger relief organizations said Monday, noting the troubling trend appeared to escalate during the most recent year on record.\nThe findings are laid out in a new report from Feed Ontario, a collective of 1,200 direct and affiliate food banks and other organizations that work to address food insecurity.\nThe annual Hunger Report, subtitled \"The Deepening Cracks in Ontario's Economic Foundation,\" found 587,000 adults and children visited the province's food banks a total of 4.3 million times betweenApril 1, 2021 and March 31, 2022. Feed Ontario said that represents a 15 per cent spike in the number of people turning to food banks for aid and a 42 per cent surge in the number of visits compared to numbers recorded in 2019.\nThe report said the findings, which mark the sixth straight year of increasing food bank users and visits, also illustrate the strain the system is facing.\n\"It is the largest amount of people accessing our services on record since we've started collecting data and writing these reports,\" Feed Ontario Executive Director Carolyn Stewart said in an interview.\n\"The pressures that ... low-income Ontarians and marginalized groups are feeling with the unaffordability of today is exceptionally concerning. The fact that so many people are now having to rely on emergency food support to get by should be worrying all of us, not just Feed Ontario.\"\nThe organization is calling on the provincial government to tackle a rise in low-quality work, invest in government-assisted housing, improve social assistance and centre people with lived experiences in the design of public policy and programs.\n\"There's concern in the food bank networks that donations will not be sufficient to meet the need, that we won't have enough food resources for this continuing growing need of people,\" Stewart said.\nThe report found one in every three visitors was a first-time food bank user.\nIt attributed the spike to long-standing issues such as precarious employment, inaccessible unemployment aid and inadequate supports for those with disabilities, as well as more recent factors like rising inflation and the increased cost of living.\n\"Social assistance rates are continuing to fall far below low-income measures,\" said Stewart. \"That, being coupled with unaffordable housing, is really making it impossible to afford the most basic necessities for many individuals.\"\nThose who visited food banks in the past two years cited food and housing costs, low wages or insufficient work hours as the driving factors behind their visits.\n\"Imagine you already had a budget that was stretched thin. You're already making impossible choices between keeping a roof over your head or keeping your lights on, buying winter clothes for your child or paying for more medication you might need,\" said Stewart, noting those who turn to food banks are facing such dilemmas every day.\nShe said 30 per cent of food bank clients are children and youth under the age of 18, a number that has remained consistent in recent years. More than 50 per cent of those accessing such centres are on some form of social assistance, she added.\nStewart said food banks are now being asked to serve a role they were never designed to fulfil.\n\"We were developed as a stopgap measure in the '80s and we were never intended to be a social safety net,\" said Stewart.\n\"We were supposed to be turned to in terms of emergencies. But now as more and more Ontarians are unable to afford their most basic necessities, at what point is it enough?\"\nThis report by The Canadian Press was first published Nov. 28, 2022.",

            "title": "More Ontarians relying on food banks and visiting them more frequently: Feed Ontario",

            "content": "TORONTO \u2014 Ontario residents have been visiting food banks in greater numbers and more often for six years running, a coalition of hunger relief organizations said Monday, noting the troubling trend appeared to escalate during the most recent year on record.\nThe findings are laid out in a new report from Feed Ontario, a collective of 1,200 direct and affiliate food banks and other organizations that work to address food insecurity.\nThe annual Hunger Report, subtitled \"The Deepening Cracks in Ontario\u2019s Economic Foundation,\" found 587,000 adults and children visited the province's food banks a total of 4.3 million times betweenApril 1, 2021 and March 31, 2022. Feed Ontario said that represents a 15 per cent spike in the number of people turning to food banks for aid and a 42 per cent surge in the number of visits compared to numbers recorded in 2019.\nThe report said the findings, which mark the sixth straight year of increasing food bank users and visits, also illustrate the strain the system is facing.\n\"It is the largest amount of people accessing our services on record since we've started collecting data and writing these reports,\" Feed Ontario Executive Director Carolyn Stewart said in an interview.\n\"The pressures that ... low-income Ontarians and marginalized groups are feeling with the unaffordability of today is exceptionally concerning. The fact that so many people are now having to rely on emergency food support to get by should be worrying all of us, not just Feed Ontario.\"\nThe organization is calling on the provincial government to tackle a rise in low-quality work, invest in government-assisted housing, improve social assistance and centre people with lived experiences in the design of public policy and programs.\n\"There's concern in the food bank networks that donations will not be sufficient to meet the need, that we won't have enough food resources for this continuing growing need of people,\" Stewart said.\nThe report found one in every three visitors was a first-time food bank user.\nIt attributed the spike to long-standing issues such as precarious employment, inaccessible unemployment aid and inadequate supports for those with disabilities, as well as more recent factors like rising inflation and the increased cost of living.\n\"Social assistance rates are continuing to fall far below low-income measures,\" said Stewart. \"That, being coupled with unaffordable housing, is really making it impossible to afford the most basic necessities for many individuals.\"\nThose who visited food banks in the past two years cited food and housing costs, low wages or insufficient work hours as the driving factors behind their visits.\n\"Imagine you already had a budget that was stretched thin. You're already making impossible choices between keeping a roof over your head or keeping your lights on, buying winter clothes for your child or paying for more medication you might need,\" said Stewart, noting those who turn to food banks are facing such dilemmas every day.\nShe said 30 per cent of food bank clients are children and youth under the age of 18, a number that has remained consistent in recent years. More than 50 per cent of those accessing such centres are on some form of social assistance, she added.\nStewart said food banks are now being asked to serve a role they were never designed to fulfil.\n\"We were developed as a stopgap measure in the '80s and we were never intended to be a social safety net,\" said Stewart.\n\"We were supposed to be turned to in terms of emergencies. But now as more and more Ontarians are unable to afford their most basic necessities, at what point is it enough?\"\nThis report by The Canadian Press was first published Nov. 28, 2022.\nJessica Smith, The Canadian Press",

            "description": "TORONTO \u2014 Ontario residents have been visiting food banks in greater numbers and more often for six years running, a coalition of hunger relief organizations said Monday, noting the troubling trend appeared to escalate during the most recent year on",
            "content": "TORONTO \u2014 Ontario residents have been visiting food banks in greater numbers and more often for six years running, a coalition of hunger relief organizations said Monday, noting the troubling trend appeared to escalate during the most recent year on record.\nThe findings are laid out in a new report from Feed Ontario, a collective of 1,200 direct and affiliate food banks and other organizations that work to address food insecurity.\nThe annual Hunger Report, subtitled \"The Deepening Cracks in Ontario\u2019s Economic Foundation,\" found 587,000 adults and children visited the province's food banks a total of 4.3 million times betweenApril 1, 2021 and March 31, 2022. Feed Ontario said that represents a 15 per cent spike in the number of people turning to food banks for aid and a 42 per cent surge in the number of visits compared to numbers recorded in 2019.\nThe report said the findings, which mark the sixth straight year of increasing food bank users and visits, also illustrate the strain the system is facing.\n\"It is the largest amount of people accessing our services on record since we've started collecting data and writing these reports,\" Feed Ontario Executive Director Carolyn Stewart said in an interview.\n\"The pressures that ... low-income Ontarians and marginalized groups are feeling with the unaffordability of today is exceptionally concerning. The fact that so many people are now having to rely on emergency food support to get by should be worrying all of us, not just Feed Ontario.\"\nThe organization is calling on the provincial government to tackle a rise in low-quality work, invest in government-assisted housing, improve social assistance and centre people with lived experiences in the design of public policy and programs.\n\"There's concern in the food bank networks that donations will not be sufficient to meet the need, that we won't have enough food resources for this continuing growing need of people,\" Stewart said.\nThe report found one in every three visitors was a first-time food bank user.\nIt attributed the spike to long-standing issues such as precarious employment, inaccessible unemployment aid and inadequate supports for those with disabilities, as well as more recent factors like rising inflation and the increased cost of living.\n\"Social assistance rates are continuing to fall far below low-income measures,\" said Stewart. \"That, being coupled with unaffordable housing, is really making it impossible to afford the most basic necessities for many individuals.\"\nThose who visited food banks in the past two years cited food and housing costs, low wages or insufficient work hours as the driving factors behind their visits.\n\"Imagine you already had a budget that was stretched thin. You're already making impossible choices between keeping a roof over your head or keeping your lights on, buying winter clothes for your child or paying for more medication you might need,\" said Stewart, noting those who turn to food banks are facing such dilemmas every day.\nShe said 30 per cent of food bank clients are children and youth under the age of 18, a number that has remained consistent in recent years. More than 50 per cent of those accessing such centres are on some form of social assistance, she added.\nStewart said food banks are now being asked to serve a role they were never designed to fulfil.\n\"We were developed as a stopgap measure in the '80s and we were never intended to be a social safety net,\" said Stewart.\n\"We were supposed to be turned to in terms of emergencies. But now as more and more Ontarians are unable to afford their most basic necessities, at what point is it enough?\"\nThis report by The Canadian Press was first published Nov. 28, 2022.\nJessica Smith, The Canadian Press",

            "description": "TORONTO \u2014 Ontario residents have been visiting food banks in greater numbers and more often for six years running, a coalition of hunger relief organizations said Monday, noting the troubling trend appeared to escalate during the most recent year on",
            "content": "TORONTO \u2014 Ontario residents have been visiting food banks in greater numbers and more often for six years running, a coalition of hunger relief organizations said Monday, noting the troubling trend appeared to escalate during the most recent year on record.\nThe findings are laid out in a new report from Feed Ontario, a collective of 1,200 direct and affiliate food banks and other organizations that work to address food insecurity.\nThe annual Hunger Report, subtitled \"The Deepening Cracks in Ontario\u2019s Economic Foundation,\" found 587,000 adults and children visited the province's food banks a total of 4.3 million times betweenApril 1, 2021 and March 31, 2022. Feed Ontario said that represents a 15 per cent spike in the number of people turning to food banks for aid and a 42 per cent surge in the number of visits compared to numbers recorded in 2019.\nThe report said the findings, which mark the sixth straight year of increasing food bank users and visits, also illustrate the strain the system is facing.\n\"It is the largest amount of people accessing our services on record since we've started collecting data and writing these reports,\" Feed Ontario Executive Director Carolyn Stewart said in an interview.\n\"The pressures that ... low-income Ontarians and marginalized groups are feeling with the unaffordability of today is exceptionally concerning. The fact that so many people are now having to rely on emergency food support to get by should be worrying all of us, not just Feed Ontario.\"\nThe organization is calling on the provincial government to tackle a rise in low-quality work, invest in government-assisted housing, improve social assistance and centre people with lived experiences in the design of public policy and programs.\n\"There's concern in the food bank networks that donations will not be sufficient to meet the need, that we won't have enough food resources for this continuing growing need of people,\" Stewart said.\nThe report found one in every three visitors was a first-time food bank user.\nIt attributed the spike to long-standing issues such as precarious employment, inaccessible unemployment aid and inadequate supports for those with disabilities, as well as more recent factors like rising inflation and the increased cost of living.\n\"Social assistance rates are continuing to fall far below low-income measures,\" said Stewart. \"That, being coupled with unaffordable housing, is really making it impossible to afford the most basic necessities for many individuals.\"\nThose who visited food banks in the past two years cited food and housing costs, low wages or insufficient work hours as the driving factors behind their visits.\n\"Imagine you already had a budget that was stretched thin. You're already making impossible choices between keeping a roof over your head or keeping your lights on, buying winter clothes for your child or paying for more medication you might need,\" said Stewart, noting those who turn to food banks are facing such dilemmas every day.\nShe said 30 per cent of food bank clients are children and youth under the age of 18, a number that has remained consistent in recent years. More than 50 per cent of those accessing such centres are on some form of social assistance, she added.\nStewart said food banks are now being asked to serve a role they were never designed to fulfil.\n\"We were developed as a stopgap measure in the '80s and we were never intended to be a social safety net,\" said Stewart.\n\"We were supposed to be turned to in terms of emergencies. But now as more and more Ontarians are unable to afford their most basic necessities, at what point is it enough?\"\nThis report by The Canadian Press was first published Nov. 28, 2022.\nJessica Smith, The Canadian Press",

            "title": "In soup vs. art, climate change protesters lose, says activist",
            "description": "Hurling soup or some sort of damaging food at famous paintings has a become a popular trend among climate activists hoping for a brighter future, but Lucy Whelan says those demonstrations aren\u2019t actually having the impact those activists would hope.",
            "content": "As It Happens 7:05 Attacking paintings with soup isn\u2019t an effective way to fight climate change, says activist\nHurling soup or some sort of damaging food at famous paintings has a become a popular trend among climate activists hoping for a brighter future, but Lucy Whelan says those demonstrations aren't actually having the impact those activists would hope.\n\"It's a kind of spectacle and nothing has really been harmed,\" Whelan, a climate activist and art historian herself, told Nil K\u00f6ksal on As It Happens. \"The problem with climate change right now is that so many people know it's scary, but we're quite complacent.\"\nIn October, protesters Phoebe Plummer and Anna Holland made headlines by throwing soup on Vincent van Gogh's Sunflowers in London's National Gallery. But the soup caused no discernible damage to the glass-covered painting.\nIn an interview with As It Happens in October, Plummer said that the action she and Holland took was necessary in the shadow of the climate crises.\n\"Right now, there's 33 million people displaced by floods in Pakistan. Thousands of people are dying of starvation in Somalia due to extreme famine from crop failure,\" said Plummer.\nThis photo from Italian news agency Ansa, shows climate activists from Last Generation and a security guard by The Sower, an 1888 painting by Dutch artist Vincent Van Gogh, after activists threw pea soup at it on Nov. 4. (ANSA/AFP/Getty Images)\n\"And it took two young people throwing soup at a painting to get more people talking about the climate crisis, [more than] the millions of lives have been destroyed by climate disasters.\"\nWhen Whelan first saw what Plummer and Holland had done, she was shocked. But she says, while she wholeheartedly agrees with the intention behind the protest, she feels it's a lose-lose scenario.\n\"Either the soup or the acidic liquid that's chosen is actually going to damage the artworks, in which case, I think that's the tragedy. It makes the protesters look like hooligans who are not interested in preserving things for the future, which they obviously are,\" said Whelan.\n\"Or it's not going to damage the work \u2026 then what we have is something that looks shocking, but actually it just makes us all go, 'Oh, it's fine,' \u2026[which] is exactly the kind of thinking that we need to stop being quite so good at.\"\nMaking a difference\nOther famous pieces of art have been hit as well. In late October, mashed potatoes were chucked Claude Monet's painting Les Meules, and someone smashed chocolate cake on a waxwork figure of King Charles.\nIn Canada, protesters with Stop Fracking Around, a group demanding an end to the Coastal GasLink Pipeline project, poured maple syrup onto Emily Carr's 1934 painting Stumps and Sky at the Vancouver Art Gallery on November 12.\n\"Her painting Stumps and Sky \u2026 is a lament of ecological destruction and her art actually paid attention to Indigenous peoples. She cared about all that binds us to the world. She seemed just the wrong person,\" said Whelan.\nProtesters said they chose the painting because the fallen trees depicted the kind of environmental destruction they are trying to prevent. But Whelan says that instead, protesters should be more thoughtful about the pieces they target.\nThis photo provided to media from the group Stop Fracking Around shows Erin Fletcher, left, and Emily Kelsall, right, in front of the Emily Carr painting 'Stumps and Sky,' at the Vancouver Art Gallery on Saturday, Nov. 12. (Stop Fracking Around)\n\"At least think about making some seriously disruptive attacks on art that celebrates consumption and economic growth,\" said Whelan.\n\"I think we need to be more thoughtful, not more art historical, about about what is the target of activism.\"\nWhelan says she is on the protesters' side. As a climate activist herself, she's tried to make meaningful changes and taken part in protests.\nShe's spoken to her local politicians in Cambridge, England, and talked with neighbours about how they can do things differently to protect the environment.\n\"There will be so many people watching attacks like this saying, 'oh, I support action on climate change. I really do. But just not this not attacks on art.' And I would encourage anyone who says that to make that real, to translate that into action,\" said Whelan.\n\"If you do support action on climate change, [and] you don't want to see it like this, contact your counselors, do something you do approve of. Walk the talk.\"\nProduced by Kate Swoger.",

Almost a quarter said they were switching to supermarket own-brands, while more than a third were \"shopping around more for offers.\"\nThe German discount chains Aldi and Lidl are attracting more shoppers in the UK, with Aldi overtaking Morrisons last month to become Britain's fourth-biggest supermarket chain by sales.\nInsider contacted Waitrose for comment.\nRead the original article on Business Insider",

            "title": "Chinese social media users are not impressed with your ham sandwich",
            "description": "A packed ham sandwich or a leafy salad might be your ordinary weekday lunch, but it is turning into a hot trend called \u201cwhite people food\u201d in China.",
            "content": "BEIJING \u2014 The plain ham sandwiches, simple salads and other American-style packed lunches that fill office refrigerators don\u2019t exactly scream \u201cInstagram.\u201d But they\u2019re a social media sensation in China, where they have their own hashtag: #WhitePeopleFood.\nThe trend appears to have started in May, when a Chinese social media user posted a video that quickly went viral of a fellow train passenger in Switzerland pulling lettuce leaves from a bag and layering them with ham and mustard.\n\u201cIs there anything more \u2018white people food\u2019 than this?\u201d the caption read.\nThe term pokes fun at the casual, on-the-go way people eat in the United States, Europe and elsewhere in the West. Compared with the warm, flavorful and elaborately prepared food people from China are used to, meals made with a handful of cold and unseasoned ingredients can seem underwhelming or just plain inedible.\nDiscussions of \u201cwhite people food\u201d on Chinese social media are filled with witty and withering descriptions: \u201cFood of suffering.\u201d \u201cA meal to maintain vital signs.\u201d \u201cWhat death must feel like.\u201d\nBut as a cuisine it does have its advantages, as stressed-out young workers are discovering in cities across China.\n\u201cI had been having sandwiches for lunch for more than half a year,\u201d said Gao Duan, a 30-year-old playwright in the northern port city of Tianjin. \u201cIt doesn\u2019t have a strong smell and it\u2019s convenient to eat in the office.\u201d\nEventually Gao had enough of the daily sandwiches, which she said she ate \u201cjust to get through the afternoon.\u201d\n\u201cThere is no point eating good food in the workplace, because food should be divine and enjoyed properly,\u201d she said.\nOn Xiaohongshu, China\u2019s equivalent of Instagram, users are rushing to post their own versions of \u201cwhite people food,\u201d a topic with more than 13 million views that is reminiscent of the \u201csad desk lunch\u201d meme from the 2010s.\nOne user told her followers that she was enjoying her bagel sandwich stuffed with ham, cheese and rocket leaves. Another showed a boxed lunch featuring cucumber and tomato, as well as ready-made boiled eggs and potatoes, captioned \u201cno heating, no cutting, no seasoning, done in two minutes.\u201d\nThough \u201cwhite people food\u201d is far from a novelty in China, which has had Western fast-food chains since the 1980s, the social media trend is driven by changes in how people are living, as well as the eye-catching hashtag.\n\u201cThe name looks a bit politically incorrect,\u201d said Cao Yu, who studies food culture at Jinan University in the southern metropolis of Guangzhou. \u201cBut it\u2019s not meant to be offensive.\u201d\n\u201cPeople simply think it doesn\u2019t look very tasty,\u201d Cao added.\nA typical Chinese lunch includes staple foods such as rice or noodles and a few warm dishes made with multiple ingredients, spices and cooking techniques from frying to stewing to braising. From start to finish, preparation can easily take 30 minutes to an hour.\nThat was easier when most workers, many of them working for state-owned companies with their own canteens, could depend on extended lunch breaks long enough for a hearty meal and an early-afternoon nap.\nBut China\u2019s modern \u201c996\u201d work culture \u2014 working from 9 a.m. to 9 p.m., six days a week \u2014 leaves little time to make or eat a complicated meal.\n\u201cYoung people want to save more time for themselves,\u201d Cao said. \u201cWhen you realize you can just eat a carrot raw, a door to a new world opens.\u201d\nSimplicity isn\u2019t the only reason \u201cwhite people food\u201d has come to look more appealing.\nLi Qiaoying, a 26-year-old marketing consultant based in Shenzhen who enjoys yoga, Pilates and dance cardio, said it aligns with her idea of a healthy diet and helps her keep fit.\n\u201cIt is lighter, unlike the deep-fried or stewed Chinese dishes,\u201d she said. \u201cFood that\u2019s too oily or strong is a burden to the body.\u201d\nIt\u2019s also considered cheaper by urban workers facing high living expenses and record youth unemployment. Making a Western-style boxed lunch costs less than 20 yuan ($2.80), Li said, compared with 30 yuan ($4.20) for takeout and 100 yuan ($13.80) to eat at a restaurant. In a country where the average worker makes less than 9,000 yuan ($1,240) a month, the difference adds up quickly.\nOther young professionals, considering the effort that would be required to make a proper Chinese meal, are so exhausted they just can\u2019t be bothered \u2014 a reflection of a growing movement to minimize work, social activities and other exertion that is known in China as \u201clying flat.\u201d\n\u201cFood is an important part of Chinese culture,\u201d said Miao Jia, assistant professor of sociology at NYU Shanghai. \u201cBut many young people will sacrifice taste since they only eat to survive.\u201d\n\u201cWhite people food\u201d still has many detractors in a country with a long and rich culinary tradition.\n\u201cIt\u2019s absurd for an adult to eat this for daily nutrition,\u201d said Xianglan Huo, a college student in the southern city of Kunming. \u201cThe portion is not even enough for my cat.\u201d\n\u201cIt\u2019s too cruel to the body,\u201d Huo said. \u201cI prefer rich cuisines.\u201d\nBut it also has its defenders \u2014 even when it comes to taste.\nAlice Dong, a 34-year-old office worker in Beijing, said she loves her salad lunches because they resemble some of the cold dishes she ate growing up in northeast China.\n\u201cMy mom used to mix radish, cucumber and shredded potato with salt, vinegar and chili sauce,\u201d she said. \u201cTo me, Western salad is just a change of a few ingredients and sauce.\u201d",

            "content": "The most recent data from Nielsen and Numerator showed that packaged food sales are in an accelerating trend as pricing continues to help offset volume weakness.\nUBS analyst Cody Ross noted Kraft Heinz (KHC), Campbell Soup (CPB), Mondelez International (MDLZ), General Mills (NYSE:GIS), TreeHouse Food (THS), and Hostess Brands (TWNK) all saw accelerating sales growth on a 2-yr CAGR basis as well.\nLooking ahead, the outlook is not so positive for the food stocks. Ross thinks the biggest question facing the sector is whether there will be a soft landing to lower organic sales growth or a hard landing with a period of flat to negative growth.\n\"A soft landing likely requires companies to step up investments in the form of innovation and marketing to drive volume growth as price gains wane. A hard landing will likely be the result of accelerating elasticity (to more normalized levels) coupled with private label share gains.\"\nRoss thinks the strong May Nielsen and Numerator data tilts the scale to a soft landing, although the issue is not seen being settled until, but acknowledges this debate will likely take many ups and downs before it is ultimately late 2023 into 2024 when the price contribution to sales begins to roll off significantly. For now, UBS recommends stocks with the highest quality portfolios and the greatest visibility to earnings beats.             "title": "Feeling \u2018swicy\u2019: Why sweet and spicy flavors are still on a hot streak",
            "description": "More than two years into a hot streak, the sweet and spicy food trend shows no sign of stopping.",
            "content": "More than two years into a hot streak, the sweet and spicy food trend shows no sign of stopping. The flavor mashup even has a cheeky portmanteau: swicy.\nAsian and Mexican cuisines have long teamed sweet and spicy flavors with delicious results. Now the complex flavor duo has hit the American mainstream, spreading from chef-driven restaurants to the snack aisle at supermarkets.\nSome sweet and spicy dishes at Dallas restaurants can be easily improvised in your kitchen. The \u201cHoney Bastard\u201d pizza at Thunderbird Pies teams spicy soppressata with sweet bacon marmalade and fiery, habanero-infused honey. But home cooks can drizzle store-bought or homemade hot honey over pepperoni, prosciutto or bacon-topped pizza \u2014 whether homemade, frozen or delivered.\nRoots Southern Kitchen\u2019s addictive pimento cheese toast is topped with house-made pepper jelly and chow chow; store-bought versions of these toppings work nicely, too.\nEat Drink D-FW The latest food and drink reviews, recipes and info on the D-FW food scene. Email Address By signing up you agree to our Terms of Service and Privacy Policy SUBMIT\nYou can also replicate some restaurant charcuterie and cheese boards by including a spicy jam, mostarda or honey to complement the savory bites. And popular brand Mike\u2019s Hot Honey is drizzled on everything from pies at local Hustle Town Pizza pop-ups to the \u201cfried charcuterie board\u201d at the State Fair of Texas.\nAll around Dallas, you\u2019ll find mass market evidence of swicy\u2019s sway: a profusion of Nashville and Korean hot chicken chain outposts; sweet and spicy nuts, Doritos and even breakfast cereal (Cinna-Fuego Toast Crunch anyone?) at grocery stores; and at specialty markets, bottled hot honey for enlivening roasted veggies, chicken and biscuits, and more.\nRead on for recipes buoyed by sweet and spicy flavors, plus a simple, customizable hot honey recipe \u2014 and 10 ways to use it.\nTina Danze is a Dallas freelance writer. Follow her on Instagram at @tinawineandfood.\nPeaches with Burrata and Hot Honey can be smeared on slices of toasted artisan bread. Serve with more of the hot honey on the side. (Juan Figueroa / Staff Photographer)\nPeaches with Burrata and Hot Honey\n4 ripe peaches\nExtra virgin olive oil, for drizzling\nCoarse salt, to taste\n\u00bd small lemon\n8 ounces burrata (can be two 4-ounce balls)\nSimple Hot Honey, to taste (recipe follows)\n5 tablespoons fresh basil or mint leaves (or a combination of the two), torn or sliced\nGrilled artisan bread\nSlice peaches into eighths; taste, and if they are very sweet and juicy, proceed to next step; if not, pan roast them in a hot, liberally oiled, large skillet, turning once, until they begin to caramelize.\nTransfer peaches to a serving platter and squeeze lemon juice evenly over them. Sprinkle with salt to taste. Nestle burrata into the center of the peaches. If you did not cook the peaches, drizzle lightly with olive oil. Drizzle peaches with 2 tablespoons hot honey. Scatter herbs over peaches. Serve with grilled bread and additional honey on the side.\nMakes 6 servings.\nSource: Tina Danze\nServe Spicy-Sweet Baked Salmon with rice and lime wedges. (Juan Figueroa / Staff Photographer)\nSpicy-Sweet Baked Salmon\n4 (6-ounce) skin-on salmon filets, preferably 1-inch thick\n7 tablespoons honey\n1 1/2 teaspoon dried chile flakes\n2 tablespoons lime juice\n1 \u00bd tablespoons soy sauce\nOlive oil for coating\nKosher salt to taste\nCilantro or chopped green onion for garnish (optional)\nLime wedges and steamed rice for serving\nRemove salmon from refrigerator 10 minutes before cooking. Heat oven to 400 degrees F.\nMeanwhile, in a small saucepan, combine honey, chile flakes, lime juice and soy sauce. Bring to a boil, then reduce heat to low and simmer very gently for 5 minutes. Remove from heat and let cool.\nLine bottom and sides of a 9-by-12-inch baking pan or rimmed baking sheet with foil, for easy clean-up. Lightly film the foil with oil.\nUsing paper towels, blot salmon filets dry. Arrange skin-side down in prepared baking pan. Using a brush (or your fingers), rub the surface of each filet with a little oil. Season with kosher salt. Spoon or brush cooled glaze evenly on top of the filets.\nRoast for 6 minutes (3 to 4 minutes if they are thinner than 1 inch), then remove from oven and baste filets with the glaze that accumulates in the pan. Return to oven and bake until fish flakes easily and is still pink in the center, for medium doneness (another 6 minutes for a 1-inch thick filet, or until an instant-read thermometer, inserted in the thickest part reads 125-130 degrees F). Thinner filets will cook quickly. Serve with rice and lime wedges. Drizzle remaining glaze over the salmon and garnish with cilantro or green onion, if using.\nMakes 4 servings.\nSource: Tina Danze\nSpicy Melon Salad can be tossed with either mint or basil for a fresh finish to a summer meal. (Juan Figueroa / Staff Photographer)\nSpicy Melon Salad\n1 teaspoon grated lime zest\n\u00bc cup freshly squeezed lime juice (about 2 limes)\n4 teaspoons honey\n1 \u00bc teaspoons finely grated ginger root\n1 teaspoon sambal oelek (Thai red chili paste)\n6 to 7 cups mixed melons (any combination of honeydew, cantaloupe and watermelon), cut into 1-inch pieces\n2 tablespoons torn fresh basil or mint leaves\nCombine lime zest, lime juice, honey, ginger and sambal/chile paste. Whisk until well incorporated. Set aside (may be prepared ahead of time and refrigerated, covered, in a glass jar or bowl). Place melon pieces in a large serving bowl. Just before serving, toss with dressing and top with the mint or basil.\nMakes 6 servings.\nSource: Adapted from Mary Kimbrough by Tina Danze\nSimple hot honey is easy to make in just a few minutes at home and, best of all, you can customize the heat. (Juan Figueroa / Staff Photographer)\nEasy homemade hot honey \u2014 and 10 great ways to use it\nStore-bought hot honey is nearly twice the price of plain honey, but it\u2019s a cinch to make in just a few minutes. Besides saving you money, there\u2019s a huge advantage to making your own: You can customize the spiciness. If you find your batch too spicy, add a bit more honey; if it\u2019s not spicy enough, a few drops of Sriracha or other hot sauce will kick up the heat instantly. If you\u2019re timid, make a half-batch of hot honey for your first run-through. Below is a simple recipe, followed by many ways to use the honey.\nSimple Hot Honey\n1 cup honey\n2 to 3 teaspoons chile flakes\n1 tablespoon cider vinegar\nBig pinch of salt\nSriracha or other hot sauce (optional, if spicier profile is desired)\nIn a small pot over medium-low heat, combine honey, red chile flakes, cider vinegar and salt. When mixture begins to simmer, immediately reduce heat to lowest setting and maintain a bare simmer for 3 to 5 minutes (longer for spicier honey), watching closely so that it doesn\u2019t boil. Remove from heat and allow honey to cool. If you like, strain out the chile flakes through a mesh sieve; the longer the flakes steep in the honey, the spicier it gets. Taste, and if you find it too spicy, add more plain honey; if it\u2019s not spicy enough, add a few drops of Sriracha sauce or other hot sauce. Store in a tightly covered glass jar in the refrigerator for up to 4 weeks; bring to room temperature before using.\n10 ways to use hot honey\n1. Toss with roasted vegetables like Brussels sprouts or sweet potatoes.\n2. Pour it over baked brie.\n3. Drizzle it on a pizza topped with pepperoni or any other salumi.\n4. Drizzle it on fried chicken, or waffles with fried chicken tenders.\n5. Slather it on buttered biscuits or cornbread.\n6. Add it to stir-fried dishes at the tail end of cooking.\n7. Drizzle it over whole milk ricotta and serve with grilled bread.\n8. Nestle a small bowl of it into a charcuterie and cheese board, accompanied by bread or crackers.\n9. Drizzle it over grilled pineapple, pears or sliced strawberries.\n10. Use it in place of simple syrup in cocktails.",

            "title": "Why are so many fast food brands obsessed with bucket hat merch lately?",
            "description": "Pizza Hut is the latest brand to jump on the fun and funky trend with its limited-edition, reversible Hut Hat",
            "content": "Whether you love 'em or hate 'em, there's no denying the fact that bucket hats, the cylindrical-shaped accessory, has made a big comeback.\nFashion experts over at Who What Wear, The Hollywood Reporter and Cosmopolitan know it's true. And so do major fast food brands, who have been launching their collection of bucket hat merchandise like clockwork. Back in July 2019, KFC Russia partnered with Russian streetwear label Mam Cupy to launch their limited-edition bucket hats, which are adorned with the brand's iconic red and white stripe pattern and logos. To the naked eye, the hats may be mistaken for an overturned bucket of the chain's signature 8 pc. fried chicken meal. But a closer look reveals that the accessory is indeed meant to be worn on your head, rather than served on the dining table.\nFollowing suit was KFC's U.K. wing, which partnered with lifestyle brand Hype to create a limited-edition clothing line inspired by their iconic logo. The complete collection included bralettes, joggers, tees, puffer jackets, backpacks, bucket hats and, even, dad caps with the slogan \"Bargain Bucket\" embroidered across the front. Here in the states, Wendy's linked up with \"Rick and Morty\" in celebration of its sixth season to release a few limited-time meal options and themed merch, like bucket hats, sweatpants, and hoodies. McDonald's also released its McFlurry Bucket Hat, which is currently on sale for just $9.99.\nThe latest fast food brand to join the bandwagon is Pizza Hut, which partnered with Chain, the Los Angeles based pop-up culinary collective, to release its limited-edition reversible Hut Hat. Per Pizza Hut, \"The Hut Hat is a reversible bucket hat with one side resembling Pizza Hut's iconic red roof and the other a checkered design with black, white and red, resembling the lampshades hanging within Pizza Hut restaurants nationwide.\"\n\"Those Pizza Hut Tiffany lamps are some of the most iconic pieces of New Americana design,\" said Nicholas Kraft, a co-founder at Chain, \"They elicit such joy and fond memories \u2014 and let's be real: they kinda look like bucket hats all on their own.\"\nThe nostalgia factor has been a major reason why bucket hats are making their resurgence in 2023. Thanks to TikTok fashion, celebrity street style and Gen Z's fascination with the Y2K aesthetic, the accessory \u2014 which was a divisive trend circa the '90s \u2014 is acquiring a new generation of fans. It's also worth mentioning that bucket hats are incredibly versatile: Depending on the fabric it's made of, the hat can either be a spring or summer accessory (if it's made from a light material like denim or cotton) or a winter accessory (if it's made from a thicker material like wool or terry cloth). And, it's effortlessly chic. As Vogue's Christian Allaire wrote:\nBut even in classic black, paired with a dressier ensemble, I've learned that the bucket adds just the right amount of extra, without going overboard. It adds instant personality to a look in a way that a dad cap simply cannot. And so what if people think you look like you're trying to dress like a teen? If the topper is good enough for Rihanna, it's good enough for all of us.\nEssentially, bucket hats are simple, convenient and timeless \u2014 which are, surprisingly, the same traits that make fast foods so popular amongst consumers. The hats also greatly resemble popular food items, making them the perfect accessory to don when you're not quite hungry but are still thinking about your favorite cravings. A simple bucket hat can be transformed into a bucket of fried chicken. Sprinkle in a bit of imagination and the hats can also be transformed into a cake, a cupcake and, yeah, even a gumdrop!\nWant more great food writing and recipes? Subscribe to Salon Food's newsletter, The Bite.\nFast food's latest fashion obsession further illustrates how more brands are appealing to their younger consumers. In recent years, fast food has become a hot and buzzy subject for content creators, who have garnered significant followings simply by sharing menu hacks, mukbangs and videos of themselves eating their go-to orders from their local Mickey D's. Such content is enticing and attractive and tempting. So, what better way to enhance those posts than with some fun and funky fashion.\nIt will be interesting to see what other fast food brands will jump on the trend next. Personally, we'd love to see a hat that looks like Auntie Anne's blue-and-yellow pretzel cups (and smells faintly like their signature cinnamon sugar pretzel). As for now, we'll wait patiently for that release and, in the meantime, try our best to secure a Hut Hat.",

            "title": "Vodka butter goes viral on TikTok on heels of #ButterBoard trend",
            "description": "A shot of vodka, a stick of butter, a whirl in the food processor and TikTok is hooked \u2014\u00a0say hello to vodka butter, the simple new sensation whipped up by chef and food blogger Carolina Gelen.",
            "content": "Mooove over, regular butter.\nVodka butter is the boozy, viral new spread intoxicating social media \u2014 and making a splash at dinner parties.\nChef and food blogger Carolina Gelen wowed the internet by whipping up the recipe for her TikTok followers \u2014 and it\u2019s easy to churn out at home.\nThe clever condiment maker combined one stick of butter with a shot of vodka in a food processor, whipping the mixture to a velvety texture.\n\u201cIt comes together in less than five minutes,\u201d Gelen assured in the 36-second clip.\nAfter perfecting her fat-and-acid blend, she spread the butter liberally over a crisp baguette.\nThe inspiration for the concoction came while sipping a favorite cocktail, Gelen told The Post.\n\u201cI love a dirty martini, so I tried making a dirty martini butter with green olives and vodka, but the olives ended up being overpowering flavor-wise,\u201d Gelen said. \u201cSo I tried the recipe again, without olives, and the result was amazing.\u201d\nGelen gleaned inspiration from her favorite drink, a dirty martini, but found the green olives too overpowering. Courtesy of Carolina Gelen\nThe chef-blogger\u2019s vodka butter recipe can be whipped up in mere minutes. Courtesy of Carolina Gelen\nAnd while the sky\u2019s the limit, Gelen suggested looking to the briny deep for the perfect pairing \u2014 say, a nice, salty tinned fish like sardines, or, for something more luxurious, a dollop of caviar.\n\u201cI wouldn\u2019t spread this on toast for breakfast but would serve it as an appetizer with crusty bread, anchovies, herring, sardines or caviar,\u201d she said.\nThe delicious discovery is just the latest example of butter\u2019s moment in the spotlight.\nLast year, butter boards \u2014 an indulgent riff on the charcuterie board, featuring butter garnished with sauces, fruits and spices \u2014 became an overnight sensation, with the hashtag #ButterBoard gaining more than 234 million views on TikTok. Many users and healthcare professionals scoffed at the idea of a full plate of butter.\nSome NYC chefs have already been experimenting with the new butter sensation.
