            "title": "Cottage Cheese \u2018Ice Cream\u2019: Is It Worth the Hype?",
            "description": "I tried the latest viral TikTok food trend, cottage cheese ice cream, and it's a sweet, satisfying, protein-packed treat.",
            "content": "You\u2019ve seen the chickles, the pancake spaghetti and the blue cookies, but TikTok\u2019s latest recipe obsession is cottage cheese \"ice cream.\" This unlikely-sounding treat has hundreds of taste-testers singing its praises, no ice cream maker required. And most versions have just a couple of base ingredients, two cups of cottage cheese and two tablespoons of honey or maple syrup, with infinite mix-ins like cookie bits, fruit, cocoa powder and peanut butter.\nDepending on the flavorings, a one-cup serving comes in around 300 calories, packing at least 25 grams of protein and almost 200 milligrams of calcium.\nThe top-viewed video on TikTok is @paigelindgren\u2019s banana cream pie tutorial, with 1.2 million views. She suggests adding graham cracker crumbs to make the pie flavor pop.\nGet Boston local news, weather forecasts, lifestyle and entertainment stories to your inbox. Sign up for NBC Boston\u2019s newsletters.\nTikToker @lainiecooks has a strawberry cheesecake version with about a million views and a game-changing method: Pour the mix right back into the original cottage cheese container for easy peasy freezing.\nDespite rave reviews and an excellent nutritional profile, a few commenters are worried about the repercussions when TikTok has a new ingredient darling. \u201cCottage cheese is the new cauliflower,\u201d quipped user @shekinah, and @leeannwithswan answered, \u201cCottage cheese gonna be $25.\u201d\nI actually agree with some of the eye-rolling over adding cottage cheese to everything, because there is such a thing as too much protein, and in my registered dietitian opinion, a lot of TikTok food trends lean hard into toxic diet culture. Nutritional tunnel vision can lead to forgetting to include other important nutrients like fiber and vitamins, and even unhealthy patterns like restriction or orthorexia.\nCottage cheese can be pretty unpredictable when added to recipes that are cooked, too. Not only do high-protein foods like eggs not need more added to make them healthy, but scramble them with cottage cheese just a few seconds too long, and you\u2019ll get watery eggs interspersed with something like mozzarella.\nI like the idea of this recipe because it takes a very sugar-heavy dessert and makes it more balanced, easier to indulge without overdoing it. It also doesn\u2019t rely on ultra-processed protein powders or diet sweeteners.\nThe apparent originator of the trend has a similar take on healthy eating. Kenzie Akers (@kenzieakers), Indiana-based wellness advocate and mom of three, got the idea to freeze her usual high-protein shakes and cheesecake dips all the way back in March 2022.\nI caught up with Akers to talk about her creation, and she says she started increasing her protein and moderating her carb intake as part of a weight loss strategy. This kind of sweet, indulgent snack with a healthy and filling twist is one of her favorite techniques, but she\u2019s a big believer in a real-food approach.\n\u201cI\u2019ve been through the entire journey of restriction and substitutions, and after all that, you still want the real stuff! So, I just eat the real stuff, and fit it into a healthy diet overall,\u201d she says. When she posted a video of a fast-food meal recently, commenters called it a \"cheat day,\" but Akers says she wants people to know it\u2019s not cheating to her \u2014 it\u2019s part of a balanced and joyful lifestyle. I can get behind that!\nCommenters\u2019 feedback has been almost totally positive, so she has a series of other cottage cheese recipes coming up, including a \u201cbrownie batter\u201d dip.\nNaturally, I couldn't resist trying it myself. One tip \u2014 if you have already used a bit of cottage cheese for something else, you\u2019ll have room to blend everything right in the container with a blade guard-equipped immersion blender, but be sure to hold it securely and vertically for safety.\nI tried several batches, blending in melted chocolate chips, fresh berries, bananas and vanilla extract. Both full-fat and 2% cottage cheese worked well. The thick texture makes it incredibly easy to fold in a perfect swirl of jam or marshmallow spread, and chopped nuts or fruit pieces don\u2019t sink to the bottom.\nAdding the graham cracker bits to a double scoop of chocolate and strawberry jam swirl yielded the best ice cream I\u2019ve ever made at home.\nExpect a little bit of a salty tang that isn\u2019t characteristic of traditional ice creams. A lot of the flavors are described as \"cheesecake,\" and I really found that to be the best description.\nMy favorite flavor? Churro! I melted sugar with cinnamon in a skillet, spread it on a silicone mat to cool and harden, and stirred it into a honey-vanilla base. The added sweetness and crunch are the perfect foil for the creamy complexity of cultured dairy.\nEverything I tried worked, but it\u2019s not entirely foolproof. Some additions, like sugar or alcohol, can inhibit solidifying if you add too much, and liquid ingredients might form larger ice crystals. One of the keys is to nail down freezing time for the best texture; it varies by freezer and container, but two to three hours is typical. If you find it\u2019s frozen too hard, resist the temptation to pop it in the microwave for a few seconds, because when you heat cottage cheese too much, you may find bits of a mix-in you\u2019d prefer to miss: mozzarella!\nThis story first appeared on TODAY.com.             "title": "What is tteokbokki? The next food trend is Korea\u2019s ultimate street food.",
            "description": "What is tteokbokki? The next food trend is Korea\u2019s ultimate street food.",
            "content": "Ask any Korean what their favorite street food is and, most likely, at the top of the list will be tteokbokki \u2014 the pillowy soft rice cakes doused in a sticky red pepper sauce. Now the dish is gaining traction in the U.S. thanks to a confluence of Korean popular culture and accessibility.\nPre-packaged versions of the food have started popping up at national wholesalers like Costco, online sales are spiking and tteokbokki-specific restaurants are expanding. There also was, of course, that moment BTS singer Jimin helped make it a trending item on social media when he was spotted eating it at a Seoul food market. Tteokbokki is also the preferred food of Ji-Young, Sesame Street\u2019s\u2019 first Asian muppet.\nO\u2019Foods, a global producer of Korean pantry staples, started selling instant pre-packaged tteokbokki in the U.S. in October 2021 through online retailers such as Walmart and Amazon. Its sales of the rice cake have increased 450% over the last year.\n\u201cOur bestselling product on online platforms has always been gochujang, but our tteokbokki product line has already become our runner-up within a year,\u201d O\u2019Foods company representative Justin Park told NBC News.\nHe added that tteokbokki has outpaced popular Western snacks at times, with his product and Quaker alternating for first and second bestsellers in the \u201crice cakes\u201d category at Amazon\u2019s Grocery and Gourmet Foods section.\nO\u2019Foods, which sells packaged tteokbokki, has had a 450% increase in sales over the last year. O'Foods\nIts sentimental value is not lost among second-generation Korean Americans, even as new clientele discover the comfort food.\n\u201cOne of my earliest memories is when I was 9 or 10 years old, I was in Korea for the summer, and my cousins took me to a jip (store) right by their house and I just remember crying, so mae-yo (spicy),\u201d said Joanne Lee Molinaro, creator behind the Instagram account The Korean Vegan and cookbook by the same name.\n\u201cI think that is a rite of passage that many Korean Americans have. As a result, its popularity kind of exploded. It\u2019s certainly exploded in my own blog,\u201d Molinaro said. \u201cPeople would print out that recipe and take it to the Korean grocery store and say, \u2018I want to make this.\u2019\u201d\nKorean food has long been gaining awareness with Western audiences. Bibimbap was an early Goop \u2014 and Gwyneth Paltrow\u2019s \u2014 favorite recipe in 2009. Korean barbeque joints in urban areas regularly command long lines. But the proliferation of Korean entertainment \u2014 much of which features critical plot lines hinging on eating (think ram-don in \u201cParasite\u201d) \u2014 has helped accelerate interest in dishes beyond Korean fried chicken and K-Bbq. And Netflix has turned Korean food shows and dramas into global sensations.\nYumi Yang pulls tteokbokki rice cakes apart before they are chopped up at San Su Jang rice cake shop in Los Angeles in February 1997. Gina Ferazzi / Los Angeles Times via Getty Images file\n\u201cRecently the boom of Korean music, K-pop, Korean dramas, Korean movies, and anything Korean culture has become a lot more popular and now accepted as something hip and cool and something to try out,\u201d said Hooni Kim, the first chef to receive a Michelin star for Korean cuisine. \u201cSomebody who has never tried Korean food but loves BTS and loves the movie \u2018Parasite,\u2019 it\u2019s much easier for them to try Korean food.\u201d\nKim sells a packaged version of tteokbokki in his premium food store in New York City, Little Banchan Shop. He sources his dduk from the rice cake maker and bakery Yedang, which he swears by. \u201cThis is the best place. I\u2019ve tried every dduk from every purveyor,\u201d he said.\nOnce a delicacy served in royal courts, the tubular rice cakes date back to the Joseon period in Korea, where it was traditionally cooked in a sweet soy sauce and beef. It is thought to be first documented in the 19th century cookbook \u201cSiuijeonseo,\u201d though its modern-day form has evolved to be red, sticky and sweet, simmered with hard boiled eggs and fish cakes. That too, however, is changing as new innovations bring new flavors.\nIn New York City, the restaurant Witch Toppoki is betting on rice cakes\u2019 growing appeal by bringing a wider selection to the public. The restaurant opened its first location in Flushing, Queens, in December 2022, and a second in nearby Bayside in February 2023.\nWitch Toppoki in New York City opened its second location in February. Youyoung Lee\nThe menu highlights a variety of rice cakes, from sweet potato to wheat to corn, and unconventional sauces like rose and carbonara. Rice cakes are short like gnocchi, medium-sized or long and skinny. Guests select ingredients from the buffet \u2014 most produced in Korea, save for the vegetables \u2014 and cook tableside.\n\u201cI think younger generations really like it, because this is the first concept in the United States,\u201d said Jerry Kim, general manager at Witch Toppoki, who said similar concepts already existed in Asia. \u201cThere\u2019s a lot of all-you-can-eat KBBQ, but [stateside] they never had a spicy rice cake.\u201d\nThe restaurant Yup Dduk, which opened in L.A. in 2015 and specializes in spicy Korean comfort foods, turned the flavors of tteokbokki into the popular hashtag \u201c#Ktownspicychallenge.\u201d It dares users to conquer rice cakes smothered in sauces so spicy they deserve a spot on \u201cHot Ones,\u201d like cheongyang pepper, which is 10,000 Scoville heat units, and the even hotter Korean ghost pepper, ttaengcho.\nYup Dduk is originally a franchise restaurant from South Korea whose massive expansion speaks to tteokbokki\u2019s potential across the pond \u2014 the franchise has 500 locations worldwide.",

            "title": "'Stubborn' food inflation leaves U.S. shoppers with slim appetite for other goods",
            "description": "As American shoppers stomach soaring food prices, they are cutting back on purchases of other goods, such as toys, clothing and housewares, in a challenging trend for retailers.",
            "content": "Summary\nSummary Companies Food retailers benefit from rocketing prices\nChoosy shoppers seek discounts on clothing, housewares\nHome improvement sales head down\nFeb 23 (Reuters) - As American shoppers stomach soaring food prices, they are cutting back on purchases of other goods, such as toys, clothing and housewares, in a challenging trend for retailers.\nCommentary this week from executives at Walmart (WMT.N) and other retailers shows how Americans are shifting their shopping habits and hunting for bargains in the face of the highest inflation in a generation.\nAt Walmart, the world's biggest retailer by revenue, Americans are still spending but are more \"choiceful, discerning, thoughtful\" about what they buy, its global Chief Executive Officer Doug McMillon told analysts.\nHigher prices on food led to soft sales of electronics, toys, home and apparel in the most recent quarter at Walmart. McMillon said he believed inflation on dry groceries and items made for immediate consumption would remain high \"for a while\".\nThe average cost for food consumed at home climbed 11.3% in January from a year earlier, Labor Department data showed.\nThat was nearly 5 percentage points above the overall rate of inflation, among the widest gaps since the 1970s. Food cost increases have outstripped broader inflation for nearly a year.\n\"Food inflation has been the most stubborn of all the categories,\" Walmart's U.S. CEO John Furner said.\nCoresight Research predicts annual grocery retail sales will grow by around $500 billion between 2022 and 2030.\nSharp sales declines in categories other than food are forcing retailers like Target (TGT.N) to slash prices on everything from toys to electronics.\n\"If I were a (U.S. retail) CFO right now, I'd probably have to be talking about the most conservative guide possible,\" said David Wagner, portfolio manager at Aptus Capital Advisors. The firm holds about $7 million in Walmart shares and recently sold its investment in Target.\nHOME IMPROVEMENT CRUNCH\nWalmart can withstand the challenge more than other retailers, investors said, as groceries make up a bigger share of its sales.\nWhile groceries comprise 56% of Walmart sales, they make up about 20% of sales at Target, which depends more on home furnishings, apparel and beauty.\nSales for last fiscal year at Target, which reports on Feb. 28, are expected to rise 2.7%, according to estimates, well below the 6.7% rise that Walmart reported.\n\"There is still risk in the market ... but for Walmart it is able to manage that risk and that's why we own it. We are overweight on it compared to, say, Target,\" said Eric McNew, portfolio manager at Summit Global Investments, which no longer holds Target shares.\nHome goods retailers are under pressure, with sales of home goods and home improvement merchandise, including gardening products, in 2022 making up just 11% of total retail sales, down from 15% in 2017, according to Coresight, which pulls data from the U.S. Census Bureau.\nAt Home Depot (HD.N), shoppers spent less on goods such as soft flooring and roofing in the most recent quarter, its Chief Executive Officer Edward Decker said.\nAt off-price clothing and homeware retailer TJX (TJX.N), sales at its HomeGoods unit slid 11% in 2022.\n\"We're still trying to figure out the home trend nationally,\" TJX CEO Ernie Herrman told analysts on Wednesday.\nLowe's Cos Inc (LOW.N), which reports on March 1, could feel a bigger pinch than larger rival Home Depot, as Lowe's tends to draw more do-it-yourself shoppers than inflation-resistant professional builders and contractors.\nIn clothing sales, Wall Street will learn more about inflation's impact in March, when specialty retailers including Kohl's (KSS.N), Nordstrom (JWN.N) and Victoria's Secret & Co (VSCO.N) report quarterly results.\n\"The macro outlook is on rocky ground and I think some of the data suggests that we may not have seen the last of this ... with recession looming on the horizon,\" said McNew.\nReporting by Siddharth Cavale, Kate Masters in New York and Aishwarya Venugupal, Uday Sampath in Bengaluru; Editing by Sonali Paul\nOur Standards: The Thomson Reuters Trust Principles.",

Perelman Department of Emergency Medicine at NYU Langone Health, told Today.com.\nIn 2018, the Food and Drug Administration issued a warning about dragon's breath, noting that injuries had \"occurred from handling or eating products prepared by adding liquid nitrogen immediately before consumption, even after the liquid nitrogen has fully evaporated due to the extremely low temperature of the food.\"\nIndonesia's Ministry of Health has warned mobile vendors against selling the snacks and advised other precautions be put into place, like informing children in schools, the Guardian reported.         {
            "title": "Are butter boards bad for you? An expert weighs in on the latest food trend",
            "description": "Here's what you may want to consider before whipping up a butter board of your own.",
            "content": "In an unexpected twist, butter seems to be back on the menu. After years of being a maligned ingredient that many people shied away from, butter has now become the latest food trend on social media, thanks to the recent popularity of \"butter boards.\"\nThese are sort of the meat-free equivalent of a charcuterie board. Butter is whipped then spread onto a chopping board, sprinkled with a variety of toppings \u2014 from sweet to savory \u2014 and served with an accompaniment of choice (such as bread or a toasted baguette).\nBut although they may be delicious, butter is still full of saturated fat \u2014 which many of us know can be harmful to our health. Here's what you may want to consider before whipping up a butter board of your own.\nIs butter really that bad?\nButter is made from cream, the fat-rich part of milk. While it's usually made from cow's milk, it can also be made from other milks such as goat milk.\nThe reason that butter has been seen as a no-go for so many years is because it's one of the ultimate sources of saturated fat. Butter contains around 80% fat, of which about two-thirds is saturated fat. It contains little else nutrient-wise.\nSaturated fats should be avoided in large amounts as they're linked with many health problems, including heart disease and shorter life expectancy. Clinical trials have also shown that saturated fats can have a negative effect on blood cholesterol levels.\nWhen it comes to butter on its own, it appears that eating it has a relatively small or neutral effect on the risk of heart disease. But research that compared butter to olive oil (another source of saturated fat) found that butter can increase levels of LDL cholesterol, which is sometimes called \"bad\" cholesterol as it's linked to greater risk of heart disease.\nBut the majority of the butter many of us consume in our diets comes from other foods such as biscuits, cakes and pastries. Alongside butter, these foods tend also to contain high amounts of sugar, while being low in other nutrients. High intakes of these types of foods is also linked with greater risk of heart disease.\nOverall, sharing a butter board with friends every now and then is unlikely to cause much harm to your health. But doing it often, or eating very large quantities, could raise cholesterol levels and increase your risk of cardiovascular disease somewhat.\nIt's also worth bearing in mind what toppings you serve your butter board with. Certain foods (such as processed or cured meats) also contain saturated fats and should only be enjoyed occasionally.\nButter alternatives\nSince butter is very calorific and fat-rich, some people may want to look at using butter alternatives for the base of their butter board.\nThe first substitute many people might look to is margarine. Margarine is chemically very similar to butter. Depending on the product though, it only contains around 40%-70% fat, making it a lighter alternative with a possibly similar taste.\nIn the past, the processes needed to make margarine solid resulted in the production of trans fats, which have been linked to increased risk of heart disease. But these processes have since been improved so margarine no longer contain trans fat. So it may be a good option for people wary of the amount of fat they consume.\nAnother alternative people may look at using is ghee, also sometimes known as clarified butter. A staple of Indian cooking, this is still made from milk, but the fat is much more concentrated as most of the water has been simmered away. This means it won't have the same creamy texture as butter.\nGrass-fed ghee is as rich in saturated fats as butter. It also contains naturally produced trans fats. However, these trans fats are different to the industrially produced types which are bad for our health. But since ghee contains more calories than butter, it may not be the best choice for a butter board, especially if you're looking for the best flavor.\nCultured butter may also be a choice for your butter board. This is made from cream which has been fermented like yogurt. However, no research to date has looked at whether the probiotics in cultured butter provide the same health benefits as those in yogurt and other fermented foods. Nutrition-wise, it contains the same amount of fat and calories as regular butter.\nAll in all, butter is not bad. But since it's very high in calories and cholesterol, you may want to try not to have too much. Sharing a butter board with some friends or loved ones every now and again is unlikely to have any long-term negative impact on your health.\nDuane Mellor, Lead for Evidence-Based Medicine and Nutrition, Aston Medical School, Aston University\nThis article is republished from The Conversation under a Creative Commons license.",
            "url": "https://www.salon.com/2022/10/27/are-butter-boards-for-you-an-expert-weighs-in-on-the-latest-trend_partner/",
            "image": "https://mediaproxy.salon.com/width/1200/https://media.salon.com/2022/09/rolled-butter-0909221.jpg",
            "publishedAt": "2022-10-28T00:35:54Z",
            "source": {
                "name": "Salon",
                "url": "https://www.salon.com"
            }

            "title": "FDA warns of viral Nyquil chicken food trend",
            "description": "Having chicken for dinner? If so, sear it, fry it or braise it but do not cook it in NyQuil, the U.S. Food & Drug Administration (FDA) warns.",
            "content": "SAVANNAH, Ga. \u2014 Having chicken for dinner? If so, sear it, fry it or braise it but do not cook it in NyQuil, the U.S. Food & Drug Administration (FDA) warns.\nThe cautioning comes after a recent social media challenge that encourages viewers to cook chicken in NyQuil (acetaminophen, dextromethorphan, and doxylamine) or another similar OTC cough and cold medication, presumably to eat.\n\u201cBoiling a medication can make it much more concentrated and change its properties in other ways.\u201d the FDA said in a written statement. \u201cEven if you don\u2019t eat the chicken, inhaling the medication\u2019s vapors while cooking could cause high levels of the drugs to enter your body. It could also hurt your lungs. Put simply: Someone could take a dangerously high amount of the cough and cold medicine without even realizing it.\u201d\nThis isn\u2019t the first time a social media challenge promoted unsafe behaviors. Earlier, there was a challenge that urged people to take large doses of the allergy medicine diphenhydramine, to try to induce hallucinations.\nAnother viral challenge involving medicine turned out to be deadly. In the challenge, people were encouraged to take large doses of diphenhydramine to try to induce hallucinations. This medicine is sold in many products, including some under the brand name Benadryl.\nThe challenge was reportedly blamed for the hospitalization of at least three Texas teens in May 2020 along with the death of a 15-year-old Oklahoma girl in August 2020.\nThe American Academy of Pediatrics advised parents to speak with their teens about which challenges are trending on social media or at school are suggest the following:\nStart a conversation by asking your kids about the biggest challenges they\u2019ve heard about in their circle of friends. Ask them (calmly and without judgment) what they think about the challenge. This helps build the skill of judging risk by talking about what could happen to someone who takes the challenge. You can still exercise your parental options such as limiting contact with certain kids or making specific activities off limits.\nIf your child is interested in participating in a challenge, use open-ended questions to encourage them to think through each step of the challenge. Ask them to consider the worst outcome. Ask them to think about why they would do it, and if it\u2019s worth it. Are likes and comments worth hours in the emergency department?\nBe sure to \u201cfriend\u201d your kids on social media, which can help you keep in touch with what goes on in their day-to-day lives. Watch their stories for clues about what is going on in school and with their friends.\nAsk questions about school trends, friends and fads. It\u2019s important to keep the lines of communication open and avoid passing judgment. Instead, calmly discuss the dangers in those choices.\nIf you believe your child has taken too much medication and is hallucinating, can\u2019t be awakened, has had or is having a seizure, has trouble breathing, has collapsed, or is showing other signs of drug misuse, call 911 to get immediate medical attention. Or contact poison control at 1-800-222-1222 or online.\nIf you have a question about a medication, including an OTC drug, call your health care provider or pharmacist or the FDA.\n\ud83d\udcf2 Download the FOX4 News app to stay updated on the go.\n\ud83d\udce7 Sign up for FOX4 email alerts to have breaking news sent to your inbox.\n\ud83d\udcbb Find today\u2019s top stories on fox4kc.com for Kansas City and all of Kansas and Missouri.",

How to master the food trend that could replace charcuterie Back to video Brooklyn-based recipe developer and food stylist Justine Doiron posted a butter board how-to on Sept. 15, sparking the viral moment. With 173.4 million views and counting, the #butterboard is sweeping TikTok. \u201cI\u2019ve been surprised since day one,\u201d says Doiron in an interview with National Post. \u201cWhen I first made the video, it was just to share a recipe that I love from one of my favourite cookbooks.\u201d The book in question is Portland, Ore., author, chef and restaurateur Joshua McFadden\u2019s 2017 debut, Six Seasons. Photo by Justine Doiron As with snack boards of other stripes \u2014 whether charcuterie or cheese \u2014 you don\u2019t need a recipe to build a butter board. Start with softened butter on a cutting board or plate, season with salt and layer the toppings of your choice. (Doiron considers flaky salt and citrus zest essential; the rest is up to you.) Doiron credits the appeal of the butter board to how customizable it is.             "content": "Meal planning is a food trend quickly becoming more popular. Here\u2019s a simple way you can get started and start reaping the benefits.\nPreparing meals for the week ahead is a food trend that\u2019s grown in popularity for its time-saving, health and lifestyle benefits. As daunting as the idea of cramming a week\u2019s worth of work into a matter of hours can be, healthy meal prep doesn\u2019t have to be complicated.\nHow do I start food prepping?\nIf you\u2019re new to meal planning and meal prepping, there are few simple steps you can take to get started. Eatingwell.com says the first step to success is determining the best prep method for you.\nCommon meal prep methods include:\nMake-ahead meals that can be reheated,\nBatch cooking and freezing multiple portions of a recipe,\nIndividually portioned grab-and-go meals, or\nPreparing ready to cook ingredients so meals can be cooked efficiently before serving.\nOnce you have your meal prep method in place, you can determine your menu and schedule some time to get cooking!\nThe next step to meal prep made simple is to take stock and shop. Take inventory of what you have in your kitchen and stock up on pantry staples like canned beans, rice, grains, dried herbs and broth.\nFinally, it\u2019s time to prepare and store your food. Eatingwell.com suggests making the most of your food prep time by beginning with foods that require the longest cooking time. It is also important you are mindful of storage life and keep prepared and cooked items organized with date labels.\nWhat is the best app for meal planning? Find out here.\nWhat foods last the longest when meal prepping?\nIf you\u2019re looking for a grocery list that won\u2019t spoil, here are a few time-tested ingredients to incorporate into your meal plan:\nBeans\nSweet potatoes\nRice\nBoiled eggs\nKale\nOats\nRoasted vegetables\nCanned tomatoes\nCanned corn\nIs meal prepping good for weight loss?\nCan you lose weight from meal prepping? The short answer is - it depends. Meal prep supports healthy eating when you incorporate healthier ingredients and more variety into weekly menus. You control what goes into the dish and it all comes down to health choices.\nRecipes, along with meal-prep strategies, can easily be modified to meet each individual or family's needs, dietary restrictions, and preferences.\nWhat are the easiest foods for meal prep?\nIf you\u2019re interested in what to cook that will last all week, Top10.com compiled a list of easy and delicious recipes for prepared meals that can be made in bulk to last the whole week.\nBlack bean and sweet potato enchiladas\nBlack bean sweet potato enchiladas make for a hearty vegetarian dish that supplies five servings in just 20 minutes of prep time. The enchilada sauce calls for salsa verde, which can be made at home or bought pre-made at the store. As an added bonus, this recipe includes modifications to make it gluten-free or vegan.\nBaked ziti\nBaked ziti allows for a lot of personalization: You can choose your preferred meat (Italian sausage, ground beef or pork, or a vegan alternative), your favorite store-bought pasta sauce (or make your own), and pasta shape. The recipe recommends using different fresh herbs depending upon the season, from basil in the summer to rosemary in winter. This dish is easy to freeze, so be sure to prep a little extra.\nVegetarian chili\nQuick and easy to make in bulk, and very freezer-friendly, this vegetarian chili is vegan, gluten-free, and packed with protein thanks to the beans and quinoa. Cumin and chili powder are key to the flavour of this recipe. Finish it off with a variety of toppings like sour cream, Greek yogurt, cheese, or chips, depending on your dietary preferences.\nSlow cooker sesame beef\nWith just a few inexpensive ingredients, slow cooker sesame beef is simple and budget-friendly. All you need to start this delicious dish is a 3-quart or larger slow cooker and 15 minutes of prep time. When the meat is finished cooking, pair it with your choice of sides like broccoli and rice, use it to top a cabbage salad, or load up a few taco shells.\nChana masala\nChana masala is a simple, delicious Indian dish featuring chickpeas. The recipe yields two servings perfect for a dinner or spread across workweek lunches. Chana masala can be spooned over jasmine or basmati rice, paired with naan bread, or served on its own.\nBaked mini frittatas\nFor fans of breakfast anytime, baked mini frittatas fit the bill. Following the recipe as given, with bacon, broccoli, and cheese, each frittata is only 127 calories; however, you can incorporate any ingredients you'd like. The recipe makes a dozen frittatas using a muffin tin: plenty for a week's worth of breakfasts.\nRed beans and rice\nRed beans and rice may take a little longer to fix than other dishes, but it's worth the time for six servings of a freezer-friendly meal. Quick-soaking the beans will decrease prep time. This recipe offers flexibility, from the type of beans and sausage to the level of spice in the dish. Ingredients for this meal are inexpensive and full of protein and fibre.\nSouthwest shrimp and corn succotash\nSouthwest shrimp and corn succotash is a healthier twist on a southern classic. The dish is given a TexMex flair by using black beans instead of lima beans, along with spice-rubbed shrimp and jalapenos.\nPro tip: Cut down on your prep time by purchasing shrimp that are already peeled and deveined. The entire dish can also be made using one pan, making clean-up quick and easy.\nSausage and kale lentil stew\nPacked with fresh veggies, sausage and kale lentil stew is quick and easy to fix with cook and prep time totalling just over an hour. The recipe makes six servings, so be sure to freeze any extra in order to enjoy it a month or two down the road.\nMoroccan pot roast\nMoroccan pot roast is a new twist on an old favourite: traditional beef chuck roast flavoured with nontraditional ingredients including eggplant, honey, and mint. This dish pairs especially well with flatbread or couscous. The slow cooking takes about seven hours, but when it's done you're left with eight servings that will last all week or can be frozen for a delicious meal later on.",

Quick-soaking the beans will decrease prep time. This recipe offers flexibility, from the type of beans and sausage to the level of spice in the dish. Ingredients for this meal are inexpensive and full of protein and fibre.\nSouthwest shrimp and corn succotash\nSouthwest shrimp and corn succotash is a healthier twist on a southern classic. The dish is given a TexMex flair by using black beans instead of lima beans, along with spice-rubbed shrimp and jalapenos.\nPro tip: Cut down on your prep time by purchasing shrimp that are already peeled and deveined. The entire dish can also be made using one pan, making clean-up quick and easy.\nSausage and kale lentil stew\nPacked with fresh veggies, sausage and kale lentil stew is quick and easy to fix with cook and prep time totalling just over an hour. The recipe makes six servings, so be sure to freeze any extra in order to enjoy it a month or two down the road.\nMoroccan pot roast\nMoroccan pot roast is a new twist on an old favourite: traditional beef chuck roast flavoured with nontraditional ingredients including eggplant, honey, and mint. This dish pairs especially well with flatbread or couscous. The slow cooking takes about seven hours, but when it's done you're left with eight servings that will last all week or can be frozen for a delicious meal later on.",

            "title": "Why gardening has more staying power than a typical trend",
            "description": "Gardeners are reaping more than just food and flowers while pursuing their new hobby",
            "content": "Kaleena Lawless, who lives in Petitcodiac, N.B., became an avid gardener during the pandemic and loves working in her new sprawling vegetable garden.VIKTOR PIVOVAROV/The Globe and Mail\nThere is a growing desire amongst people to grow their own food, to aid pollinators, to disconnect from screens and reconnect with the Earth. Supply chain shortages and inflation amplified this desire, culminating in many Canadians planting seeds in their gardens \u2013 and now those gardeners are hooked.\nChristian Lajoie, who owns Montreal-based Natural Seeds Canada, says that a significant number of these gardeners are parents gardening with their children, despite often being novices themselves.\nArley McNeney, a university instructor based in Vancouver, is one of those parents. McNeney began gardening in March this year after she and her daughter moved into a new place with a garden box in the front yard.\n\u201cWe\u2019d had a bit of a rough week in March, and I just went to the plant store [and] I bought some seeds,\u201d she explains. McNeney also bought some broccoli and strawberry plants, which she thought she would plant in neat rows, but things didn\u2019t go as planned.\n\u201cMy daughter just scattered the seeds,\u201d says McNeney. \u201c[Now] it\u2019s kind of grown into a tangle that we call \u2018The chaos garden.\u2019 \"\nDespite its name, McNeney describes the chaos garden as a source of tranquility and connection for her family \u2013 something she needed after her divorce which prompted her move and led her to lose most of her possessions, including her grandmother\u2019s dishes and coffee table.\nMcNeney\u2019s grandmother was an avid gardener, and gardening is a way for her to make up for that loss. \u201c[I realized] I\u2019m not going to get those things back, they\u2019re gone, but I can still connect with my grandmother in this one little, tiny way, and make us feel a bit more at home,\u201d says McNeney.\nGardening has also forged connections between McNeney and her neighbours, who offer her plant-related advice since gardening is often trial and error.\nSeed sales jumped during the pandemic as more people picked up gardening. Though sales are subsiding somewhat this year after remaining strong in 2021, many neophyte gardeners are sticking with their new hobby, says Benjamin Hutchinson, operations manager at Ontario Seeds Company.\nKaleena Lawless is another new gardener. She bought a house in Petitcodiac, N.B. in 2018: The house came with an acre of land that\u2019s now home to Lawless\u2019 sprawling vegetable garden. She picked up gardening when the pandemic lockdowns limited access to spaces like indoor gyms.\n\u201cI have definitely tried everything; I have a big garden tower where last year I did all vegetables like lettuce and cherry tomatoes,\u201d she says.\nTo reduce gardening mistakes, many new gardeners flocked to local gardening groups on Facebook, such as Gardening in New Brunswick Canada, which Lawless joined two years ago and now moderates. The group now has 19,000 members and counting.\nJennifer Jobin, a seasoned gardener and an administrator of the Manitoba Gardeners Facebook group, which has 24,000 members and counting, says the group enables new gardeners to learn and improve.\n\u201cI built the group and it\u2019s become a very large group,\u201d says Jobin. Gardeners appreciate the ongoing learning as well as the community. Lawless, for example, often shares extra harvest with her neighbours.\nThough gardening enriches gardeners\u2019 lives, it is often inaccessible to many Canadians \u2013 sometimes literally, since it requires physical labour and at least a bit of land.\nAs a result, many Canadians are now gardening on their apartment balconies as well as in community gardens. Amanda Johnson, who works with the Yellowknife Community Garden Collective, recalls that in the spring of 2020, all the plots across the collective\u2019s six community gardens filled up quickly. Interest in the lots has persisted, she adds.\nNew gardeners seem to be particularly interested in growing their own food, according to Jim Ternier, founder of Saskatchewan-based Prairie Garden Seeds. Ternier mentions an increase in sales of fruit and vegetable seeds \u2013 carrots and sweet corn are especially popular \u2013 as well as wildflowers to support pollinators. Lajoie is also selling a lot of saffron, which, to many of his customers\u2019 surprise, can grow in Canada.\nMany Canadians developed green thumbs that they have continued to nurture because, not only is doing so therapeutic and connects them to their communities, but the fruits, as Lawless says, are bountiful.\n\u201cThe garden is a gift that keeps on giving,\u201d she says. \u201cThe more you put into it, the more you keep getting back.\u201d",

            "title": "Inflation means more food insecurity for Canada's post-secondary students",
            "description": "The stereotype of students living off ramen shows how normalized this trend has become, writes Abby Jenkins",
            "content": "The rate of inflation in Canada climbed to 8.1 per cent this past June, reaching a 39-year high.\nThis increase in the cost of living is affecting the health and livelihoods of people across the country, and students are especially vulnerable.\nFifty-three per cent of young Canadians aged 15 to 29 reported being very concerned about their ability to afford housing or rent. This concern hints at the sacrifices students must make due to the cost of living: making rent or buying food.\nAs many as 40 per cent of Canadian post-secondary students are food insecure, meaning they do not have reliable access to enough affordable and nutritious food.\nMost students have a relatively small income during the year, which might come from a summer job, part-time work or informal jobs like babysitting. As expenses continue to increase with inflation, many students lack financial savings and do not have the capacity to work alongside their studies.\nAs a student myself, I can see how rising costs will continue to worsen food insecurity in the student population. Between the costs of tuition, textbooks and rent, many students have little to nothing left in their budget to buy nutritious and fresh food.\nThe popular stereotype of students living off dry packaged goods like cereal and ramen to keep them functioning illustrates just how normalized student food insecurity has become.\nThese foods are not sufficient for supporting optimal health. For many students, options like fresh produce are too expensive, perishable, and require prep time and cooking skills, which many students do not have.\nFood is essential for health and well-being, and should never be something students are forced to sacrifice. We need to vote and encourage institutions to have better support for students. These supports need to include: lower tuition costs, university supported breakfast programs and affordable produce markets, and greater OSAP (Ontario Student Assistance Program) funding, so students are not forced to use their food money for rent.\nAbby Jenkins is a policy and communications summer student at York Region Food Network. She is going into her fourth year at Western University studying global development, and is interested in holistic, sustainable and long-term approaches to systems issues.\nRELATED STORIES Ontario's 2023 rent increase will make more households food insecure\nSHARE:",

The breakup allows the snacks business to solely focus on still-strong pandemic demand and the broader consumer shift to on-the-go eating.\nOur search started with a list of North American firms with strong snack brands. All successfully compete in this expanding niche, which continues to bolster their prospects. We then applied our TSI Dividend Sustainability Rating System to a short list of income payers. It awards points to a stock based on key factors:\nOne point for five years of continuous dividend payments \u2013 two points for more than five;\ntwo points if it has raised the payment in the past five years;\none point for management\u2019s commitment to dividends;\none point for operating in non-cyclical industries;\none point for limited exposure to foreign currency rates and freedom from political interference;\ntwo points for a strong balance sheet, including manageable debt and adequate cash;\ntwo points for a long-term record of positive earnings and cash flow to cover dividends;\none point if the company\u2019s an industry leader.\nCompanies with 10 to 12 points have the most secure dividends, or the highest sustainability.             "title": "BEYOND LOCAL: The new Instagram food trend is normal-looking food",
            "description": "Since finding and eating edible food was crucial for survival, we may be hard-wired to feel intrinsically good when we simply see food we know we can eat.",
            "content": "Since finding and eating edible food was crucial for survival, we may be hard-wired to feel intrinsically good when we simply see food we know we can eat.\nThis article by Matthew Philp, Toronto Metropolitan University; Ethan Pancer, Saint Mary\u2019s University, and Jenna Jacobson, Toronto Metropolitan University originally appeared on the Conversation and is published here with permission.\nThe past decade has seen the rise of the Instagrammable food trend, where restaurants have altered menus to prioritize visual uniqueness \u2014 often at the expense of taste.\nIn a competitive social media landscape where users are inundated with content, the question for restaurateurs has been how to stand out and generate audience engagement in the form of likes, comments and shares.\nUnder the assumption that creating unique food items will help businesses stand out and garner more engagement on social media, the Instagrammable food trend has given birth to novelty items like unicorn lattes and poop cafes.\nBut does this strategy actually work? Do unique, distinct and atypical-appearing foods garner the most engagement? Or do people engage more with normal, familiar and typical-appearing foods?\nWhat people think Instagrammable food is\nSince social media platforms use rank-ordering algorithms to prioritize and boost content, figuring out which foods garner more social media engagement will help restaurants and food content creators determine how to better amplify the reach of their online content.\nConventional social media wisdom suggests that people will engage with social media content they deem entertaining, where \u201centertaining\u201d is synonymous with unique, distinct and atypical content.\nIn a food context, it has been assumed that entertaining means food that looks more unique, distinct and atypical.\nThis assumption has sparked an industry trend where restaurants have abandoned taste in lieu of visual aesthetics, such as bright and unusual colours, to spark engagement on visual-based social media platforms, such as Instagram.\nThere are many different examples of this over-the-top food trend on Instagram, from the Bagel Store in Brooklyn, N.Y., to Fugo Desserts, the Enchanted Poutinerie and Glory Hole Doughnuts in Toronto.\nWhat Instagrammable food really is\nOur recent investigation, published in the Journal of Business Research, investigates which foods are truly the most Instagrammable \u2014 in other words, which ones garner the most likes, comments and shares.\nOur research examined over 10,000 images of food on Instagram from over 850 top restaurants (according to Eater.com) using Google Vision, a machine-learning algorithm that extracts insights from images.\nWe found that when Google Vision was more confident that an image contained actual food \u2014 a proxy for how normal and typical the food actually is \u2014 the more social media engagement it received.\nA followup experiment suggests that positive affect, which is the extent to which we feel good, helps explain this relationship.\nWhile social media forecasters may suggest that unique foods are a trend, this logic contradicts some principles of evolutionary psychology. Humans evolved to quickly visually recognize foods, not just for what is edible, but also for what is calorie-dense.\nSince finding and eating edible food was crucial for survival when humans were hunter-gatherers, we may be hard-wired to feel intrinsically good when we simply see food we know we can eat.\nSocial media food marketing\nHow is this relevant to social media? The average user spends over two hours a day on social media platforms, exposing them to hundreds of different posts in a single scrolling session.\nWhile rapidly processing content, the brain may instinctively feel more positively toward images that are more easily recognized as food. These positive feelings can then transfer to behaviours directed toward the post, thereby increasing the likelihood of the post receiving likes, comments or shares.\nSince people feel better when they see foods that are easily recognizable as food, normal-looking food tends get more likes. On the other hand, unique foods tend to result in lower social media engagement because they are harder for people to recognize and classify as food.\nDespite food industry bloggers and social media trends suggesting that people crave unique, eye-catching content, the most successful Instagrammable foods are the normal-looking ones that are more easily recognized as food.\nNot ice cream disguised as feces served in a toilet, waffles shaped like penises or unusually coloured ice cream. Instead, consumers appear to engage more with regular food, like a classic burger or normal pizza \u2014 no unconventional shapes or colours required.\nMatthew Philp, Assistant Professor, Marketing, Toronto Metropolitan University; Ethan Pancer, Associate Professor of Marketing, Sobey School of Business, Saint Mary\u2019s University, and Jenna Jacobson, Assistant Professor, Ted Rogers School of Management, Toronto Metropolitan University\nThis article is republished from The Conversation under a Creative Commons license. Read the original article.",

            "title": "BEYOND LOCAL: The new Instagram food trend is normal-looking food",
            "description": "Since finding and eating edible food was crucial for survival, we may be hard-wired to feel intrinsically good when we simply see food we know we can eat.",
            "content": "Since finding and eating edible food was crucial for survival, we may be hard-wired to feel intrinsically good when we simply see food we know we can eat.\nThis article by Matthew Philp, Toronto Metropolitan University; Ethan Pancer, Saint Mary\u2019s University, and Jenna Jacobson, Toronto Metropolitan University originally appeared on the Conversation and is published here with permission.\nThe past decade has seen the rise of the Instagrammable food trend, where restaurants have altered menus to prioritize visual uniqueness \u2014 often at the expense of taste.\nIn a competitive social media landscape where users are inundated with content, the question for restaurateurs has been how to stand out and generate audience engagement in the form of likes, comments and shares.\nUnder the assumption that creating unique food items will help businesses stand out and garner more engagement on social media, the Instagrammable food trend has given birth to novelty items like unicorn lattes and poop cafes.\nBut does this strategy actually work? Do unique, distinct and atypical-appearing foods garner the most engagement? Or do people engage more with normal, familiar and typical-appearing foods?\nWhat people think Instagrammable food is\nSince social media platforms use rank-ordering algorithms to prioritize and boost content, figuring out which foods garner more social media engagement will help restaurants and food content creators determine how to better amplify the reach of their online content.\nConventional social media wisdom suggests that people will engage with social media content they deem entertaining, where \u201centertaining\u201d is synonymous with unique, distinct and atypical content.\nIn a food context, it has been assumed that entertaining means food that looks more unique, distinct and atypical.\nThis assumption has sparked an industry trend where restaurants have abandoned taste in lieu of visual aesthetics, such as bright and unusual colours, to spark engagement on visual-based social media platforms, such as Instagram.\nThere are many different examples of this over-the-top food trend on Instagram, from the Bagel Store in Brooklyn, N.Y., to Fugo Desserts, the Enchanted Poutinerie and Glory Hole Doughnuts in Toronto.\nWhat Instagrammable food really is\nOur recent investigation, published in the Journal of Business Research, investigates which foods are truly the most Instagrammable \u2014 in other words, which ones garner the most likes, comments and shares.\nOur research examined over 10,000 images of food on Instagram from over 850 top restaurants (according to Eater.com) using Google Vision, a machine-learning algorithm that extracts insights from images.\nWe found that when Google Vision was more confident that an image contained actual food \u2014 a proxy for how normal and typical the food actually is \u2014 the more social media engagement it received.\nA followup experiment suggests that positive affect, which is the extent to which we feel good, helps explain this relationship.\nWhile social media forecasters may suggest that unique foods are a trend, this logic contradicts some principles of evolutionary psychology. Humans evolved to quickly visually recognize foods, not just for what is edible, but also for what is calorie-dense.\nSince finding and eating edible food was crucial for survival when humans were hunter-gatherers, we may be hard-wired to feel intrinsically good when we simply see food we know we can eat.\nSocial media food marketing\nHow is this relevant to social media? The average user spends over two hours a day on social media platforms, exposing them to hundreds of different posts in a single scrolling session.\nWhile rapidly processing content, the brain may instinctively feel more positively toward images that are more easily recognized as food. These positive feelings can then transfer to behaviours directed toward the post, thereby increasing the likelihood of the post receiving likes, comments or shares.\nSince people feel better when they see foods that are easily recognizable as food, normal-looking food tends get more likes. On the other hand, unique foods tend to result in lower social media engagement because they are harder for people to recognize and classify as food.\nDespite food industry bloggers and social media trends suggesting that people crave unique, eye-catching content, the most successful Instagrammable foods are the normal-looking ones that are more easily recognized as food.\nNot ice cream disguised as feces served in a toilet, waffles shaped like penises or unusually coloured ice cream. Instead, consumers appear to engage more with regular food, like a classic burger or normal pizza \u2014 no unconventional shapes or colours required.\nMatthew Philp, Assistant Professor, Marketing, Toronto Metropolitan University; Ethan Pancer, Associate Professor of Marketing, Sobey School of Business, Saint Mary\u2019s University, and Jenna Jacobson, Assistant Professor, Ted Rogers School of Management, Toronto Metropolitan University\nThis article is republished from The Conversation under a Creative Commons license.             "title": "BEYOND LOCAL: The new Instagram food trend is normal-looking food",
            "description": "Since finding and eating edible food was crucial for survival, we may be hard-wired to feel intrinsically good when we simply see food we know we can eat.",
            "content": "Since finding and eating edible food was crucial for survival, we may be hard-wired to feel intrinsically good when we simply see food we know we can eat.\nThis article by Matthew Philp, Toronto Metropolitan University; Ethan Pancer, Saint Mary\u2019s University, and Jenna Jacobson, Toronto Metropolitan University originally appeared on the Conversation and is published here with permission.\nThe past decade has seen the rise of the Instagrammable food trend, where restaurants have altered menus to prioritize visual uniqueness \u2014 often at the expense of taste.\nIn a competitive social media landscape where users are inundated with content, the question for restaurateurs has been how to stand out and generate audience engagement in the form of likes, comments and shares.\nUnder the assumption that creating unique food items will help businesses stand out and garner more engagement on social media, the Instagrammable food trend has given birth to novelty items like unicorn lattes and poop cafes.\nBut does this strategy actually work? Do unique, distinct and atypical-appearing foods garner the most engagement? Or do people engage more with normal, familiar and typical-appearing foods?\nWhat people think Instagrammable food is\nSince social media platforms use rank-ordering algorithms to prioritize and boost content, figuring out which foods garner more social media engagement will help restaurants and food content creators determine how to better amplify the reach of their online content.\nConventional social media wisdom suggests that people will engage with social media content they deem entertaining, where \u201centertaining\u201d is synonymous with unique, distinct and atypical content.\nIn a food context, it has been assumed that entertaining means food that looks more unique, distinct and atypical.\nThis assumption has sparked an industry trend where restaurants have abandoned taste in lieu of visual aesthetics, such as bright and unusual colours, to spark engagement on visual-based social media platforms, such as Instagram.\nThere are many different examples of this over-the-top food trend on Instagram, from the Bagel Store in Brooklyn, N.Y., to Fugo Desserts, the Enchanted Poutinerie and Glory Hole Doughnuts in Toronto.\nWhat Instagrammable food really is\nOur recent investigation, published in the Journal of Business Research, investigates which foods are truly the most Instagrammable \u2014 in other words, which ones garner the most likes, comments and shares.\nOur research examined over 10,000 images of food on Instagram from over 850 top restaurants (according to Eater.com) using Google Vision, a machine-learning algorithm that extracts insights from images.\nWe found that when Google Vision was more confident that an image contained actual food \u2014 a proxy for how normal and typical the food actually is \u2014 the more social media engagement it received.\nA followup experiment suggests that positive affect, which is the extent to which we feel good, helps explain this relationship.\nWhile social media forecasters may suggest that unique foods are a trend, this logic contradicts some principles of evolutionary psychology. Humans evolved to quickly visually recognize foods, not just for what is edible, but also for what is calorie-dense.\nSince finding and eating edible food was crucial for survival when humans were hunter-gatherers, we may be hard-wired to feel intrinsically good when we simply see food we know we can eat.\nSocial media food marketing\nHow is this relevant to social media? The average user spends over two hours a day on social media platforms, exposing them to hundreds of different posts in a single scrolling session.\nWhile rapidly processing content, the brain may instinctively feel more positively toward images that are more easily recognized as food. These positive feelings can then transfer to behaviours directed toward the post, thereby increasing the likelihood of the post receiving likes, comments or shares.\nSince people feel better when they see foods that are easily recognizable as food, normal-looking food tends get more likes. On the other hand, unique foods tend to result in lower social media engagement because they are harder for people to recognize and classify as food.\nDespite food industry bloggers and social media trends suggesting that people crave unique, eye-catching content, the most successful Instagrammable foods are the normal-looking ones that are more easily recognized as food.\nNot ice cream disguised as feces served in a toilet, waffles shaped like penises or unusually coloured ice cream. Instead, consumers appear to engage more with regular food, like a classic burger or normal pizza \u2014 no unconventional shapes or colours required.\nMatthew Philp, Assistant Professor, Marketing, Toronto Metropolitan University; Ethan Pancer, Associate Professor of Marketing, Sobey School of Business, Saint Mary\u2019s University, and Jenna Jacobson, Assistant Professor, Ted Rogers School of Management, Toronto Metropolitan University\nThis article is republished from The Conversation under a Creative Commons license. Read the original article.",

            "publishedAt": "2022-06-20T00:55:00Z",

                "url": "https://www.baytoday.ca"

            "title": "Plant-based faux meat proving to have lots of sizzle, but little steak",
            "description": "After exploding on to the scene as the next big thing in food, the plant-based meat trend has come back down to earth of late as consumers and investors learn there's not a lot of meat on the bones when it comes to sales growth \u2014 or profits.",
            "content": "After exploding on to the scene as the next big thing in food, the plant-based meat trend has come back down to earth of late as consumers and investors discover that, behind all the sizzle, there's not a lot of steak.\nThe poster child for the trend is Beyond Meat, the meat alternative company whose highly touted IPO in 2019 saw it more than double in value on its first day.\nThe business case was simple: For economic, environmental and health reasons, people around the world would soon decide to eat less meat, and instead consume plant-based proteins.\nBeyond Meat had developed a burger patty based on pea proteins that tasted like real meat, and it was flying off the shelves. Other products designed to mimic chicken and pork soon followed, and investors piled into a company that seemed well on its way to hundreds of billions of dollars in sales.\nBut it hasn't really worked out that way. According to analyst Jennifer Bartashus at Bloomberg Intelligence, after growing by 13 per cent in 2019 and almost 40 per cent in 2020, sales of meat alternatives at the five biggest North American producers slipped by four per cent last year.\nThat's a group of companies that includes Canada's Maple Leaf Foods, which jumped into the trend with both feet in 2019 when it committed $310 million to build a massive new factory in Indiana to produce meat alternatives.\nMeat prices are up by 10 per cent in the last year, but alternative proteins have been hit by the same inflationary forces. (LM Otero/The Associated Press)\nWhile the company's plant-based products that sell under the Lightlife, Field Roast and other brand names booked almost $45 million in sales in the first quarter of this year, they \"no longer expect spectacular category growth rates,\" it told investors in announcing financial results last month. \"We do see the plant category growing, but at a steady pace\" which is why the company plans to repurpose some of its plant-based factory space to making meat products again.\nThe company still thinks its plant-based meat business is on track to ring in up to $10 billion in sales within the decade. But that's less than half what it was anticipating before.\nIndustry-wide, the sales slowdown is partly caused by the same inflationary and supply chain problems that the entire food business is dealing with. And consumers are proving to be less willing to swallow higher prices.\nRising prices\nAnyone who has been into a grocery store recently knows that the price of food is going up quickly, and of meat in particular.\nStatistics Canada data showed that meat prices rose more than 10 per cent in the year up to April. But meat alternatives haven't been able to benefit from the growing consumer trend to save money on food because they're up sharply, too.\nSylvain Charlebois, director of the Agri-Food Analytics Lab at Dalhousie University in Halifax, calculated in a recent report that plant-based meat is, on average, 38 per cent more expensive at the retail level than its meat-based alternative.\nThe gap is widest for things like chicken nuggets and burger patties, but even comparatively cheaper items like bacon and hot dogs have a premium price attached to them for plant-based versions.\n\"With a very high inflation rate, a lot of people are looking for bargains. But if you're actually looking at plant-based products, that's not what you're getting,\" he said in an interview. \"These products may be good for the environment and good for your health, but they're certainly not good for your budget.\"\nDespite those cost considerations, Bloomberg's Bartashus still thinks there's lot of room for growth to come, and is forecasting sales for all plant-based meat products to grow from about $30 billion today, to almost $170 billion by 2031.\nThat would be about 10 per cent of what the world spends on protein every year, and a big reason for her optimism is the environmental argument.\n\"Concerns about sustainably feeding a growing population are driving interest in plant-based products that can serve as a replacement for conventional proteins,\" Bartashus said in a recent report to clients.\n\"We expect growth for plant-based meat and dairy alternatives will outpace conventional products, supported by innovation, increased production capacity, lower prices, broader distribution gains and consumer acceptance.\"\nOthers are not convinced that plant-based eating is anything more than a trendy food craze that may have run its course. \"Three years ago, we were seeing these large sales \u2026 to a very large number of consumers that were very curious and wanted to try it out,\" said Simon Somogyi, a professor who studies the food business at the University of Guelph. \"But the true marketplace is showing itself now.\"\nWhile vegetarianism is a steady and growing force in the food business, Somogyi says most people interested in plant-based eating are happy to eat more fruits and vegetables and conventional proteins like beans and lentils, as opposed to factory-made plant products that masquerade as meat.\n\"They were all the rage and consumers were curious to try them out, but now they've tried them and that fad has sort of worn off,\" he said.",

            "title": "Why foraging is the viral food trend of the moment",
            "description": "\u2018Wild foods in general have lots of micronutrients that you don\u2019t get in any other foods, and the act of foraging and getting out there and living an active lifestyle is super healthy,\u2019 says chef Paul Moran",
            "content": "Foraging for plants like mushrooms and fiddlehead ferns is becoming a popular new hobby.Gabrielle Drolet\nThere\u2019s a word popping up all over the menus at the trendiest restaurants in town: \u201cwild.\u201d Wild mushrooms, wild leeks, wild fiddleheads, wild seaweed.\nPlants plucked straight from their natural habitats have made their way onto our plates, with many chefs and home cooks seeing their local woodlands, fields and bodies of water through a new, plentiful lens.\nWhy the sudden fondness for wild ingredients? \u201cI think basically people are paying more attention to what they\u2019re putting in their bodies,\u201d says Tofino-based chef Paul Moran, whose family has been foraging for five generations.\n\u201cThere\u2019s definitely been a shift toward health and nutrition recently, and wild foods offer some of the best nutrition and health benefits. And more recently, with COVID-19, people have been spending more time outdoors, and that has a lot to do with it too.\u201d\nIn 2019, Moran impressed the judges on Top Chef Canada with, among other things, his stuffed foraged morels. In fact, he prepared them three times over the course of the show, which he went on to win.\nExpert forager Peter Blush leads groups in search of spring's hottest menu item, fiddleheads.Terry Manzo\nHe now shares his exceptional knowledge and cooking through Wild Origins, a company he founded that caters events and runs \u201cforage and feast adventures\u201d where he brings guests on foraging tours that end with a multi-course gourmet meal prepped by Moran incorporating the day\u2019s bounty.\n\u201cOur horizons have definitely broadened,\u201d he says. \u201cThere used to be, say, wild mushrooms on menus, but now some of the top restaurants are making sure they have wild plants and seaweeds on menus, which wasn\u2019t necessarily the case 15 or 20 years ago.\u201d Wild garlic, it seems, is 2022\u2032s answer to 2015\u2032s quinoa, or 2018\u2032s kefir.\nExpert forager Peter Blush is also appreciating the uptick in interest. Through his company, Puck\u2019s Plenty, in Stratford, Ont., he\u2019s been sharing his passion for the wild foods of Southwestern Ontario with both curious newbies and more seasoned foragers since 2010.\n'The act of foraging and getting out there and living an active lifestyle is super healthy,' says Moran.Peter Blush\n\u201cAll our events are sold out \u2013 I don\u2019t even advertise anymore,\u201d he says. \u201cAs far as culinary movements go, people are interested in the fact that \u2018Hey, right out there in the woods, we can go and harvest food that actually tastes really good.\u2019 They want something different when it comes to taste, and that can set them on the road to foraging.\u201d\nMany of Blush\u2019s guests really dig into foraging after a tour. \u201cA common comment I get is \u2018From now on, I\u2019ll never go into the forest and look at things like I used to,\u2019\u201d he says.\nOne of those converts is Mississauga-based architect Jenny Chau, who attended her first Puck\u2019s Plenty tour three years ago and has been keen on discovering the fruits of the forest ever since.\n\u201cIt\u2019s a connection to nature,\u201d she says. \u201cBefore, we would go on hikes and go, \u2018Oh, interesting plants,\u2019 but we weren\u2019t looking for [edible ones]. Now we go hiking and we look for mushrooms; we don\u2019t necessarily want to pick them because we\u2019re not knowledgeable enough yet, but it just makes your eyes more attuned to what\u2019s available.\u201d\nChau was long interested in learning about foraging but was wary about going out on her own and picking something inedible. (\u201cIt\u2019s not a pleasant ride through your digestive tract,\u201d Blush says drily of eating the wrong plants.) But now that she\u2019s had a solid intro, she\u2019s building up to foraging independently.\n\u201cIt\u2019s very relaxing. You don\u2019t rush \u2013 you just have to observe [and determine] what\u2019s good and what\u2019s not. And when you pick some things, you feel very accomplished,\u201d she adds.\nTop Chef Canada winner Paul Moran is a fifth generation forager who now uses his expertise to guide groups in search of wild ingredients.Dominic Hall\nBoth Blush and Moran encourage anyone who\u2019s interested to join foraging groups on social media or go out with a knowledgeable guide the first couple of times. \u201cKnowing what to forage and how to forage is really important,\u201d say Blush. \u201cYou have to know what\u2019s poisonous and what\u2019s not. For example, wild leeks and lily of the valley look similar, but lily of the valley is poisonous.\u201d\nBlush stresses that the most important consideration is sustainability: knowing how to pick things without depleting the growth in that area. \u201cWild leeks grow in beds, and if you take the entire bed, they\u2019re not going to come back,\u201d he says by way of example. \u201cSo you don\u2019t take a lot and you take from the middle of the bed because you want it to keep expanding.\u201d\nUltimately, foraging isn\u2019t going to go very far in supplementing people\u2019s diets \u2013 but it\u2019s a good exercise in learning about our natural environment and an opportunity to enjoy wild foods. \u201cThey\u2019re a nice accompaniment for people to incorporate into their diet from time to time or when they\u2019re in season,\u201d says Moran. Foraged foods are also a way for people to eat more sustainably, a \u201chyper local\u201d menu with no guilt-inducing carbon footprint (unless, of course, you drove to that patch in the woods \u2013 and even then, that\u2019s nothing on the refrigerated miles logged by most other produce).\n\u201cWild foods in general have lots of micronutrients that you don\u2019t get in any other foods, and the act of foraging and getting out there and living an active lifestyle is super healthy,\u201d says Moran.\nA trend we can all go wild for.\nSign up for The Globe\u2019s arts and lifestyle newsletters for more news, columns and advice in your inbox.",

            "title": "Record number of people without enough to eat in 2021: UN",
            "description": "The UN says the total number of people without adequate food every day increased by 40 million last year, confirming a \u2018worrisome trend\u2019 of annual increases over several years",
            "content": "The World Food Programme headquarters in Rome in 2020.ALBERTO PIZZOLI/AFP/Getty Images\nThe United Nations said Wednesday that the number of people without enough to eat on a daily basis reached all-time high last year and is poised to hit \u201cappalling\u201d new levels as the Ukraine war affects global food production.\nAlmost 193 million people in 53 countries suffered acute food insecurity in 2021 due to what the UN said was a \u201ctoxic triple combination\u201d of conflict, weather extremes and the economic effects of the coronavirus pandemic.\nThe UN said the total number of people without adequate food every day increased by 40 million last year, confirming a \u201cworrisome trend\u201d of annual increases over several years.\nThe figures appeared in the Global Report on Food Crisis, which is produced jointly by the UN Food and Agriculture Organization, the World Food Program and the European Union.\nCountries experiencing protracted conflicts, including Afghanistan, Congo, Ethiopia, Nigeria, South Sudan, Syria and Yemen, had the most food-insecure populations, according to the report.\nThe report forecasts that Somalia will face one of the world\u2019s worst food crises in 2022 due to prolonged drought, increasing food prices and persistent violence. The various factors could lead 6 million Somalis into acute food crisis, the UN said.\n\u201cToday, if more is not done to support rural communities, the scale of the devastation in terms of hunger and lost livelihoods will be appalling,\u201d the UN said. \u201cUrgent humanitarian action is needed on a massive scale to prevent that from happening.\u201d\nThe war in Ukraine poses further risks for Somalia and many other African countries that reply on Ukraine and Russia for wheat, fertilizer and other food supplies.\nThe United Nations previously said the war was helping to send prices for commodities such as grains and vegetable oils to record highs, threatening millions with hunger and malnourishment.\n\u201cWhen we look at the consequences of what\u2019s happening as a result of the war in Ukraine, there is real cause for concern of how this will amplify the acute food needs that exist in these food crisis countries,\u201d said Rein Paulsen, director of the Food and Agriculture Organization\u2019s office of emergencies and resilience.\nThe report called for greater investment in agriculture and appealed for $1.5 billion to help farmers in at-risk regions with the upcoming planting season to help stabilize and increase local food production.\nOur Morning Update and Evening Update newsletters are written by Globe editors, giving you a concise summary of the day\u2019s most important headlines.             "description": "The COVID-19 pandemic brought us many food trends, from sourdough bread to Dalgona whipped coffee, many of which I\u2019m pretty much over.
