            "content": "This advertisement has not loaded yet, but your article continues below.\nKaren Gordon: Sushi bake, despite the name, is nothing like real sushi The one food trend from the pandemic that's made it into Karen Gordon's regular dinner rotation is sushi bake Photo by Karen Gordon / PNG\nReviews and recommendations are unbiased and products are independently selected. Postmedia may earn an affiliate commission from purchases made through links on this page.\nArticle content The COVID-19 pandemic brought us many food trends, from sourdough bread to Dalgona whipped coffee, many of which I\u2019m pretty much over. However, I found one food trend to be so delicious that it\u2019s made its way into my regular dinner menu \u2014 and that is, sushi bake.\nAdvertisement 2 Story continues below This advertisement has not loaded yet, but your article continues below.\nArticle content We apologize, but this video has failed to load.\ntap here to see other videos from our team. Try refreshing your browser, or Karen Gordon: Sushi bake, despite the name, is nothing like real sushi Back to video I\u2019m no sushi connoisseur but I\u2019ve certainly eaten my fair share of it. I can attest that sushi bake is unlike any sushi you\u2019ve ever eaten. It\u2019s a novel concept with origins in the Philippines and Hawaii. While it has the word sushi in it, it\u2019s really nothing like real sushi. In other words, it contains no raw fish and it\u2019s not even rolled. While it has the trappings of sushi, like rice and nori \u2014 Japanese dried seaweed \u2014 that\u2019s where the similarities end. Sushi bake is served warm, in a casserole dish, family style. It\u2019s a deconstructed sushi roll served on top of Nori. It\u2019s a layer of deliciously seasoned sushi rice, creamy and cheesy crabmeat, topped with a generous drizzle of Japanese kewpie mayo, unagi sauce and then baked until slightly caramelized. It\u2019s then cut into 1-1/2\u201d by 3\u201d size pieces or scooped with a spoon onto pieces of nori, folded up and eaten like a Mexican taco. It\u2019s great for those who find eating raw fish a little intimidating.\nAdvertisement 3 Story continues below This advertisement has not loaded yet, but your article continues below.\nArticle content And for those who love their California rolls, then this is the dish for you. The rice is warm, sticky, and slightly tangy, while the filling is rich, creamy and decadent. The combination is simply delicious! There are many ways of making sushi bakes. In its most basic form, it consists of a layer of sushi rice topped with a creamy mixture of imitation crabmeat, Japanese kewpie mayo, cream cheese and then baked until warm and slightly caramelized. However, other ingredients like cooked flaked salmon, crabmeat, tobiko also known as flying fish roe, avocado, green onions, unagi (eel sauce) and sriracha may also be added to add another layer of deliciousness to the dish. Here are a few tips for making a knockout sushi bake:\nAdvertisement 4 Story continues below This advertisement has not loaded yet, but your article continues below.\nArticle content \u2022 Use Japanese short-grain sushi rice. It\u2019s stickier than white rice and is used specifically for sushi making. It\u2019s sticky with a chewy bite. \u2022 Use a good, seasoned rice vinegar. My personal favourite is Marukan Seasoned Gourmet Rice Vinegar available in the Asian section of supermarkets. \u2022 Wash the rice well before cooking. This helps get rid of excess starch and prevents gumminess. \u2022 Add the vinegar mixture to the rice when the rice is hot. This will help the rice to absorb the flavour of the vinegar and give it that deliciously tangy flavour. \u2022 Use butter to grease the baking dish to prevent sticking. While you can use olive oil, butter adds additional flavour and another level of richness to the dish. \u2022 Cool the rice before compressing it in the casserole pan. Cooled rice will hold its shape, reducing the chance of the sushi bake falling apart when removing it from the pan.\nAdvertisement 5 Story continues below This advertisement has not loaded yet, but your article continues below.\nArticle content \u2022 Use Kewpie Japanese-style mayonnaise. Unlike regular mayo, Kewpie mayo is thicker and richer tasting. It\u2019s also a little sweeter and tangier which adds another level of complexity to the dish. \u2022 Don\u2019t skip the Furikake. Furikake (pronounced as foo-ree-kar-kay) is a Japanese condiment that adds a briny, umami taste to the sushi bake. It simply won\u2019t be the same without it! Photo by Karen Gordon / PNG California Style Sushi Bake Sushi Rice: 2 cups raw sushi rice 3 cups cold water 3 tbsp seasoned rice wine vinegar 1/8 tsp salt 1 tsp granulated sugar Filling: 1 lb. (480g) imitation crabmeat, chopped 1 block (250g) cream cheese, softened to room temperature 1/2 cup Kewpie mayo 1 tbsp sriracha sauce, more or less to taste 1/2 tsp sesame oil 1/8 cup tobiko, raw flying fish roe\nAdvertisement 6 Story continues below This advertisement has not loaded yet, but your article continues below.\nArticle content Topping: Furikake Kewpie Mayo Unagi Sauce Roasted seaweed flakes (optional) Green onions, chopped Dried nori seaweed sheets or roasted seaweed packets Soy sauce (optional) Instructions: Rinse rice very well under cold running water. Once the water runs clear, drain, and add 3 cups of cold water. Cook rice in a rice-cooker until done. In a separate bowl, add the seasoned rice wine vinegar, salt, and granulated sugar. Whisk until the salt and sugar is dissolved. Once the rice is done, fluff the rice lightly, immediately add the seasoned vinegar to the hot rice. Continue mixing gently. Do not mush up the rice. Once the grains are well seasoned, set the rice aside to cool. While rice is cooling, prepare the filling.\nAdvertisement 7 Story continues below This advertisement has not loaded yet, but your article continues below.\nArticle content In a medium size mixing bowl, add the cream cheese. Using a wooden spoon, mash up the cream cheese and stir until smooth. Add the mayo, sriracha, and sesame oil. Stir to combine. Add the chopped imitation crabmeat and tobiko. Stir well to combine. Cover with plastic wrap and refrigerate until needed. Preheat oven to 450 degrees Fahrenheit. Butter a 9\u00d713\u201d casserole dish. Add the cooled seasoned rice. Using the back of a wooden spoon, compress the rice into the pan, creating an even layer. Sprinkle Furikake over the rice then top with the imitation crab mixture. Using the back of a spoon, spread the crab mixture evenly over the rice. Drizzle kewpie mayo in a diagonal pattern over the crab mixture. Then drizzle sriracha in the opposite direction, creating a cross-hatch pattern. Sprinkle roasted seaweed flakes, if using. Bake for approximately 15 minutes until the top is caramelized and bubbly. Remove from the oven and garnish with chopped green onions. Using a knife, slice the sushi bake into pieces measuring 1-1/2\u201d wide by 3\u201d long. Then use a spatula to lift and remove the sushi bake from the pan. To serve, place sushi bake on top of a piece of seaweed. Dip in soy sauce, if using, before eating. Enjoy! Karen Gordon is a Food Blogger from North Vancouver who shares her recipe creations online at karentology.com and on Instagram at @karen.t.ology\nShare this article in your social network\nLatest National Stories Advertisement 1 Story continues below This advertisement has not loaded yet, but your article continues below.\nThe Province Headline News Sign up to receive daily headline news from The Province, a division of Postmedia Network Inc.             "title": "This is the pandemic food trend you need to try",
            "description": "A deconstructed sushi roll in a casserole format? Yes, please.",
            "content": "Sourdough and dalgona coffee may have gotten the bulk of pandemic food trend headlines, but fans of Filipino cooking likely had their social media feeds bombarded with pictures of the sushi bake as well.\nWhat is the sushi bake? In essence it\u2019s a sushi roll but deconstructed as a layered casserole. It usually starts with a bottom layer of sushi rice and is then topped with the stuff you\u2019d get in a California or Philly roll, or whatever the cook has on hand. Sheets of nori are served on the side where diners can scoop the rice into it like a wrap.\nWhile the sushi bake already existed for a few years, the pandemic catapulted it into viral fame, starting with home cooks in the Philippines before the trend reached Canada.\nWhile cafes and dessert spots have fully embraced dalgona-flavoured everything, Diona Joyce, owner of the Kanto by Tita Flips stall at the Market 707 outdoor food court, and the Tita Flips restaurant and catering company in the Junction, doesn\u2019t expect the sushi bake to dominate restaurant menus. In the realm of pandemic food trends at Filipino restaurants, the take-home kamayan has been a more ubiquitous hit as cooks condensed a table-sized feast into pizza boxes (Joyce prefers the sturdier aluminum tray for hers).\nJoyce first heard of the sushi bake when one of her regular customers requested it at the start of the pandemic.\n\u201cI saw it on my (social media) feeds with a lot of home bakers so I spent a week trying to make one myself,\u201d she said. \u201cWe offer it on the catering side because it\u2019s so different from our restaurant menu, and it\u2019s not something you can just make quickly for takeout. It\u2019s mostly Filipinos who order it because I don\u2019t know if the non-Filipinos even heard of it.\u201d\nAs an off-menu special request at Tita Flips, sushi bake starts at $20 to $25 for an eight-inch circular casserole that will feed between two and four people.\n\u201cIt\u2019s all about the layering of flavours,\u201d Joyce said. \u201cStart with good sushi rice and then you want something crispy. You also need a balance of flavours, something sweet or a little bit sour and salty.\u201d\nThe toppings vary, but the kani version (think California roll) is the most basic: on top of warm sushi rice is imitation crab, sweet corn, cream cheese, avocado, tobiko, kewpie mayo and okonomiyaki sauce (think of it as a sweet-and-sour Japanese version of barbecue sauce).\nThe salmon version has Joyce blowtorching chunks of raw salmon until they\u2019re slightly charred to give off a smoky aroma to contrast the sharp acidity of Sriracha and the smoothness of the kewpie (there\u2019s also a scallop version).\nBut the version Joyce is most proud of is the one with more traditional Filipino toppings: pork cracklings, sisig (chopped pig ears and snouts), lechon kawali (crispy pork belly) and lots of garlic (she has also done versions with adobo and bistek). It\u2019s a deceptively filling dish.\nWhile pandemic food trends come and go (anyone still growing their own green onions?), the sushi bake does have some signs it could have a bit of longevity: it\u2019s a relatively easy-to-make dish that comes with the comfort of casseroles, which can be made plain or fancy.\n\u201cYou\u2019re staying at home and trapped with two or four people in a household so it\u2019s something different to have,\u201d said Joyce. \u201cThe innovation comes from not having the skills to make sushi, so people are like, we\u2019ll make this instead.\u201d",

Postmedia may earn an affiliate commission from purchases made through links on this page.\nArticle content One of the biggest food trends taking place this year revolves around comfort.\nAdvertisement Story continues below This advertisement has not loaded yet, but your article continues below.\nArticle content As a nation, people are looking to be comforted with nourishing, rich, one-dish wonders that release soul-embracing memories, as well as offer a bit of tranquility in a volatile, pandemic-strewn world. We apologize, but this video has failed to load.\ntap here to see other videos from our team. Try refreshing your browser, or Award-winning Canadian company offers classic comfort dishes for this year's latest food trend Back to video This isn\u2019t a new trend \u2013 it actually gained steam in the last year, as more and more people sought solace in recreating family favourites, or learning to make things on their own, like bread, which continues to be one of the most popular foods being made. Social media has been awash with professional chefs and home cooks pulling out all the stops and offering recipes on such traditional favourites along with brand new riffs on such classics as soups, stews, roasts, sandwiches, along with soothing, made-from-scratch puddings, cookies and fruit squares.\nAdvertisement Story continues below This advertisement has not loaded yet, but your article continues below.\nArticle content And if any one company understands the allure of comfort foods, it would be Canada\u2019s award-winning Brandt Meats, celebrating more than 60 years of successfully feeding Canadians coast-to-coast with an array of smoked meats, deli and more, products made from family recipes brought in from the old country by Gerhard and Ida Brandt who started crafting their traditional sausages and deli meats \u2013 their kielbasa sausages, salami and Black Forest hams, to name a few \u2013 almost immediately when they first landed in Canada. Gerhard Brandt (who came from Prussia, while Ida hails from Furth, a city in northern Bavaria, Germany) brought with him the secrets of the work \u2013 he was a master sausage maker and butcher, a time-honoured trade he learned from his own father and grandfather.\nAdvertisement Story continues below This advertisement has not loaded yet, but your article continues below.\nArticle content The couple started out in a small storefront operation in Toronto\u2019s Junction neighbourhood, in the city\u2019s west-end. From a small mom-and-pop company and handful of people, the company has grown to include a massive wholesale operation and more than 150 employees, with Brandt products being sold in Canada coast to coast. The company also has a substantial European Food Market in Mississauga, crammed with old-world charm, not to mention a deli counter that goes on forever, and a hot table bursting with delights like Sauerbraten pot roast, schnitzel, cabbage rolls and more. supplied Although Gerhard passed away a few years ago, family matriarch Ida Brandt continues at the helm of the business \u2013 her daughter, Brigitte and adult grandchildren, Melissa and Richard all working side-by-side to continue in the family\u2019s proud tradition. Ida was once honoured as a Toronto SUN Woman on the Move for spearheading women in business at a time when the glass ceiling was one of the biggest obstacles facing many women of that time. \u201cThe success of a company is based on customer loyalty, and it\u2019s a message I\u2019ve been living all these years,\u201d said Ida Brandt.\nAdvertisement Story continues below This advertisement has not loaded yet, but your article continues below.\nArticle content \u201cIt\u2019s funny \u2013 the company has really grown and evolved throughout the years, but it\u2019s still family-run, and we\u2019re a tight-knit family,\u201d said Brigitte recently, her mother and adult children nodding in unison. \u201cI do believe having those old-fashioned values has contributed to the company\u2019s success,\u201d added Melissa, whose role includes bringing the company up to snap on areas of social media, and having a strong online presence. \u201cBut we\u2019re moving forward with new innovations while keeping the company\u2019s core values at the forefront,\u201d added Richard. Has the company been impacted by the pandemic? \u201cIt has \u2013 what company hasn\u2019t? \u2013 but we\u2019ve come back, stronger than ever,\u201d said Melissa, adding \u201cI think it\u2019s because of the very nature of the foods we do create\u2026customers just feel comforted.\u201d\nAdvertisement Story continues below This advertisement has not loaded yet, but your article continues below.\nArticle content Melissa admits that, even with her brother, Richard, \u201d you can tell there\u2019s a bunch of strong women who run this place!\u201d she says with a laugh. Photo by supplied / Brandt Smoked Farmer Sausage Casserole 2 Tbsp. olive oil\n2 large onions\n2 cloves garlic, minced\n1/2 cup dry white wine\n1-750 mL canned plum tomatoes\n2 medium red peppers\n2 medium yellow peppers\n1 yam or sweet potato diced,\nHandful mini carrots,\n4 smoked farmer sausages\nSalt and pepper to taste Heat olive oil in a large skillet over medium heat. Remove and cut sausage about 1/2-inch thick.             "title": "Alberta\u2019s food scene will see a large-scale mentality change in the year ahead",
            "description": "Some shifts could be seen as a rising trend in the eye of the beholder, while others feel like notable tidal waves",
            "content": "Ceviche and hibiscus tostada at Fonda Fora restaurant in Calgary, Alberta, on Sept. 9, 2021.Todd Korol/Todd Korol/The Globe and Mail\nAs COVID-19 marches onward for the foreseeable future, restaurants are more keen on successful operations as opposed to chasing a cool new ingredient or cooking technique. This will not be \u201cthe year of broccoli\u201d or anything of the sort. Instead it will be a year of large-scale mentality changes in an industry that continues to find itself in flux.\nWhile some of the accompanying shifts could be viewed as a rising trend (or hope) in the eye of the beholder, others feel like notable tidal waves.\nNon-alcoholic craft drinks as menu mainstays\nWhile the idea of a non-alcoholic beer is nothing new (looking at you, O\u2019Douls), the drink category has seen massive growth over the past couple of years. Now, many Canadian craft brewers have set out to create their own booze-free brews under their own brand or as new subbrands.\nHarmon\u2019s Organic, from one of the co-founders of Toronto\u2019s Mill Street Brewery, is the latest offering to hit the market nationally, while other producers such as Libra and Sober Carpenter have been gaining popularity for some time. Locally in Calgary you can easily find non-alcoholic beers by Partake, One For The Road, Village Brewery and Tool Shed Brewing at liquor stores, select grocers and, of course, some pubs and restaurants too.\nThe proliferation of non-alcoholic drinks applies to \u201cspirits\u201d as well. One can now track down all sorts of alternative products that draw inspiration from amaro, Aperol, gin and more.\nWill we see a non-alcoholic \u201cliquor\u201d store pop up in the Prairies this year? I\u2019d say it is not out of the realm of possibility.\nDiversity at hospitality awards, festivals and events\nSocial media platforms and recent culinary television productions are to thank for helping create a new crop of food personalities that better showcase Canada\u2019s diverse population. Top Chef Canada\u2019s 2021 season making a star out of non-binary chef Kym Nguyen is a perfect example of this.\nNow, here\u2019s to hoping our awards systems, food festivals and collaborative chef events take note when they are able to resume normal operations.\nThis week, an e-mail from the head of a national restaurant awards system landed in my inbox explaining an evolution to its (what many have viewed as) out-of-date approach to determining the country\u2019s best eateries. Perhaps a baby step in the right direction, but a step nonetheless.\nThe death of the solo restaurateur and continued growth of hospitality groups\nEver-escalating operating costs will make it incredibly difficult for a new restaurateur to open a business in 2022. There is always strength in numbers, and in food service multiple locations or concepts can in some ways help with shared labour, buying power and just easier operations overall.\nRestaurant groups aren\u2019t necessarily a bad thing, and are typically responsible for some of a city\u2019s most popular spots. Their pooled resources allow for a new eatery to open with plenty of buzz, seasoned staff and less risk.\nWith the prominence of hospitality groups across Alberta (Banff Hospitality Collective, Century Hospitality Group, Concorde Group), Saskatchewan (Taste Hospitality) and Manitoba (Academy Hospitality), it is more likely we will see talented young chefs being given their own restaurants to run under an existing umbrella, as opposed to venturing out on their own.\nThe reinvention of convenience stores and small grocers\nCalgary has been a bit ahead of the curve when it comes to funky convenience stores (Lukes Drug Mart, the Blue Store) and small-scale grocers (Bridgeland Market) \u2013 now it\u2019s time for the rest of the Prairies to catch up.\nIf what has happened in Calgary in the past few years \u2013 and, much more recently, Vancouver \u2013 is any indication, we will see a new generation of corner-store owners getting creative with humble neighbourhood shop spaces by stocking more locally minded products and including grab-and-go food stalls.\nYes, I still want the occasional slushy, but if I can pick up some craft sodas made by Dandy Brewing Co. and some cured meats by Empire Provisions too, all the better.\nCollaborations between restaurants and local creatives\nIt\u2019s becoming commonplace for businesses, including restaurants, to collaborate with visual artists, muralists and illustrators. The trend is fuelled in part by an earnest interest in supporting the local arts scene \u2013 but also by a desire to stand out in a crowded scene.\nIn Calgary, contemporary Mexican eatery Fonda Fora boasts striking portraits by Maya Gohill; the General Block building in Bridgeland (which houses Una Pizza, Village Ice Cream and Phil & Sebastian Coffee Roasters) regularly asks local muralists to create stunning artwork on its north-facing exterior.\nAnd while it\u2019s not an art collaboration per se, the new EQ3 flagship store in Winnipeg, the furniture maker\u2019s home base, uses its wares to bring to life a caf\u00e9 concept by celebrity chef Mandel Hitzer.\nWe have a weekly Western Canada newsletter written by our B.C. and Alberta bureau chiefs, providing a comprehensive package of the news you need to know about the region and its place in the issues facing Canada. With the current ban on indoor dining, something that transitions successfully from restaurant to home is Lucky Belly's healthy boxes.\nThe healthy boxes are prepped daily with both freshly chopped vegetables and house pickled vegetables. There is a choice of a bed of rice, salad greens or quinoa and optional protein. The choices tick all the boxes: keto, gluten friendly, vegetarian, vegan or spicy. With several options to choose from, each has a distinctive, house made vinaigrette.\nA house favourite is the 'beet box' with shredded kale, pears, diced beets, feta cheese, shredded carrots, roasted honey peanuts, with arugula pesto vinaigrette. Add your choice of brown rice, greens or quinoa and optional protein.\nRecently, due to the popularity of their healthy boxes, the popular north end restaurant has offered the house dressings for take home purchase.\nChoose from balsamic vinaigrette, arugula pesto vinaigrette, avocado lime vinaigrette, lemon dill vinaigrette or garlic aioli. Available in a half-litre jar, it's perfect to dress a salad or add to an entree. Even the packaging, a mason jar, screams homemade. Use them for a salad or make your own rendition of a \u201cfood bowl.\" With five different dressings to choose from, the combinations are endless. My classic, homemade food bowl combines quinoa, baby greens, shaved carrots, pickled beets, pickled onions, goat\u2019s cheese, sunflower seeds, and grilled chicken breast. The balsamic vinaigrette would finish this off nicely.\nThe food bowl trend started a few years ago in North America and is gaining popularity as it can satisfy all special diet requirements plus provide a complete, nutritional meal in one entree.\nTypically grains, vegetables and protein are all arranged side by side with the dressing on the side. They are visually beautiful and as we eat with our eyes first, this is a healthy dish we can\u2019t get enough of.\nAlthough the healthy boxes are a customer favourite at Lucky Belly, they also have a full menu of delicious tacos, sandwiches, soups, sides, and desserts as well. Something of note is that all the dishes are all homemade, from scratch, not just the delicious dressings.\nWhen you make everything from scratch it adds to the prep time and labour costs, but the results speak for themselves. The seasoned chef/owner, Dino Roumeliotis, says this is the most labour-intensive menu he has ever had at one of his establishments. The result is what I call \u201cclean food.\"\nMany in Guelph\u2019s baseball community know Roumeliotis as a baseball personality, but some may not know him as a successful restaurateur.\nHe is a co-founder of the Greek Garden here in Guelph and managed the largest dinner theatre in the country before venturing back to his roots here in Guelph. He opened Lucky Belly with seasoned restaurateur John Melehes in 2016, but since 2020 he has taken over as sole-owner.\nThe concept for the licensed Guelph eatery is unlike the usual local, casual eateries. I call it fresh and healthy, with just enough kicked up, diner favourites for when you have the urge to indulge.\nRoumeliotis tells me that: \u201cvalue, quality and consistency are rule number one,\" and I think he has successfully achieved that here. That, and the great customer service provided from staff that have mostly been here since inception in 2016.\nWhen you have a leader that was raised by lifelong restaurateurs who said things like: \u201cDino, make people feel like they are in your home and they will come back\u2026,\u201d you can\u2019t help but have a winning combination.\nNow, I am eagerly awaiting the the reintroduction of their house-made, free, pickle bar with three varieties of pickles. Pickles are low calorie and healthy, right?",

        {
            "title": "\u2018No-recipe recipes\u2019 are the food trend we need right now",
            "description": "Cooking the no-recipe way is like playing music by ear, adding your own riffs as desired, says Sam Sifton of the New York Times Cooking, author of \u201cNo...",
            "content": "In 2011, the biggest thing in cookbooks (literally and figuratively) was Nathan Myhrvold\u2019s \u201cModernist Cuisine.\u201d The five-volume, 52-pound opus delved into the art and science of molecular gastronomy, with detailed instructions on how to suspend tomato water in spheres of basil oil and otherwise turn your condo kitchen into the chef\u2019s table at El Bulli.\nA decade later, however, El Bulli is shuttered and the appetite for the kind of deconstructed food they served has waned. With pandemic lockdowns forcing people to work with whatever is in our pantries, a newer, gentler approach to cooking has emerged. Welcome to the era of the no-recipe recipe.\n\u201cIf the pandemic has taught me anything, it\u2019s that a whole bunch more of us are cooking all of the time now,\u201d says Sam Sifton, founding editor of the New York Times Cooking and author of \u201cNo-Recipe Recipes,\u201d a new cookbook with an appropriately no-fuss title. \u201cYou\u2019ve got people who previously would have gone out on a Friday night for an expensive dinner, who\u2019re staying home and having a roast chicken, a salad and a bottle of wine.\u201d\nAfter more than a year of cooking at home, Sifton says, home cooks are discovering the joys of creating simple meals, without worrying too much about quantities, special ingredients and other mainstays of traditional cookbooks.\nIn \u201cNo-Recipe Recipes,\u201d he offers a wide range of preparations, from peanut butter smoothies to chorizo nachos to soba noodles with tofu and kimchi, all of which promise big flavour and as little or as much complexity as you care to add. What you won\u2019t find are ingredient specifics measured to the millilitre, or the usual, precise step-by-step directives.\n\u201cIt\u2019s a recipe, just a different kind of recipe,\u201d says Sifton, who compares cooking the no-recipe way to playing music by ear, adding your own riffs and flourishes as desired. \u201cBasically you know it\u2019s going to go A to C to D and back to A, but how you get there, and what you do in between, is up to you.\u201d\nThe beauty of the no-recipe recipe, Sifton says, is that it has as much to offer beginners as seasoned home cooks. \u201cIf novice cooks get a great outcome from a no-recipe recipe, their confidence in the kitchen is going to soar,\u201d he says. \u201cAnd the expert home cook can take the recipe in a direction that he or she wants with greater ease than following a traditional step-by-step recipe.\u201d\nSifton isn\u2019t the only one embracing this approach to cuisine. Pam Anderson\u2019s bestseller \u201cHow to Cook Without a Book\u201d \u2014 which was ahead of its time when it was first published, in 2000 \u2014 was completely updated and re-released in 2018.\nMore recently, Momofuku founder David Chang and food writer Priya Krishna teamed up to work on \u201cCooking at Home: Or, How I Learned to Stop Worrying About Recipes (And Love My Microwave),\u201d which is due out in October. Chang has also lent his support to Anyday, a dishware startup specializing in bowls with steam-trapping lids that you can use to make meals entirely in the microwave. The alluring premise is that cooking can be so fast, easy and improv-friendly, you can do it with whatever ingredient(s) you have on hand and a few pushes of a button.\n\u201cThese recipes are crucial right now,\u201d says Maggie Hoffman, digital director at cooking website Epicurious, whose No Recipe Needed series offers riffable versions of favourites like roast chicken thighs, baked pasta and Thai curry. \u201cThey\u2019re the ones that are flexible enough that you can work them out on a busy weeknight, even if you haven\u2019t gotten groceries recently. You get the basic gist, and then you do your thing.\u201d\nNeither Sifton nor the experts at Epicurious insist that every recipe can or should be a no-recipe recipe; baking, for instance, doesn\u2019t usually lend itself to substitutions and omissions. For most things, however, no-recipe cooking is just a matter of taking what you\u2019ve got and making it the way you like it, adding inspiration and advice from the pros as needed.\nIt\u2019s far from a novel or revolutionary idea, but it\u2019s one with the potential to inspire and empower a whole new generation of enthusiastic home chefs. \u201cMy kids learn to cook by watching TikTok,\u201d Sifton says. \u201cThere\u2019s nothing written down. They just watch a bunch of videos about making a Korean soft tofu stew, and then give it a shot.\u201d\nFour tips for success in the no-recipe kitchen\nStock your pantry. No-recipe recipes are only as good as what you put in them, and since weeknight dinner prep doesn\u2019t lend itself to last-minute groceries, your success will depend on keeping a supply of trusty staples. \u201cIt\u2019s really helpful to have hot sauces, vinegars and oils,\u201d says the New York Times Cooking\u2019s Sam Sifton. \u201cAnd in my case, plenty of peanut butter, chili garlic sauce, pickles and different starches.\u201d\nSubstitute as needed. The best thing about no-recipe cooking is its adaptability, so don\u2019t worry if you don\u2019t have Aleppo peppers or shallots; red pepper flakes and yellow onion will do just as well in most cases.\nEmbrace seasonality. Once you\u2019ve stocked up on the basics, you can get inspired by whatever looks freshest at the grocery store or farmer\u2019s market, and use that as a jumping-off point for your next no-recipe meal.\nUse a recipe (at least at first). \u201cFor some folks, going off-book can be intimidating,\u201d says Epicurious\u2019s Maggie Hoffman. \u201cSo one move is to make a recipe as written first, and see what it tastes like, and how you\u2019d like it to taste, [before you] personalize it the next time.\u201d\nSam Sifton\u2019s Roasted Mushrooms with Buttered Baguette\nSimplicity itself and beyond delicious, even if you aren\u2019t able to secure a pound of perfect chanterelles.\nBaguette\nButter\nWild mushrooms\nOlive oil\nThyme\nParsley\nBuy a baguette and some very good butter, along with a pound of wild mushrooms, preferably chanterelles.\nClean the mushrooms gently with a damp paper towel, then toss them with olive oil, salt, pepper, and some fresh thyme leaves, if you have them, or a scant scattering of dry if you don\u2019t. (If you don\u2019t have those either, no worries.)\nPut the mushrooms on a sheet pan and roast at 375\u00b0F for about 15 minutes, or until they\u2019ve released a fair amount of liquid. Remove the pan from the oven and drain off the liquid into a food storage container.\nContinue roasting the mushrooms for 25 to 30 minutes longer, or until they\u2019re a little crisp on the outside and soft inside and awesome.\nCut the baguette and slather with butter. Serve with the mushrooms, scattering some chopped parsley over the top of both, if you have some to spare.\nTip: You can use the mushroom liquid to add as a seasoning for soup. Store in the refrigerator for up to a week or in the freezer indefinitely.\nRecipe excerpted with permission from \u201cThe New York Times Cooking: No-Recipe Recipes.\u201d Text \u00a9 2021 by Sam Sifton and the New York Times Company. Photographs \u00a9 2021 by David Malosh. Food styling by Simon Andrews. Published by Ten Speed Press, an imprint of Random House, a division of Penguin Random House.             "title": "Loblaw Q2 profit soars even as grocery sales level off from pandemic highs",
            "description": "Canada's biggest grocery store chain said renewed lockdowns this spring amid the third wave of COVID-19 kept food sales strong as the pandemic's eat-at-home trend continued. Loblaw Companies Ltd. said Wednesday its grocery sales dipped 0.",
            "content": "Canada's biggest grocery store chain said renewed lockdowns this spring amid the third wave of COVID-19 kept food sales strong as the pandemic's eat-at-home trend continued. Loblaw Companies Ltd. said Wednesday its grocery sales dipped 0.\nCanada's biggest grocery store chain said renewed lockdowns this spring amid the third wave of COVID-19 kept food sales strong as the pandemic's eat-at-home trend continued.\nLoblaw Companies Ltd. said Wednesday its grocery sales dipped 0.1 per cent in its latest quarter, almost reaching the pandemic surge recorded during the same period the year before.\nBut in a sign of how consumer behaviour is expected to shift as the country emerges from lockdowns, the company said basket sizes \u2014 the amount of groceries customers buy at one time \u2014 edged down in its second quarter while traffic in its stores picked up. During the pandemic, Canadian grocers said customers tended to shop less frequently but stock up more with each visit.\nLoblaw said it's expecting shoppers to return to its discount stores as restrictions ease, something it started to see in the three months ended June 19.\nThe company operates conventional grocery stores like Loblaws, Zehrs and Provigo as well as a discount division that includes No Frills and Maxi. Many consumers turned to full-service stores during the pandemic as part of a one-stop-shopping trend to reduce trips outside the home.\n\"Our grocery divisions performed well in the first part of the quarter, aided by COVID restrictions that kept Canadians eating at home during the spring and early summer,\" Loblaw chairman and president Galen G. Weston said during a conference call with analysts. He returned to the company as president in May following the retirement of Sarah Davis.\n\"As communities across the country began opening up, we were particularly pleased with the re-emergence of value-seeking shopping trips, which was good for our discount formats.\"\nMeanwhile, the company's drugstore division, which includes Shoppers Drug Mart and Pharmaprix, recorded a virtually non-existent cough and cold season as public health measures aimed at curbing the spread of COVID-19 also reduced the spread of other viruses.\nBut the uptick in outdoor adventures as many indoor activities remained off limits provided a boost to the pharmacy's allergy medication sales.\nGiven the significant impact of the pandemic on the company's grocery and pharmacy divisions, Loblaw's outlook for the remainder of the year is difficult to predict.\n\"A great deal of uncertainty remains as the course of the pandemic, the reopening of the economy and the resulting impact on consumer behaviour remains dynamic,\" Loblaw chief financial officer Richard Dufresne told analysts. \"Sales trajectory remains difficult to forecast.\"\nImproving vaccination rates and easing restrictions \"will change our business yet again,\" Weston said.\n\"It is difficult to predict exactly what the magnitude of those impacts will be.\"\nLoblaw's second-quarter earnings beat analysts estimates, with adjusted profits for the quarter hitting $464 million or $1.35 per diluted share, up from $260 million or 72 cents per share in the second quarter of 2020.\nRevenues were $12.49 billion, compared with $11.96 billion in the prior year quarter as food same-store sales declined 0.1 per cent and Shoppers Drug Mart same-store sales increased 9.6 per cent.\nLoblaw was expected to post $1.21 per share in adjusted profits on $12.16 billion of revenues, according to financial data firm Refinitiv.\nThe company said its net income attributable to common shareholders was $375 million or $1.09 per diluted share, up 121.9 per cent from $169 million or 47 cents per share a year earlier.\nThis report by The Canadian Press was first published July 28, 2021.\nCompanies in this story: (TSX:L)\nBrett Bundale, The Canadian Press",

During the pandemic, Canadian grocers said customers tended to shop less frequently but stock up more with each visit.\nLoblaw said it's expecting shoppers to return to its discount stores as restrictions ease, something it started to see in the three months ended June 19.\nThe company operates conventional grocery stores like Loblaws, Zehrs and Provigo as well as a discount division that includes No Frills and Maxi. activities remained off limits provided a boost to the pharmacy's allergy medication sales.\nGiven the significant impact of the pandemic on the company's grocery and pharmacy divisions, Loblaw's \"will change our business yet again,\" Weston said.\n\"It is difficult to predict exactly what the magnitude of those impacts will be.\"\nLoblaw's second-quarter earnings beat analysts estimates,             "title": "Loblaw Q2 profit soars even as grocery sales level off from pandemic highs",
            "description": "Canada's biggest grocery store chain said renewed lockdowns this spring amid the third wave of COVID-19 kept food sales strong as the pandemic's eat-at-home trend continued. Loblaw Companies Ltd. said Wednesday its grocery sales dipped 0.",
            "content": "Canada's biggest grocery store chain said renewed lockdowns this spring amid the third wave of COVID-19 kept food sales strong as the pandemic's eat-at-home trend continued. Loblaw Companies Ltd. said Wednesday its grocery sales dipped 0.\nCanada's biggest grocery store chain said renewed lockdowns this spring amid the third wave of COVID-19 kept food sales strong as the pandemic's eat-at-home trend continued.\nLoblaw Companies Ltd. said Wednesday its grocery sales dipped 0.1 per cent in its latest quarter, almost reaching the pandemic surge recorded during the same period the year before.\nBut in a sign of how consumer behaviour is expected to shift as the country emerges from lockdowns, the company said basket sizes \u2014 the amount of groceries customers buy at one time \u2014 edged down in its second quarter while traffic in its stores picked up. During the pandemic, Canadian grocers said customers tended to shop less frequently but stock up more with each visit.\nLoblaw said it's expecting shoppers to return to its discount stores as restrictions ease, something it started to see in the three months ended June 19.\nThe company operates conventional grocery stores like Loblaws, Zehrs and Provigo as well as a discount division that includes No Frills and Maxi. Many consumers turned to full-service stores during the pandemic as part of a one-stop-shopping trend to reduce trips outside the home.\n\"Our grocery divisions performed well in the first part of the quarter, aided by COVID restrictions that kept Canadians eating at home during the spring and early summer,\" Loblaw chairman and president Galen G. Weston said during a conference call with analysts. He returned to the company as president in May following the retirement of Sarah Davis.\n\"As communities across the country began opening up, we were particularly pleased with the re-emergence of value-seeking shopping trips, which was good for our discount formats.\"\nMeanwhile, the company's drugstore division, which includes Shoppers Drug Mart and Pharmaprix, recorded a virtually non-existent cough and cold season as public health measures aimed at curbing the spread of COVID-19 also reduced the spread of other viruses.\nBut the uptick in outdoor adventures as many indoor activities remained off limits provided a boost to the pharmacy's allergy medication sales.\nGiven the significant impact of the pandemic on the company's grocery and pharmacy divisions, Loblaw's outlook for the remainder of the year is difficult to predict.\n\"A great deal of uncertainty remains as the course of the pandemic, the reopening of the economy and the resulting impact on consumer behaviour remains dynamic,\" Loblaw chief financial officer Richard Dufresne told analysts. \"Sales trajectory remains difficult to forecast.\"\nImproving vaccination rates and easing restrictions \"will change our business yet again,\" Weston said.\n\"It is difficult to predict exactly what the magnitude of those impacts will be.\"\nLoblaw's second-quarter earnings beat analysts estimates, with adjusted profits for the quarter hitting $464 million or $1.35 per diluted share, up from $260 million or 72 cents per share in the second quarter of 2020.\nRevenues were $12.49 billion, compared with $11.96 billion in the prior year quarter as food same-store sales declined 0.1 per cent and Shoppers Drug Mart same-store sales increased 9.6 per cent.\nLoblaw was expected to post $1.21 per share in adjusted profits on $12.16 billion of revenues, according to financial data firm Refinitiv.\nThe company said its net income attributable to common shareholders was $375 million or $1.09 per diluted share, up 121.9 per cent from $169 million or 47 cents per share a year earlier.\nThis report by The Canadian Press was first published July 28, 2021.\nCompanies in this story: (TSX:L)\nBrett Loblaw Companies Ltd. said Wednesday its grocery sales dipped 0.\nCanada's biggest grocery store chain said renewed lockdowns this spring amid the third wave of COVID-19 kept food sales strong as the pandemic's eat-at-home trend continued.\nLoblaw Companies Ltd. said Wednesday its grocery sales dipped 0.1 per cent in its latest quarter, almost reaching the pandemic surge recorded during the same period the year before.\nBut in a sign of how consumer behaviour is expected to shift as the country emerges from lockdowns, the company said basket sizes \u2014 the amount of groceries customers buy at one time \u2014 edged down in its second quarter while traffic in its stores picked up. During the pandemic, Canadian grocers said customers tended to shop less frequently but stock up more with each visit.\nLoblaw said it's expecting shoppers to return to its discount stores as restrictions ease, something it started to see in the three months ended June 19.\nThe company operates conventional grocery stores like Loblaws, Zehrs and Provigo as well as a discount division that includes No Frills and Maxi. Many consumers turned to full-service stores during the pandemic as part of a one-stop-shopping trend to reduce trips outside the home.\n\"Our grocery divisions performed well in the first part of the quarter, aided by COVID restrictions that kept Canadians eating at home during the spring and early summer,\" Loblaw chairman and president Galen G. Weston said during a conference call with analysts. He returned to the company as president in May following the retirement of Sarah Davis.\n\"As communities across the country began opening up, we were particularly pleased with the re-emergence of value-seeking shopping trips, which was good for our discount formats.\"\nMeanwhile, the company's drugstore division, which includes Shoppers Drug Mart and Pharmaprix, recorded a virtually non-existent cough and cold season as public health measures aimed at curbing the spread of COVID-19 also reduced the spread of other viruses.\nBut the uptick in outdoor adventures as many indoor activities remained off limits provided a boost to the pharmacy's allergy medication sales.\nGiven the significant impact of the pandemic on the company's grocery and pharmacy divisions, Loblaw's outlook for the remainder of the year is difficult to predict.\n\"A great deal of uncertainty remains as the course of the pandemic, the reopening of the economy and the resulting impact on consumer behaviour remains dynamic,\" Loblaw chief financial officer Richard Dufresne told analysts. \"Sales trajectory remains difficult to forecast.\"\nImproving vaccination rates and easing restrictions \"will change our business yet again,\" Weston said.\n\"It is difficult to predict exactly what the magnitude of those impacts will be.\"\nLoblaw's second-quarter earnings beat analysts estimates, with adjusted profits for the quarter hitting $464 million or $1.35 per diluted share, up from $260 million or 72 cents per share in the second quarter of 2020.\nRevenues were $12.49 billion, compared with $11.96 billion in the prior year quarter as food same-store sales declined 0.1 per cent and Shoppers Drug Mart same-store sales increased 9.6 per cent.\nLoblaw was expected to post $1.21 per share in adjusted profits on $12.16 billion of revenues, according to financial data firm Refinitiv.\nThe company said its net income attributable to common shareholders was $375 million or $1.09 per diluted share, up 121.9 per cent from $169 million or 47 cents per share a year earlier.\nThis report by The Canadian Press was first published July 28, 2021.\nCompanies in this story: (TSX:L)\nBrett             "title": "Loblaw Q2 profit soars even as grocery sales level off from pandemic highs",
            "description": "Canada's biggest grocery store chain said renewed lockdowns this spring amid the third wave of COVID-19 kept food sales strong as the pandemic's eat-at-home trend continued. Loblaw Companies Ltd. said Wednesday its grocery sales dipped 0.",
            "content": "Canada's biggest grocery store chain said renewed lockdowns this spring amid the third wave of COVID-19 kept food sales strong as the pandemic's eat-at-home trend continued. Loblaw Companies Ltd. said Wednesday its grocery sales dipped 0.\nCanada's biggest grocery store chain said renewed lockdowns this spring amid the third wave of COVID-19 kept food sales strong as the pandemic's eat-at-home trend continued.\nLoblaw Companies Ltd. said Wednesday its grocery sales dipped 0.1 per cent in its latest quarter, almost reaching the pandemic surge recorded during the same period the year before.\nBut in a sign of how consumer behaviour is expected to shift as the country emerges from lockdowns, the company said basket sizes \u2014 the amount of groceries customers buy at one time \u2014 edged down in its second quarter while traffic in its stores picked up. During the pandemic, Canadian grocers said customers tended to shop less frequently but stock up more with each visit.\nLoblaw said it's expecting shoppers to return to its discount stores as restrictions ease, something it started to see in the three months ended June 19.\nThe company operates conventional grocery stores like Loblaws, Zehrs and Provigo as well as a discount division that includes No Frills and Maxi. Many consumers turned to full-service stores during the pandemic as part of a one-stop-shopping trend to reduce trips outside the home.\n\"Our grocery divisions performed well in the first part of the quarter, aided by COVID restrictions that kept Canadians eating at home during the spring and early summer,\" Loblaw chairman and president Galen G. Weston said during a conference call with analysts. He returned to the company as president in May following the retirement of Sarah Davis.\n\"As communities across the country began opening up, we were particularly pleased with the re-emergence of value-seeking shopping trips, which was good for our discount formats.\"\nMeanwhile, the company's drugstore division, which includes Shoppers Drug Mart and Pharmaprix, recorded a virtually non-existent cough and cold season as public health measures aimed at curbing the spread of COVID-19 also reduced the spread of other viruses.\nBut the uptick in outdoor adventures as many indoor activities remained off limits provided a boost to the pharmacy's allergy medication sales.\nGiven the significant impact of the pandemic on the company's grocery and pharmacy divisions, Loblaw's outlook for the remainder of the year is difficult to predict.\n\"A great deal of uncertainty remains as the course of the pandemic, the reopening of the economy and the resulting impact on consumer behaviour remains dynamic,\" Loblaw chief financial officer Richard Dufresne told analysts. \"Sales trajectory remains difficult to forecast.\"\nImproving vaccination rates and easing restrictions \"will change our business yet again,\" Weston said.\n\"It is difficult to predict exactly what the magnitude of those impacts will be.\"\nLoblaw's second-quarter earnings beat analysts estimates, with adjusted profits for the quarter hitting $464 million or $1.35 per diluted share, up from $260 million or 72 cents per share in the second quarter of 2020.\nRevenues were $12.49 billion, compared with $11.96 billion in the prior year quarter as food same-store sales declined 0.1 per cent and Shoppers Drug Mart same-store sales increased 9.6 per cent.\nLoblaw was expected to post $1.21 per share in adjusted profits on $12.16 billion of revenues, according to financial data firm Refinitiv.\nThe company said its net income attributable to common shareholders was $375 million or $1.09 per diluted share, up 121.9 per cent from $169 million or 47 cents per share a year earlier.\nThis report by The Canadian Press was first published July 28, 2021.\nCompanies in this story: (TSX:L)\nBrett             "title": "'Punk' health trend",
            "description": "I was researching odd food traditions for this week\u2019s column, and I came across a new trend in China called \u201cpunk health.\u201d I was so intrigued I stopped looking. You will have to wait for another week for me to offer a list, as I\u2019d like to expand on this concept, which I don\u2019t think is new or unique to China.",
            "content": "Photo: Contributed\nI was researching odd food traditions for this week\u2019s column, and I came across a new trend in China called \u201cpunk health.\u201d I was so intrigued I stopped looking. You will have to wait for another week for me to offer a list, as I\u2019d like to expand on this concept, which I don\u2019t think is new or unique to China.\n\u201cPunk health\u201d is translated from a Chinese term, and it describes the behaviour of doing something excessive but then following it up with something overtly healthy. It has been popular with young people in urban China for a few years now, and there are products that cater to the trend.\nHave you had goji berries in your beer? Or coffee infused with medicinal herbs? Maybe you tried a healthy meal after a night out partying, or the reverse of working out like crazy and then indulging in fried chicken? These are all examples of punk health.\nI would argue this is a kind of youthful rebellion that has existed for generations. As a youth, you feel the invincibility from your shorter time on the planet, but you are still pressured by the counsel from elders about taking care of yourself. Punk health is the latest way of expressing that compromise.\nWhen I was young and working as a waitress at Earl\u2019s (we didn\u2019t call ourselves servers way back then), a popular food order with young people was potato skins and diet coke. Potato skins were served with a thick layer of melted cheese and a generous bowl of sour cream for dipping. I\u2019m not sure the diet coke was enough to offset that indulgence, but the choice did seem to make the diners feel better.\nThe contradiction of this kind of behaviour is the badge of honour that we wear when we are young. With every generation there is a struggle to create a new sort of identity, something different than our parents \u2013 we believe we have a better approach than they did. This comes amidst the pressure of wanting to be recognized as a success, ironically in a society where those giving recognition are often our elders.\nDeep down we can all feel like we need to make up for our lack of success in being our best selves at times. Combining junk food with healthy components might just be the latest innovation, like the new candies with melatonin that help insomniacs sleep.\nIn lesser extremes, there is an effort to balance that works well if we back up to the middle ground. A big cup of herbal tea after a night out will help re-hydrate you and your cookies n\u2019 cream protein shake after a workout does help your muscles recover.\nDo you remember exotic healthy remedies for excessive partying when you were young, or healthy obsessions that were meant to offset the marathon study binges? I certainly had a few. I suppose this is a stage we try out as we learn the balance of life, juggling our responsibilities and ambitions.\nIn China, young people are describing themselves either as \u201csalted fish\u201d \u2013 like a dead fish, with no ambitions \u2013 or \u201cbuddha youth,\u201d a disinterested generation that\u2019s unfazed by worldly obligations. They are disillusioned with seeing their parents who worked hard to earn money and in their elder years have health problems that money cannot solve.\nWe all need to find our own path. Young people have re-evaluated what they see as the most important goals in life for many generations. I look forward to seeing what the current youth come up with, and how they move past the punk phase.\nIn the meantime, I\u2019m going to enjoy my regular coffee with nothing in it to boost me; at my age, I\u2019m happy with my middle ground.",

            "title": "Reusable takeout containers are a popular pandemic trend",
            "description": "In this week's issue of our environment newsletter, we look at innovations in reusable takeout containers and why some food lovers are taking beef off their menus.",
            "content": "Hello, Earthlings! This is our weekly newsletter on all things environmental, where we highlight trends and solutions that are moving us to a more sustainable world. (Sign up here to get it in your inbox every Thursday.)\nThis week:\nReusable takeout containers are a popular pandemic trend\nDriving schools get charged up about electric vehicles\nWhy beef is off the menu for some climate-conscious foodies\nReusable takeout containers are a popular pandemic trend\n(Reusables)\nWe've previously talked about the waste that takeout generates and some of the potential solutions . Since the pandemic started, a lack of indoor dining has made takeout the only restaurant option in many places, and as a result, both the problems \u2014 and the solutions \u2014 have multiplied.\nAt least five new startups across Canada are hoping to eliminate waste by allowing you to order your meal in reusable, returnable containers. A Friendlier Company (in Guelph, Ont.), Suppli (Toronto), Ekko (Waterloo, Ont.) and Reusables (Vancouver) are already serving restaurants and customers, while Re-Vita (Edmonton) is getting ready to launch.\nWhy now? The pandemic might not be an obvious time to launch new services for the struggling restaurant industry. But many people have started ordering more takeout and feel guilty about the waste, said Anastasia Kiku, co-founder of Reusables (whose wares are shown in the photo above). \"We just wanted to solve this problem,\" she said.\nAlso, in October, the federal government announced it would ban \"hard-to-recycle\" single-use plastic food takeout containers , such as black plastic and polystyrene. Kiku and the others say reusables are the best solution.\nJacqui Hutchings, co-founder and chief operating officer of A Friendlier Company, said the pandemic has also forced people to get used to change. \"Now is the time to implement a new system,\" she said. \"Just offer it as an option and see what happens.\"\nWhat kind of container: In most cases, the food comes in stainless steel containers. Crystal Kruis, who co-founded Ekko with her daughter Chloe, said metal is more durable and is what customers said they wanted.\nThe exception is A Friendlier Company, which uses polypropylene plastic containers. The company says the containers are light, stackable and their low cost reduces fees (it's the only service that's free for customers).\nHow it works: Suppli, Reusables and A Friendlier Company give customers the option to order through popular food delivery apps, although you generally have to sign up through their own website or app first, and leave a note about the use of reusable containers in the delivery instructions.\nSuppli and Ekko have per-order fees ranging from 50 cents to $2, while Reusables charges a $5 monthly fee. A Friendlier Company doesn't charge customers for the service, although diners do have to pay a refundable deposit of 50 cents to $1 per container.\nRestaurants pay the companies a fee that includes the container rental, cleaning and restocking. Generally, the fee is designed to be comparable to what the restaurants are already paying for disposable containers.\nThe companies track their containers using QR codes or electronic ID tags to make sure they're returned to a participating restaurant or drop-off point within one to two weeks. Otherwise, customers get charged.\nHow it's going: So far, each company has between four and 30 participating restaurants.\nCrystal Kruis at Ekko said in the first two days after her company launched in April, the company replaced 231 takeout containers with reusables \u2014 roughly enough to stack up to the height of a four-storey building.\nShe said some customers told her they had been avoiding takeout because of the waste. Having a reusable option allowed them to resume supporting local restaurants.\nHutchings at A Friendlier Company, which launched in October, said some participating restaurants have switched completely to reusables and stopped offering customers disposable options at all.\n\u2014 Emily Chung\nReader feedback\n\"Thanks for the story on banning short-haul flights ,\" writes K Braun. \"Europe has such a good train network; the rise of companies like EasyJet and RyanAir that made it so cheap to fly those short hops across the continent supported a terrible trend to fly instead of taking the train. I hope more jurisdictions in Europe punish short-haul flights and incentivize train travel.\n\"And I'd love to see this come to Canada. Why are people flying between Montreal/Toronto/Ottawa? Take the train! Why isn't there a better train link between Toronto and Kitchener-Waterloo? Why is there no high-speed train between Calgary and Edmonton? Canada was united by the train but it has been neglected in favour of the wasteful highway. I'd love to see train travel \u2014 as a genuine option for not only travel but also commuting \u2014 become a genuine option in Canada.\"\nWrite us at whatonearth@cbc.ca.\nOld issues of What on Earth? are right here .\nThere's also a radio show and podcast! Mired. Bogged down. Swamped. You might not think much of wetlands, but it turns out they are one of nature's best climate solutions. This week, What on Earth host Laura Lynch squishes into the mucky ground. What on Earth airs Sunday at 12:30 p.m., 1 p.m. in Newfoundland. Subscribe on your favourite podcast app or hear it on demand at CBC Listen .\nThe Big Picture: Electric vehicle driving schools\nThe dramatic rise of Tesla, along with pledges from legacy carmakers to produce fleets of zero-emission vehicles, have primed the driving public to believe that electric transport is the future. With that in mind, a number of jurisdictions in Canada have opened up all-electric driving schools . As with most things to do with electric vehicles (EVs), Quebec is arguably furthest in front. The province's Transportation Ministry chipped in $4.5 million toward a two-year pilot project called E-roule. Launched in 2020, it has so far facilitated funding for 30 driving schools to teach students in EVs. While E-roule estimates that its efforts have already saved 102 tonnes of CO2 emissions, its larger impact will likely be in helping produce a generation of drivers who may never handle a gas-powered vehicle.\n(Guillaume Lavallee/AFP via Getty Images)\nHot and bothered: Provocative ideas from around the web\nThe World Steel Association says the industry is responsible for up to nine per cent of worldwide emissions from the burning of fossil fuels. Historically, metallurgical coal has been key in the manufacture of steel. But Swedish steel maker SSAB is looking to clean hydrogen as a way to make \"fossil-fuel-free steel.\"\nSpeaking of the Swedes, furniture giant Ikea has undertaken a number of steps over the years to reduce its environmental footprint. The company has talked about striving for a more circular mode of consumption, where its items are recycled or reused rather than simply discarded. To that end, Ikea stores in the U.K. will start a program that has been tested in Portugal and Australia, where they buy back unwanted furniture items .\nWhy beef is off the menu for some climate-conscious foodies\n(Evan Mitsui/CBC)\nGrowing up on a farm in southern Ontario, Toronto chef Ikeila Wright says she ate enough beef to last her a lifetime.\nBack then, her parents grew crops and raised livestock. Now, she's the chef and owner at One Love Vegetarian, a Jamaican restaurant in Toronto that serves hearty dishes such as callaloo, a barbecue tofu stir fry, potato and chickpea rotis and a signature corn soup.\n\"What I eat, what I put on my plate, is personal. And I think for everyone it should be personal, but it also should be conscious,\" Wright said. \"We have to think about future generations, because history will find us accountable for the choices that we make now.\"\nWright, who chose to become vegetarian for health and environmental reasons, is part of a growing number of people worried about the carbon footprint of meat \u2014 and beef in particular, which the UN's Food and Agriculture Organization estimates is responsible for 41 per cent of all livestock emissions.\nLast week, the U.S. food magazine and website Epicurious announced it was no longer publishing beef recipes because of how carbon-intensive the protein is.\nIn an explanatory post , the magazine's editors cited statistics from the World Resources Institute that beef requires 20 times more land and makes 20 times more greenhouse gases than common plant proteins, such as beans. It is also three times more carbon-intensive than poultry and pork.\nDavid Tamarkin, one of the co-authors of the post, told CBC Radio's As It Happens the \"whole point\" of a food publication like Epicurious \"is to influence the way that people eat,\" he said.\nGiven it receives millions of users every month, \"if we were successful in replacing one beef meal with one vegetarian meal a month, that is a huge win. Because if everybody did that, that would make an enormous impact on the sustainability of our diets.\"\nSo how much beef do people need to cut down on to make an impact on greenhouse gas emissions?\nResearcher Jim Dyer set out to answer this question in a report last year for Agriculture and Agri-Food Canada. The study, aimed at the livestock industry, modelled scenarios where Canadians tweaked their meat consumption without reducing their overall protein intake or going fully vegetarian.\nThe modelling found that if red meat consumption dropped 25 per cent \u2014 in line with medical recommendations \u2014 and was one-quarter beef and three-quarters pork, Canada's greenhouse gas emissions from the livestock sector dropped 10.7 per cent. (The study assumed that any drop in red-meat consumption was replaced with chicken.)\nOther analyses, including the planetary health diet published in The Lancet in 2019 , recommend cutting meat consumption down to just one serving of beef per week.\nDyer's paper did not model the impact of cutting out meat entirely from the Canadian diet and replacing it with plant-based proteins like pulses. But he said shifting to a vegan diet has an even greater impact on emissions.\n\"The first message was really quite simple, and that is: eat less beef,\" he said.\nTypically, grass-fed beef \u2014 where cattle graze in a pasture \u2014 has been analyzed as higher in emissions than feedlot beef, in part because of land use. But many studies, including Dyer's, don't account for the other environmental benefits of grass-fed beef, such as the carbon sequestration in the grass and soil.\nThat can mean the higher emissions from grass-fed beef are offset by the carbon sequestered in the pasture, according to a 2018 study , although uncertainty remains about how much carbon is sequestered.\nThat's important for Cedric MacLeod, a grass-fed beef producer in New Brunswick. MacLeod and his family operate Local Valley Farm, where cattle roam free and feed on 40 hectares of strategically planted grass. The farm uses as little fertilizer as possible by planting specific types of grasses and using manure \"very effectively,\" and runs on solar energy.\nMacLeod, a soil scientist by training, said principles of sustainability are top of mind. \"We do everything we can, certainly, to minimize our emissions.\"\nMacLeod said that people should be concerned about where and how their food is raised, and be willing to pay for it.\n\"The chicken growers play a role. The potato growers play a role. The corn and soybean guys play a role. The cattle sector plays an important role, because we're managing the grassland,\" MacLeod said.\n\"We're all contributing to the sector's contribution to the fight against climate change.\"\n\u2014 Inayat Singh and Alice Hopton\nStay in touch!\nAre there issues you'd like us to cover? Questions you want answered? Do you just want to share a kind word? We'd love to hear from you. Email us at whatonearth@cbc.ca.\nSign up here to get What on Earth? in your inbox every Thursday.\nEditor: Andre Mayer | Logo design: Sk\u00f6dt McNalty",

            "publishedAt": "2021-05-07T08:00:00Z",

                "url": "https://www.cbc.ca"

            "title": "Latest pandemic restaurant trend: multiple food brands under one roof",

            "content": "Share this Story: Latest pandemic restaurant trend brings multiple food brands under one roof\nLatest pandemic restaurant trend brings multiple food brands under one roof These new kitchens and eateries are seizing a moment that has been gruelling for other restaurants, but is seemingly in their favour. Photo by Errol McGihon / Postmedia\nArticle content In May last year, after the novel coronavirus began reshaping how Ottawa\u2019s restaurants would operate, Nara Sok was convinced that running commercial kitchens with no dining rooms attached to them was the new way to go. This spring, his fledgling business Casper Kitchen has given him lots of reasons to think he was right: more than 210,000, if you assign a reason to every dollar of revenue the York Street enterprise has earned over the last three months. We apologize, but this video has failed to load.\ntap here to see other videos from our team. Try refreshing your browser, or Latest pandemic restaurant trend brings multiple food brands under one roof Back to video \u201cIt\u2019s crazy \u2026 We\u2019ve been surprising ourselves,\u201d Sok says, sharing an impressive report on his food sales via the third-party delivery service Uber Eats. His business partner, David Wen, adds more concretely: \u201cEvery quarter we\u2019re doubling our business. If we continue this trajectory, within our first year, we might become a million-dollar company.\u201d That\u2019s not be bad at all for a business selling to-go Vietnamese food, Korean fried chicken and Southern barbecue fare. For that matter, many an Ottawa restaurant whose sales have withered during the COVID-19 era might be envious.\nAdvertisement Story continues below This advertisement has not loaded yet, but your article continues below.\nArticle content Of course, Wen\u2019s proposition is a big one given the incessant uncertainties businesses face during the pandemic. But currently, with locked-down food lovers waiting for dining rooms and patios to open, never mind vaccinations, the optimism at Casper Kitchen is hard to counter. It\u2019s also shared by other Ottawa businesses that host multiple food brands under their roofs. While business models may differ somewhat, these new kitchens and eateries are seizing a moment that has been gruelling for other restaurants, but is seemingly in their favour. Casper Kitchen\u2019s name riffs on \u201cghost kitchen,\u201d the industry term for a stand-alone kitchen focused on takeout and delivery rather than dine-in business. Casper\u2019s Viet Fresh brand took its first order in early December. Since then, the brands Banh Mi Bros, which makes Vietnamese sandwiches, Old\u2019s BBQ and Fried Chicken for the Seoul have opened. Wen and Sok say Casper Kitchen owes its success to savvy digital marketing, premium ingredients and packaging and a tireless focus on customer satisfaction. The future could see more brands added to the Casper Kitchen roster, plus an in-house delivery service and an app, they add. \u201cIt\u2019s undecided if we will ever have our own dine-in (service). We don\u2019t need it right now,\u201d says Sok, who also owns Tomo, the pan-Asian restaurant and lounge on Clarence Street. Wen, whose background is in technology, thinks of Casper Kitchen as more than a money-maker gratifying hungry customers. He calls it an incubator, developing brands that could proliferate elsewhere downtown, be spun off as dine-in restaurants in Ottawa\u2019s suburbs or even be sold to a franchising company wanting to take a brand nation-wide.\nAdvertisement Story continues below This advertisement has not loaded yet, but your article continues below.\nArticle content \u201cEvery brand has its own identity and growth trajectory,\u201d Wen says. \u201cWe have unlimited potential. We\u2019re only limited by our creativity.\u201d In recent months, the Mad Radish restaurant chain has expanded its offerings, following a rationale similar to that of Casper Kitchen. The flagship Mad Radish in downtown Ottawa focused on salads when it opened in July 2017. But a push began last summer to sell Mexican food under the Luisa\u2019s Burritos & Bowls brand and thin-crust pizzas under the Revival Pizza brand from Mad Radish locations. \u201cThe pandemic forced us to think much more critically about our business,\u201d Mad Radish founder David Segal says. \u201cIt made a lot of sense to extend into new product categories. \u201cWe\u2019re combining the best elements of a ghost kitchen with the best elements of a restaurant,\u201d Segal continues. \u201cThe food\u2019s got to be made to order, it\u2019s got to be fresh, but it\u2019s got to be built to travel.\u201d Mad Radish\u2019s Albert Street location, once the chain\u2019s top performer, suffered as Ottawa\u2019s pandemic-stricken downtown emptied, Segal says. But the multi-brand Barrhaven store, which opened in March, is doing well despite the current lockdown. In Barrhaven, customers can select from all three brands in one order, sharing a Revival pizza and a Mad Radish salad, with a Luisa\u2019s burrito on the side, Segal says. \u201cThey like the variety.\u201d In keeping with Segal\u2019s suburban strategy, a multi-brand Mad Radish is to open in Kanata next year, and an Orl\u00e9ans store is envisioned. They will still have 20 to 30 seats \u2014 \u201cBut you don\u2019t need 40 to 50,\u201d Segal says \u2014 for dine-in customers. \u201cWe believe future of restaurants is multi-channel,\u201d Segal says.\nAdvertisement Story continues below This advertisement has not loaded yet, but your article continues below.\nArticle content This month\u2019s opening of Chop Shop in Hintonburg sees an Ottawa-centred example of the thinking that made Mad Radish diversify. Launched in the space that was the recently shuttered Bar Laurel, Chop Shop will offer the \u201cgreatest hits\u201d from the downtown eateries El Camino, Shelby Burger and the yet-to-open pizzeria Giulia, general manager Jana Renaud says. Photo by Julie Oliver / Postmedia The restaurants under Chop Shop\u2019s banner are commonly owned, and the owners saw an opportunity to expand into a new neighbourhood when Bar Laurel closed, Renaud says. \u201cIt\u2019s a new concept for us to be cooking burgers and tacos at the same time, and eventually pizza,\u201d she says. The kitchen Chop Shop inherited had to be reconfigured to suit the new business\u2019s needs, she adds. Currently serving tacos only, Chop Shop will welcome dine-in customers when pandemic restrictions relax. Once it\u2019s fully operational, it be a sort of \u201cmicro food hall,\u201d Renaud says. In the City Centre building, the ghost kitchen called the Kitchen was a pandemic pivot by the company Lunch, partner Jordan Lazarovitz says. After more than a decade of selling grab-and-go lunches from its stores and vending machines, Lunch was \u201cleft with zero business\u201d after downtown was deserted, Lazarovitz says. Lunch\u2019s City Centre commissary pivoted last fall to become a ghost kitchen, renting space to food businesses. The Kitchen rents by the week and tenants have come and gone. These days, the 3,000-square-foot space\u2019s tenants include Chef Jae-Anthony Popup, which serves Caribbean food, Peruvian kitchen Raphael Express and Filipino food business Lola\u2019s Kitchen. Lazarovitz also has Side Piece and Wing Ding, his own chicken-based brands. Given the blow the pandemic dealt Lunch, it makes sense that Lazarovitz does not brim with the optimism that buoys Casper Kitchen and Mad Radish. \u201cYes, more people order in now. But for how long? We\u2019ll see if it has legs or not,\u201d Lazarovitz says of his ghost kitchen. phum@postmedia.ocm\nShare this article in your social network\nLatest National Stories Advertisement Story continues below This advertisement has not loaded yet, but your article continues below.\nOttawa Citizen Headline News Sign up to receive daily headline news from Ottawa Citizen, a division of Postmedia Network Inc.             "description": "Sourdough bread may have been the top do-it-yourself food trend to come out of 2020, but this year, it's pickling that's on the rise.",
            "content": "Article content\nSourdough bread may have been the top do-it-yourself food trend to come out of 2020, but this year, it\u2019s pickling that appears to be on the rise.\n\u201cSimilar to baking bread, pickling is time consuming and takes a bit of research before jumping in. With more time at home, people are finally ready to tackle hobbies and activities they have always wanted to try but have not had time for in other years \u2014 or were too intimidated to take the first step into,\u201d Steve McVicker, co-founder of the Canadian company Matt & Steve\u2019s, says. \u201cAlso, people are snacking a lot more now that they are home all day and I feel they are looking for healthy snacks that have great flavour.\nWe apologize, but this video has failed to load.\ntap here to see other videos from our team. Try refreshing your browser, or See you later, sourdough: Pickling is the hot DIY food trend of 2021 Back to video\n\u201cPickling is a good way to turn up the taste on veggies.\u201d\nBut, pickling has been around for a very long time (dating back to 2030 BC, according to PBS). So, in many ways, the pickling predilection never really went anywhere. Instead, what\u2019s changed, is the access to proper products and information that makes the process more approachable at home.\n\u201cWith the resurgence of pickling \u2014 either by canning, fridge pickling, or lacto-fermentation \u2014 there are a lot of great resources out there,\u201d Executive Winery Chef Alexander Keith \u201cAK\u201d Campbell of Time Winery & Kitchen in Penticton, says. \u201cIt doesn\u2019t have to be difficult; it doesn\u2019t have to be expensive, and it\u2019s a great way to preserve the best of what\u2019s in season from your local farmer, or to best capitalize on the bounty that can come from even a small garden.\u201d",
